Thou delicious Fay\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  The guardian of a Sprite that lives to tease thee\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Loving in earnest, chiding but in play\r\n  The merry mocking Bruno! Who, that sees thee,\r\n  Can fail to love thee, Darling, even as I?\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  My sweetest Sylvie, we must say \xe2\x80\x98Good-bye!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                                PREFACE.\r\n\r\n\r\nI must begin with the same announcement as in the previous Volume (which\r\nI shall henceforward refer to as \xe2\x80\x9cVol. I.,\xe2\x80\x9d calling the present Volume\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVol. II.\xe2\x80\x9d), viz. that the Locket, at p. 405, was drawn by \xe2\x80\x98Miss Alice\r\nHavers.\xe2\x80\x99 And my reason, for not stating this on the title-page\xe2\x80\x94that it\r\nseems only due, to the artist of these wonderful pictures, that his name\r\nshould stand there alone\xe2\x80\x94has, I think, even greater weight in Vol. II.\r\nthan it had in Vol. I. Let me call especial attention to the three\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Birds\xe2\x80\x9d borders, at pp. 365, 371, 377. The way, in which he has\r\nmanaged to introduce the most minute details of the stanzas to be\r\nillustrated, seems to me a triumph of artistic ingenuity.\r\n\r\n\r\nLet me here express my sincere gratitude to the many Reviewers who have\r\nnoticed, whether favorably or unfavorably, the previous Volume. Their\r\nunfavorable remarks were, most probably, well-deserved; the favorable\r\nones less probably so. Both kinds have no doubt served to make the book\r\nknown, and have helped the reading Public to form their opinions of it.\r\nLet me also here assure them that it is not from any want of respect for\r\ntheir criticisms, that I have carefully forborne from reading _any_ of\r\nthem. I am strongly of opinion that an author had far better _not_ read\r\nany reviews of his books: the unfavorable ones are almost certain to\r\nmake him cross, and the favorable ones conceited; and _neither_ of these\r\nresults is desirable.\r\n\r\nCriticisms have, however, reached me from private sources, to some of\r\nwhich I propose to offer a reply.\r\n\r\nOne such critic complains that Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s strictures, on sermons and on\r\nchoristers, are too severe. Let me say, in reply, that I do _not_ hold\r\nmyself responsible for _any_ of the opinions expressed by the characters\r\nin my book. They are simply opinions which, it seemed to me, might\r\nprobably be held by the persons into whose mouths I put them, and which\r\nwere worth consideration.\r\n\r\nOther critics have objected to certain innovations in spelling, such as\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cwo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9ctraveler.\xe2\x80\x9d In reply, I can only plead my firm\r\nconviction that the popular usage is _wrong_. As to \xe2\x80\x9cca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d it will\r\nnot be disputed that, in all _other_ words ending in \xe2\x80\x9cn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d these\r\nletters are an abbreviation of \xe2\x80\x9cnot\xe2\x80\x9d; and it is surely absurd to suppose\r\nthat, in this solitary instance, \xe2\x80\x9cnot\xe2\x80\x9d is represented by \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x9d! In fact\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccan\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x9d is the _proper_ abbreviation for \xe2\x80\x9ccan it,\xe2\x80\x9d just as \xe2\x80\x9cis\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x9d is for\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cis it.\xe2\x80\x9d Again, in \xe2\x80\x9cwo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d the first apostrophe is needed, because the\r\nword \xe2\x80\x9cwould\xe2\x80\x9d is here _abridged_ into \xe2\x80\x9cwo\xe2\x80\x9d: but I hold it proper to spell\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x9d with only _one_ apostrophe, because the word \xe2\x80\x9cdo\xe2\x80\x9d is here\r\n_complete_. As to such words as \xe2\x80\x9ctraveler,\xe2\x80\x9d I hold the correct principle\r\nto be, to _double_ the consonant when the accent falls on that syllable;\r\notherwise to leave it _single_. This rule is observed in most cases\r\n(e.g. we double the \xe2\x80\x9cr\xe2\x80\x9d in \xe2\x80\x9cpreferred,\xe2\x80\x9d but leave it single in\r\n\xe2\x80\x9coffered\xe2\x80\x9d), so that I am only extending, to other cases, an existing\r\nrule. I admit, however, that I do not spell \xe2\x80\x9cparallel,\xe2\x80\x9d as the rule\r\nwould have it; but here we are constrained, by the etymology, to insert\r\nthe double \xe2\x80\x9cl\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the Preface to Vol. I. were two puzzles, on which my readers might\r\nexercise their ingenuity. One was, to detect the 3 lines of \xe2\x80\x9cpadding,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwhich I had found it necessary to supply in the passage extending from\r\nthe top of p. 35 to the middle of p. 38. They are the 14th, 15th, and\r\n16th lines of p. 37. The other puzzle was, to determine which (if any)\r\nof the 8 stanzas of the Gardener\xe2\x80\x99s Song (see pp. 65, 78, 83, 90, 106,\r\n116, 164, 168) were adapted to the context, and which (if any) had the\r\ncontext adapted to them. The last of them is the only one that was\r\nadapted to the context, the \xe2\x80\x9cGarden-Door that opened with a key\xe2\x80\x9d having\r\nbeen substituted for some creature (a Cormorant, I think) \xe2\x80\x9cthat nestled\r\nin a tree.\xe2\x80\x9d At pp. 78, 106, and 164, the context was adapted to the\r\nstanza. At p. 90, neither stanza nor context was altered: the connection\r\nbetween them was simply a piece of good luck.\r\n\r\nIn the Preface to Vol. I., at pp. ix., x., I gave an account of the\r\nmaking-up of the story of \xe2\x80\x9cSylvie and Bruno.\xe2\x80\x9d A few more details may\r\nperhaps be acceptable to my Readers.\r\n\r\nIt was in 1873, as I _now_ believe, that the idea first occurred to me\r\nthat a little fairy-tale (written, in 1867, for \xe2\x80\x9cAunt Judy\xe2\x80\x99s Magazine,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nunder the title \xe2\x80\x9cBruno\xe2\x80\x99s Revenge\xe2\x80\x9d) might serve as the nucleus of a\r\nlonger story. This I surmise, from having found the original draft of\r\nthe last paragraph of Vol. II., dated 1873. So that this paragraph has\r\nbeen waiting 20 years for its chance of emerging into print\xe2\x80\x94more than\r\ntwice the period so cautiously recommended by Horace for \xe2\x80\x98repressing\xe2\x80\x99\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s literary efforts!\r\n\r\nIt was in February, 1885, that I entered into negotiations, with Mr.\r\nHarry Furniss, for illustrating the book. Most of the substance of\r\n_both_ Volumes was then in existence in manuscript: and my original\r\nintention was to publish the _whole_ story at once. In September, 1885,\r\nI received from Mr. Furniss the first set of drawings\xe2\x80\x94the four which\r\nillustrate \xe2\x80\x9cPeter and Paul\xe2\x80\x9d (see I. pp. 144, 147, 150, 154): in\r\nNovember, 1886, I received the second set\xe2\x80\x94the three which illustrate the\r\nProfessor\xe2\x80\x99s song about the \xe2\x80\x9clittle man\xe2\x80\x9d who had \xe2\x80\x9ca little gun\xe2\x80\x9d (Vol. II.\r\npp. 265, 266, 267): and in January, 1887, I received the third set\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nfour which illustrate the \xe2\x80\x9cPig-Tale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo we went on, illustrating first one bit of the story, and then\r\nanother, without any idea of sequence. And it was not till March, 1889,\r\nthat, having calculated the number of pages the story would occupy, I\r\ndecided on dividing it into _two_ portions, and publishing it half at a\r\ntime. This necessitated the writing of a _sort_ of conclusion for the\r\nfirst Volume: and _most_ of my Readers, I fancy, regarded this as the\r\n_actual_ conclusion, when that Volume appeared in December, 1889. At any\r\nrate, among all the letters I received about it, there was only _one_\r\nwhich expressed _any_ suspicion that it was not a _final_ conclusion.\r\nThis letter was from a child. She wrote \xe2\x80\x9cwe were so glad, when we came\r\nto the end of the book, to find that there was no ending-up, for that\r\nshows us that you are going to write a sequel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt may interest some of my Readers to know the _theory_ on which this\r\nstory is constructed. It is an attempt to show what might _possibly_\r\nhappen, supposing that Fairies really existed; and that they were\r\nsometimes visible to us, and we to them; and that they were sometimes\r\nable to assume human form: and supposing, also, that human beings might\r\nsometimes become conscious of what goes on in the Fairy-world\xe2\x80\x94by actual\r\ntransference of their immaterial essence, such as we meet with in\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Esoteric Buddhism.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI have supposed a Human being to be capable of various psychical states,\r\nwith varying degrees of consciousness, as follows:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n(_a_) the ordinary state, with no consciousness of the presence of\r\nFairies;\r\n\r\n(_b_) the \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 state, in which, while conscious of actual\r\nsurroundings, he is _also_ conscious of the presence of Fairies;\r\n\r\n(_c_) a form of trance, in which, while _un_conscious of actual\r\nsurroundings, and apparently asleep, he (i.e. his immaterial essence)\r\nmigrates to other scenes, in the actual world, or in Fairyland, and is\r\nconscious of the presence of Fairies.\r\n\r\nI have also supposed a Fairy to be capable of migrating from Fairyland\r\ninto the actual world, and of assuming, at pleasure, a Human form; and\r\nalso to be capable of various psychical states, viz.\r\n\r\n(_a_) the ordinary state, with no consciousness of the presence of Human\r\nbeings;\r\n\r\n(_b_) a sort of \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 state, in which he is conscious, if in the\r\nactual world, of the presence of actual Human beings; if in Fairyland,\r\nof the presence of the immaterial essences of Human beings.\r\n\r\nI will here tabulate the passages, in both Volumes, where abnormal\r\nstates occur.\r\n\r\n  Historian\xe2\x80\x99s Locality and State. (_a_); Lady Muriel (_b_).\r\n   311-345 At lodgings                      _c_\r\n   351-399 do.                              _c_\r\n  407-end. do.                        _b_\r\n\r\nIn the Preface to Vol. I., at p. x., I gave an account of the\r\n_origination_ of some of the ideas embodied in the book. A few more such\r\ndetails may perhaps interest my Readers:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nI. p. 203. The very peculiar use, here made of a dead mouse, comes from\r\nreal life. I once found two very small boys, in a garden, playing a\r\nmicroscopic game of \xe2\x80\x98Single-Wicket.\xe2\x80\x99 The bat was, I think, about the\r\nsize of a table-spoon; and the utmost distance attained by the ball, in\r\nits most daring flights, was some 4 or 5 yards. The _exact_ length was\r\nof course a matter of _supreme_ importance; and it was always carefully\r\nmeasured out (the batsman and the bowler amicably sharing the toil) with\r\na dead mouse!\r\n\r\nI. p. 259. The two quasi-mathematical Axioms, quoted by Arthur at p. 259\r\nof Vol. I., (\xe2\x80\x9cThings that are greater than the same are greater than one\r\nanother,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cAll angles are equal\xe2\x80\x9d) were actually enunciated, in all\r\nseriousness, by undergraduates at a University situated not 100 miles\r\nfrom Ely.\r\n\r\nII. p. 10. Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s remark (\xe2\x80\x9cI can, if I like, &c.\xe2\x80\x9d) was actually made by\r\na little boy.\r\n\r\nII. p. 12. So also was his remark (\xe2\x80\x9cI know what it _doesn\xe2\x80\x99t_ spell.\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\nAnd his remark (\xe2\x80\x9cI just twiddled my eyes, &c.\xe2\x80\x9d) I heard from the lips of\r\na little girl, who had just solved a puzzle I had set her.\r\n\r\nII. p. 57. Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s soliloquy (\xe2\x80\x9cFor its father, &c.\xe2\x80\x9d) was actually spoken\r\nby a little girl, looking out of the window of a railway-carriage.\r\n\r\nII. p. 138. The remark, made by a guest at the dinner-party, when asking\r\nfor a dish of fruit (\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been wishing for them, &c.\xe2\x80\x9d) I heard made by\r\nthe great Poet-Laureate, whose loss the whole reading-world has so\r\nlately had to deplore.\r\n\r\nII. p. 163. Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s speech, on the subject of the age of \xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nembodies the reply of a little girl to the question \xe2\x80\x9cIs your grandmother\r\nan _old_ lady?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know if she\xe2\x80\x99s an _old_ lady,\xe2\x80\x9d said this\r\ncautious young person; \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99s _eighty-three_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nII. p. 203. The speech about \xe2\x80\x98Obstruction\xe2\x80\x99 is no mere creature of my\r\nimagination! It is copied _verbatim_ from the columns of the Standard,\r\nand was spoken by Sir William Harcourt, who was, at the time, a member\r\nof the \xe2\x80\x98Opposition,\xe2\x80\x99 at the \xe2\x80\x98National Liberal Club,\xe2\x80\x99 on July the 16th,\r\n1890.\r\n\r\nII. p. 329. The Professor\xe2\x80\x99s remark, about a dog\xe2\x80\x99s tail, that \xe2\x80\x9cit doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbite at _that_ end,\xe2\x80\x9d was actually made by a child, when warned of the\r\ndanger he was incurring by pulling the dog\xe2\x80\x99s tail.\r\n\r\nII. p. 374. The dialogue between Sylvie and Bruno, which occupies lines\r\n6 to 15, is a _verbatim_ report (merely substituting \xe2\x80\x9ccake\xe2\x80\x9d for \xe2\x80\x9cpenny\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\nof a dialogue overheard between two children.\r\n\r\n\r\nOne story in this Volume\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s Picnic\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94I can vouch for as suitable\r\nfor telling to children, having tested it again and again; and, whether\r\nmy audience has been a dozen little girls in a village-school, or some\r\nthirty or forty in a London drawing-room, or a hundred in a High School,\r\nI have always found them earnestly attentive, and keenly appreciative of\r\nsuch fun as the story supplied.\r\n\r\nMay I take this opportunity of calling attention to what I flatter\r\nmyself was a successful piece of name-coining, at p. 42 of Vol. I. Does\r\nnot the name \xe2\x80\x98Sibimet\xe2\x80\x99 fairly embody the character of the Sub-Warden?\r\nThe gentle Reader has no doubt observed what a singularly useless\r\narticle in a house a brazen trumpet is, if you simply leave it lying\r\nabout, and never blow it!\r\n\r\nReaders of the first Volume, who have amused themselves by trying to\r\nsolve the two puzzles propounded at pp. xi., xii. of the Preface, may\r\nperhaps like to exercise their ingenuity in discovering which (if any)\r\nof the following parallelisms were intentional, and which (if any)\r\naccidental.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cLittle Birds.\xe2\x80\x9d  Events, and Persons.\r\n  Stanza       1.  Banquet.\r\n               2.  Chancellor.\r\n               3.  Empress and Spinach (II. 325).\r\n               4.  Warden\xe2\x80\x99s Return.\r\n               5.  Professor\xe2\x80\x99s Lecture (II. 339).\r\n               6.  Other Professor\xe2\x80\x99s song (I. 138).\r\n               7.  Petting of Uggug.\r\n               8.  Baron Doppelgeist.\r\n               9.  Jester and Bear (I. 119). Little Foxes.\r\n              10.  Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s Dinner-Bell; Little Foxes.\r\n\r\nI will publish the answer to this puzzle in the Preface to a little book\r\nof \xe2\x80\x9cOriginal Games and Puzzles,\xe2\x80\x9d now in course of preparation.\r\n\r\nI have reserved, for the last, one or two rather more serious topics.\r\n\r\n\r\nI had intended, in this Preface, to discuss more fully, than I had done\r\nin the previous Volume, the \xe2\x80\x98Morality of Sport\xe2\x80\x99, with special reference\r\nto letters I have received from lovers of Sport, in which they point out\r\nthe many great advantages which men get from it, and try to prove that\r\nthe suffering, which it inflicts on animals, is too trivial to be\r\nregarded.\r\n\r\nBut, when I came to think the subject out, and to arrange the whole of\r\nthe arguments \xe2\x80\x98pro\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98con\xe2\x80\x99, I found it much too large for treatment\r\nhere. Some day, I hope to publish an essay on this subject. At present,\r\nI will content myself with stating the net result I have arrived at.\r\n\r\nIt is, that God has given to Man an absolute right to take the _lives_\r\nof other animals, for _any_ reasonable cause, such as the supply of\r\nfood: but that He has _not_ given to Man the right to inflict _pain_,\r\nunless when _necessary_: that mere pleasure, or advantage, does not\r\nconstitute such a necessity: and, consequently, that pain, inflicted for\r\nthe purposes of _Sport_, is cruel, and therefore wrong. But I find it a\r\nfar more complex question than I had supposed; and that the \xe2\x80\x98case\xe2\x80\x99, on\r\nthe side of the Sportsman, is a much stronger one than I had supposed.\r\nSo, for the present, I say no more about it.\r\n\r\n\r\nObjections have been raised to the severe language I have put into the\r\nmouth of \xe2\x80\x98Arthur\xe2\x80\x99, at p. 277, on the subject of \xe2\x80\x98Sermons,\xe2\x80\x99 and at pp.\r\n273, 274, on the subjects of Choral Services and \xe2\x80\x98Choristers.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nI have already protested against the assumption that I am ready to\r\nendorse the opinions of characters in my story. But, in these two\r\ninstances, I admit that I am much in sympathy with \xe2\x80\x98Arthur.\xe2\x80\x99 In my\r\nopinion, far too many sermons are expected from our preachers; and, as a\r\nconsequence, a great many are preached, which are not worth listening\r\nto; and, as a consequence of _that_, we are very apt _not_ to listen.\r\nThe reader of this paragraph probably heard a sermon last Sunday\r\nmorning? Well, let him, if he can, name the text, and state how the\r\npreacher treated it!\r\n\r\nThen, as to \xe2\x80\x98Choristers,\xe2\x80\x99 and all the other accessories\xe2\x80\x94of music,\r\nvestments, processions, &c.,\xe2\x80\x94which have come, along with them, into\r\nfashion\xe2\x80\x94while freely admitting that the \xe2\x80\x98Ritual\xe2\x80\x99 movement was sorely\r\nneeded, and that it has effected a vast improvement in our\r\nChurch-Services, which had become dead and dry to the last degree, I\r\nhold that, like many other desirable movements, it has gone too far in\r\nthe opposite direction, and has introduced many new dangers.\r\n\r\nFor the Congregation this new movement involves the danger of learning\r\nto think that the Services are done _for_ them; and that their bodily\r\n_presence_ is all they need contribute. And, for Clergy and Congregation\r\nalike, it involves the danger of regarding these elaborate Services as\r\n_ends in themselves_, and of forgetting that they are simply _means_,\r\nand the very hollowest of mockeries, unless they bear fruit in our\r\n_lives_.\r\n\r\nFor the Choristers it seems to involve the danger of self-conceit, as\r\ndescribed at p. 274, the danger of regarding those parts of the Service,\r\nwhere their help is not required, as not worth attending to, the danger\r\nof coming to regard the Service as a mere outward form\xe2\x80\x94a series of\r\npostures to be assumed, and of words to be said or sung, while the\r\n_thoughts_ are elsewhere\xe2\x80\x94and the danger of \xe2\x80\x98familiarity\xe2\x80\x99 breeding\r\n\xe2\x80\x98contempt\xe2\x80\x99 for sacred things.\r\n\r\nLet me illustrate these last two forms of danger, from my own\r\nexperience. Not long ago, I attended a Cathedral-Service, and was placed\r\nimmediately behind a row of men, members of the Choir; and I could not\r\nhelp noticing that they treated the _Lessons_ as a part of the Service\r\nto which they needed not to give _any_ attention, and as affording them\r\na convenient opportunity for arranging music-books, &c., &c. Also I have\r\nfrequently seen a row of little choristers, after marching in procession\r\nto their places, kneel down, as if about to pray, and rise from their\r\nknees after a minute spent in looking about them, it being but too\r\nevident that the attitude was a mere mockery. Surely it is very\r\ndangerous, for these children, to thus accustom them to _pretend_ to\r\npray? As an instance of irreverent treatment of holy things, I will\r\nmention a custom, which no doubt many of my readers have noticed in\r\nChurches where the Clergy and Choir enter in procession, viz. that, at\r\nthe end of the private devotions, which are carried on in the vestry,\r\nand which are of course inaudible to the Congregation, the final \xe2\x80\x9cAmen\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nis _shouted_, loud enough to be heard all through the Church. This\r\nserves as a signal, to the Congregation, to prepare to rise when the\r\nprocession appears: and it admits of no dispute that it is for this\r\npurpose that it is thus shouted. When we remember to Whom that \xe2\x80\x9cAmen\xe2\x80\x9d is\r\n_really_ addressed, and consider that it is here _used_ for the same\r\npurpose as one of the Church-bells, we must surely admit that it is a\r\npiece of gross irreverence? To _me_ it is much as if I were to see a\r\nBible used as a footstool.\r\n\r\nAs an instance of the dangers, for the Clergy themselves, introduced by\r\nthis new movement, let me mention the fact that, according to _my_\r\nexperience, Clergymen of this school are _specially_ apt to retail comic\r\nanecdotes, in which the most sacred names and words\xe2\x80\x94sometimes actual\r\ntexts from the Bible\xe2\x80\x94are used as themes for jesting. Many such things\r\nare repeated as having been originally said by _children_, whose utter\r\nignorance of evil must no doubt acquit _them_, in the sight of God, of\r\nall blame; but it must be otherwise for those who _consciously_ use such\r\ninnocent utterances as material for their unholy mirth.\r\n\r\nLet me add, however, _most_ earnestly, that I fully believe that this\r\nprofanity is, in many cases, _un_conscious: the \xe2\x80\x98environment\xe2\x80\x99 (as I have\r\ntried to explain at p. 123) makes all the difference between man and\r\nman; and I rejoice to think that many of these profane stories\xe2\x80\x94which _I_\r\nfind so painful to listen to, and should feel it a sin to repeat\xe2\x80\x94give to\r\n_their_ ears no pain, and to _their_ consciences no shock; and that\r\n_they_ can utter, not less sincerely than myself, the two prayers,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hallowed be Thy Name_\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c_from hardness of heart, and contempt of\r\nThy Word and Commandment, Good Lord, deliver us!_\xe2\x80\x9d To which I would\r\ndesire to add, for their sake and for my own, Keble\xe2\x80\x99s beautiful\r\npetition, \xe2\x80\x9c_help us, this and every day, To live more nearly as we\r\npray!_\xe2\x80\x9d It is, in fact, for its _consequences_\xe2\x80\x94for the grave dangers,\r\nboth to speaker and to hearer, which it involves\xe2\x80\x94rather than for what it\r\nis _in itself_, that I mourn over this clerical habit of profanity in\r\nsocial talk. To the _believing_ hearer it brings the danger of loss of\r\nreverence for holy things, by the mere act of listening to, and\r\nenjoying, such jests; and also the temptation to retail them for the\r\namusement of others. To the _unbelieving_ hearer it brings a welcome\r\nconfirmation of his theory that religion is a fable, in the spectacle of\r\nits accredited champions thus betraying their trust. And to the speaker\r\nhimself it must surely bring the danger of _loss of faith_. For surely\r\nsuch jests, if uttered with no consciousness of harm, must necessarily\r\nbe also uttered with no consciousness, at the moment, of the _reality_\r\nof God, as a _living being_, who hears all we say. And he, who allows\r\nhimself the habit of thus uttering holy words, with no thought of their\r\nmeaning, is but too likely to find that, for him, God has become a myth,\r\nand heaven a poetic fancy\xe2\x80\x94that, for him, the light of life is gone, and\r\nthat he is at heart an atheist, lost in \xe2\x80\x9c_a darkness that may be felt_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere is, I fear, at the present time, an increasing tendency to\r\nirreverent treatment of the name of God and of subjects connected with\r\nreligion. Some of our theatres are helping this downward movement by the\r\ngross caricatures of clergymen which they put upon the stage: some of\r\nour clergy are themselves helping it, by showing that they can lay aside\r\nthe spirit of reverence, along with their surplices, and can treat as\r\njests, when _outside_ their churches, names and things to which they pay\r\nan almost superstitious veneration when _inside_: the \xe2\x80\x9cSalvation Army\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhas, I fear, with the best intentions, done much to help it, by the\r\ncoarse familiarity with which they treat holy things: and surely every\r\none, who desires to _live_ in the spirit of the prayer \xe2\x80\x9c_Hallowed be thy\r\nName_,\xe2\x80\x9d ought to do what he can, however little that may be, to check\r\nit. So I have gladly taken this unique opportunity, however unfit the\r\ntopic may seem for the Preface to a book of this kind, to express some\r\nthoughts which have weighed on my mind for a long time. I did not\r\nexpect, when I wrote the Preface to Vol. I, that it would be read to any\r\nappreciable extent: but I rejoice to believe, from evidence that has\r\nreached me, that it _has_ been read by many, and to hope that this\r\nPreface will also be so: and I think that, among them, some will be\r\nfound ready to sympathise with the views I have put forwards, and ready\r\nto help, with their prayers and their example, the revival, in Society,\r\nof the waning spirit of reverence.\r\n\r\n                                                      _Christmas_, 1893.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CONTENTS.\r\n\r\n\r\n  CHAPTER                                                            PAGE\r\n  I. \xe2\x80\x98IT IS LOVE!\xe2\x80\x99                                                       411\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                            SYLVIE AND BRUNO\r\n                               CONCLUDED.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CHAPTER I.\r\n                            BRUNO\xe2\x80\x99S LESSONS.\r\n\r\n\r\nDuring the next month or two my solitary town-life seemed, by contrast,\r\nunusually dull and tedious. I missed the pleasant friends I had left\r\nbehind at Elveston\xe2\x80\x94the genial interchange of thought\xe2\x80\x94the sympathy which\r\ngave to one\xe2\x80\x99s ideas a new and vivid reality: but, perhaps more than all,\r\nI missed the companionship of the two Fairies\xe2\x80\x94or Dream-Children, for I\r\nhad not yet solved the problem as to who or what they were\xe2\x80\x94whose sweet\r\nplayfulness had shed a magic radiance over my life.\r\n\r\nIn office-hours\xe2\x80\x94which I suppose reduce most men to the mental condition\r\nof a coffee-mill or a mangle\xe2\x80\x94time sped along much as usual: it was in\r\nthe pauses of life, the desolate hours when books and newspapers palled\r\non the sated appetite, and when, thrown back upon one\xe2\x80\x99s own dreary\r\nmusings, one strove\xe2\x80\x94all in vain\xe2\x80\x94to people the vacant air with the dear\r\nfaces of absent friends, that the real bitterness of solitude made\r\nitself felt.\r\n\r\nOne evening, feeling my life a little more wearisome than usual, I\r\nstrolled down to my Club, not so much with the hope of meeting any\r\nfriend there, for London was now \xe2\x80\x98out of town,\xe2\x80\x99 as with the feeling that\r\nhere, at least, I should hear \xe2\x80\x98sweet words of human speech,\xe2\x80\x99 and come\r\ninto contact with human thought.\r\n\r\nHowever, almost the first face I saw there _was_ that of a friend. Eric\r\nLindon was lounging, with rather a \xe2\x80\x98bored\xe2\x80\x99 expression of face, over a\r\nnewspaper; and we fell into conversation with a mutual satisfaction\r\nwhich neither of us tried to conceal.\r\n\r\nAfter a while I ventured to introduce what was just then the main\r\nsubject of my thoughts. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so the Doctor\xe2\x80\x9d (a name we had adopted by a\r\ntacit agreement, as a convenient compromise between the formality of\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Doctor Forester\xe2\x80\x99 and the intimacy\xe2\x80\x94to which Eric Lindon hardly seemed\r\nentitled\xe2\x80\x94of \xe2\x80\x98Arthur\xe2\x80\x99) \xe2\x80\x9chas gone abroad by this time, I suppose? Can you\r\ngive me his present address?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is still at Elveston\xe2\x80\x94I believe,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I have not been\r\nthere since I last met you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI did not know which part of this intelligence to wonder at most. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nmight I ask\xe2\x80\x94if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t taking too much of a liberty\xe2\x80\x94when your\r\nwedding-bells are to\xe2\x80\x94or perhaps they _have_ rung, already?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eric, in a steady voice, which betrayed scarcely a trace of\r\nemotion: \xe2\x80\x9c_that_ engagement is at an end. I am still \xe2\x80\x98Benedick the\r\n_un_married man.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter this, the thick-coming fancies\xe2\x80\x94all radiant with new possibilities\r\nof happiness for Arthur\xe2\x80\x94were far too bewildering to admit of any further\r\nconversation, and I was only too glad to avail myself of the first\r\ndecent excuse, that offered itself, for retiring into silence.\r\n\r\nThe next day I wrote to Arthur, with as much of a reprimand for his long\r\nsilence as I could bring myself to put into words, begging him to tell\r\nme how the world went with him.\r\n\r\nNeeds must that three or four days\xe2\x80\x94possibly more\xe2\x80\x94should elapse before I\r\ncould receive his reply; and never had I known days drag their slow\r\nlength along with a more tedious indolence.\r\n\r\nTo while away the time, I strolled, one afternoon, into Kensington\r\nGardens, and, wandering aimlessly along any path that presented itself,\r\nI soon became aware that I had somehow strayed into one that was wholly\r\nnew to me. Still, my elfish experiences seemed to have so completely\r\nfaded out of my life that nothing was further from my thoughts than the\r\nidea of again meeting my fairy-friends, when I chanced to notice a small\r\ncreature, moving among the grass that fringed the path, that did not\r\nseem to be an insect, or a frog, or any other living thing that I could\r\nthink of. Cautiously kneeling down, and making an _ex tempore_ cage of\r\nmy two hands, I imprisoned the little wanderer, and felt a sudden thrill\r\nof surprise and delight on discovering that my prisoner was no other\r\nthan _Bruno_ himself!\r\n\r\nBruno took the matter _very_ coolly, and, when I had replaced him on the\r\nground, where he would be within easy conversational distance, he began\r\ntalking, just as if it were only a few minutes since last we had met.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoos oo know what the _Rule_ is,\xe2\x80\x9d he enquired, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen oo catches a\r\nFairy, withouten its having tolded oo where it was?\xe2\x80\x9d (Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s notions of\r\nEnglish Grammar had certainly _not_ improved since our last meeting.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know there was any Rule about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _think_ oo\xe2\x80\x99ve got a right to _eat_ me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the little fellow,\r\nlooking up into my face with a winning smile. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m not pruffickly\r\nsure. Oo\xe2\x80\x99d better not do it wizout asking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt did indeed seem reasonable not to take so irrevocable a step as\r\n_that_, without due enquiry. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll certainly _ask_ about it, first,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cBesides, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know yet whether you would be _worth_ eating!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI guess I\xe2\x80\x99m _deliciously_ good to eat,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno remarked in a satisfied\r\ntone, as if it were something to be rather proud of.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what are you doing here, Bruno?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_That\xe2\x80\x99s_ not my name!\xe2\x80\x9d said my cunning little friend. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t oo know my\r\nname\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98Oh Bruno!\xe2\x80\x99? That\xe2\x80\x99s what Sylvie always calls me, when I says mine\r\nlessons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, what are you doing here, oh Bruno?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoing mine lessons, a-course!\xe2\x80\x9d With that roguish twinkle in his eye,\r\nthat always came when he knew he was talking nonsense.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _that\xe2\x80\x99s_ the way you do your lessons, is it? And do you remember\r\nthem well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways can \xe2\x80\x99member _mine_ lessons,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s _Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s_\r\nlessons that\xe2\x80\x99s so _dreffully_ hard to \xe2\x80\x99member!\xe2\x80\x9d He frowned, as if in\r\nagonies of thought, and tapped his forehead with his knuckles. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\n_ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_ think enough to understand them!\xe2\x80\x9d he said despairingly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwants _double_ thinking, I believe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where\xe2\x80\x99s Sylvie gone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just what _I_ want to know!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno disconsolately. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\never\xe2\x80\x99s the good of setting me lessons, when she isn\xe2\x80\x99t here to \xe2\x80\x99splain\r\nthe hard bits?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ find her for you!\xe2\x80\x9d I volunteered; and, getting up, I wandered\r\nround the tree under whose shade I had been reclining, looking on all\r\nsides for Sylvie. In another minute I _again_ noticed some strange thing\r\nmoving among the grass, and, kneeling down, was immediately confronted\r\nwith Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s innocent face, lighted up with a joyful surprise at seeing\r\nme, and was accosted, in the sweet voice I knew so well, with what\r\nseemed to be the _end_ of a sentence whose beginning I had failed to\r\ncatch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94and I think he ought to have _finished_ them by this time. So I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ngoing back to him. Will you come too? It\xe2\x80\x99s only just round at the other\r\nside of this tree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was but a few steps for _me_; but it was a great many for Sylvie; and\r\nI had to be very careful to walk slowly, in order not to leave the\r\nlittle creature so far behind as to lose sight of her.\r\n\r\nTo find Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s _lessons_ was easy enough: they appeared to be neatly\r\nwritten out on large smooth ivy-leaves, which were scattered in some\r\nconfusion over a little patch of ground where the grass had been worn\r\naway; but the pale student, who ought by rights to have been bending\r\nover them, was nowhere to be seen: we looked in all directions, for some\r\ntime, in vain; but at last Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s sharp eyes detected him, swinging on\r\na tendril of ivy, and Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s stern voice commanded his instant return\r\nto _terra firma_ and to the business of Life.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: SYLVIE\xe2\x80\x99S TRUANT-PUPIL]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleasure first and business afterwards\xe2\x80\x9d seemed to be the motto of these\r\ntiny folk, so many hugs and kisses had to be interchanged before\r\nanything else could be done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Bruno,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said reproachfully, \xe2\x80\x9cdidn\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you you were to\r\ngo on with your lessons, unless you heard to the contrary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I _did_ heard to the contrary!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno insisted, with a mischievous\r\ntwinkle in his eye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_ did you hear, you wicked boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt were a sort of noise in the air,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno: \xe2\x80\x9ca sort of a\r\nscrambling noise. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t _oo_ hear it, Mister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, anyhow, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t go to _sleep_ over them, you lazy-lazy!\xe2\x80\x9d For\r\nBruno had curled himself up, on the largest \xe2\x80\x98lesson,\xe2\x80\x99 and was arranging\r\nanother as a pillow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _wasn\xe2\x80\x99t_ asleep!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, in a deeply-injured tone. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I shuts\r\nmine eyes, it\xe2\x80\x99s to show that I\xe2\x80\x99m _awake_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, how much have you learned, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve learned a little tiny bit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, modestly, being evidently\r\nafraid of overstating his achievement. \xe2\x80\x9c_Ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_ learn no more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh Bruno! You know you _can_, if you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse I can, if I _like_,\xe2\x80\x9d the pale student replied; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t if\r\nI _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ like!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie had a way\xe2\x80\x94which I could not too highly admire\xe2\x80\x94of evading Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlogical perplexities by suddenly striking into a new line of thought;\r\nand this masterly stratagem she now adopted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must say _one_ thing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid oo know, Mister Sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno thoughtfully remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Sylvie\r\nca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t count? Whenever she says \xe2\x80\x98I must say _one_ thing,\xe2\x80\x99 I _know_ quite\r\nwell she\xe2\x80\x99ll say _two_ things! And she always doos.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo heads are better than one, Bruno,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, but with no very\r\ndistinct idea as to what I meant by it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind having two _heads_,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said softly to himself:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cone head to eat mine dinner, and one head to argue wiz Sylvie\xe2\x80\x94doos oo\r\nthink oo\xe2\x80\x99d look prettier if oo\xe2\x80\x99d got _two_ heads, Mister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe case did not, I assured him, admit of a doubt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe reason why Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s so cross\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno went on very seriously,\r\nalmost sadly.\r\n\r\nSylvie\xe2\x80\x99s eyes grew large and round with surprise at this new line of\r\nenquiry\xe2\x80\x94her rosy face being perfectly radiant with good humour. But she\r\nsaid nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it be better to tell me after the lessons are over?\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nsuggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said with a resigned air: \xe2\x80\x9conly she wo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t be cross\r\nthen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s only three lessons to do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cSpelling, and\r\nGeography, and Singing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot _Arithmetic_?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he hasn\xe2\x80\x99t a head for Arithmetic\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse I haven\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cMine head\xe2\x80\x99s for _hair_. The\r\nProfessor said so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie was arranging some letters on a board\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94E\xe2\x80\x94V\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94L. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Bruno,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat does _that_ spell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno looked at it, in solemn silence, for a minute. \xe2\x80\x9cI knows what it\r\n_doosn\xe2\x80\x99t_ spell!\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s no good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat _does_ it spell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno took another look at the mysterious letters. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98LIVE,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nbackwards!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. (I thought it was, indeed.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow _did_ you manage to see that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI just twiddled my eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, \xe2\x80\x9cand then I saw it directly. Now\r\nmay I sing the King-fisher Song?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeography next,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know the Rules?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thinks there oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to be such a lot of Rules, Sylvie! I thinks\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, there _ought_ to be such a lot of Rules, you wicked, wicked boy!\r\nAnd how dare you _think_ at all about it? And shut up that mouth\r\ndirectly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo, as \xe2\x80\x98that mouth\xe2\x80\x99 didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem inclined to shut up of itself, Sylvie\r\nshut it for him\xe2\x80\x94with both hands\xe2\x80\x94and sealed it with a kiss, just as you\r\nwould fasten up a letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that Bruno is fastened up from talking,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, turning to\r\nme, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll show you the Map he does his lessons on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd there it was, a large Map of the World, spread out on the ground. It\r\nwas so large that Bruno had to crawl about on it, to point out the\r\nplaces named in the \xe2\x80\x98King-fisher Lesson.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen a King-fisher sees a Lady-bird flying away, he says \xe2\x80\x98_Ceylon_, if\r\nyou _Candia_!\xe2\x80\x99 And when he catches it, he says \xe2\x80\x98Come to _Media_! And if\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re _Hungary_ or thirsty, I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you some _Nubia_!\xe2\x80\x99 When he takes\r\nit in his claws, he says \xe2\x80\x98_Europe!_\xe2\x80\x99 When he puts it into his beak, he\r\nsays \xe2\x80\x98_India!_\xe2\x80\x99 When he\xe2\x80\x99s swallowed it, he says \xe2\x80\x98_Eton!_\xe2\x80\x99 That\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s _quite_ perfect,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cNow you may sing the King-fisher\r\nSong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill _oo_ sing the chorus?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said to me.\r\n\r\nI was just beginning to say \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I don\xe2\x80\x99t know the _words_,\xe2\x80\x9d when\r\nSylvie silently turned the map over, and I found the words were all\r\nwritten on the back. In one respect it was a _very_ peculiar song: the\r\nchorus to each verse came in the _middle_, instead of at the _end_ of\r\nit. However, the tune was so easy that I soon picked it up, and managed\r\nthe chorus as well, perhaps, as it is possible for _one_ person to\r\nmanage such a thing. It was in vain that I signed to Sylvie to help me:\r\nshe only smiled sweetly and shook her head.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cKing Fisher courted Lady Bird\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  _Sing Beans, sing Bones, sing Butterflies!_\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98Find me my match,\xe2\x80\x99 he said,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98With such a noble head\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  With such a beard, as white as curd\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    With such expressive eyes!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yet pins have heads,\xe2\x80\x99 said Lady Bird\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  _Sing Prunes, sing Prawns, sing Primrose-Hill!_\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98And, where you stick them in,\r\n    They stay, and thus a pin\r\n  Is very much to be preferred\r\n    To one that\xe2\x80\x99s never still!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Oysters have one single word\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    Not if you crowned them Kings!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Needles have eyes,\xe2\x80\x99 said Lady Bird\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  _Sing Cats, sing Corks, _me_!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: KING FISHER\xe2\x80\x99S WOOING]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he went away,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno added as a kind of postscript, when the last\r\nnote of the beginning to glitter.\r\n\r\n_Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s_ eyes filled with tears in a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it, Bruno,\r\n_darling_!\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered; and the rest of the argument was lost \xe2\x80\x98amid\r\nthe tangles of Ne\xc3\xa6ra\xe2\x80\x99s hair,\xe2\x80\x99 while the two disputants hugged and kissed\r\neach other.\r\n\r\nBut this new form of argument was brought to a sudden end by a flash of\r\nlightning, which was closely followed by a peal of thunder, and by a\r\ntorrent of rain-drops, which came hissing and spitting, almost like live\r\ncreatures, through the leaves of the tree that sheltered us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s raining cats and dogs!\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd all the _dogs_ has come down _first_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno: \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing\r\nbut _cats_ coming down now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn another minute the pattering ceased, as suddenly as it had begun. I\r\nstepped out from under the tree, and found that the storm was over; but\r\nI looked in vain, on my return, for my tiny companions. They had\r\nvanished with the storm, and there was nothing for it but to make the\r\nbest of my way home.\r\n\r\nOn the table lay, awaiting my return, an envelope of that peculiar\r\nyellow tint which always announces a telegram, and which must be, in the\r\nmemories of so many of us, inseparably linked with some great and sudden\r\nsorrow\xe2\x80\x94something that has cast a shadow, never in this world to be\r\nwholly lifted off, on the brightness of Life. No doubt it has _also_\r\nheralded\xe2\x80\x94for many of us\xe2\x80\x94some sudden news of joy; but this, I think, is\r\nless common: human life seems, on the whole, to contain more of sorrow\r\nthan of joy. And yet the world goes on. Who knows why?\r\n\r\nThis time, however, there was no shock of sorrow to be faced: in fact,\r\nthe few words it contained (\xe2\x80\x9cCould not bring myself to write. Come soon.\r\nAlways welcome. A letter follows this. Arthur.\xe2\x80\x9d) seemed so like Arthur\r\nhimself speaking, that it gave me quite a thrill of pleasure, and I at\r\nonce began the preparations needed for the journey.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER II.\r\n                             LOVE\xe2\x80\x99S CURFEW.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFayfield Junction! Change for Elveston!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat subtle memory could there be, linked to these commonplace words,\r\nthat caused such a flood of happy thoughts to fill my brain? I\r\ndismounted from the carriage in a state of joyful excitement for which I\r\ncould not at first account. True, I had taken this very journey, and at\r\nthe same hour of the day, six months ago; but many things had happened\r\nsince then, and an old man\xe2\x80\x99s memory has but a slender hold on recent\r\nevents: I sought \xe2\x80\x98the missing link\xe2\x80\x99 in vain. Suddenly I caught sight of\r\na bench\xe2\x80\x94the only one provided on the cheerless platform\xe2\x80\x94with a lady\r\nseated on it, and the whole forgotten scene flashed upon me as vividly\r\nas if it were happening over again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought. \xe2\x80\x9cThis bare platform is, for me, rich with the memory\r\nof a dear friend! She was sitting on that very bench, and invited me to\r\nshare it, with some quotation from Shakespeare\xe2\x80\x94I forget what. I\xe2\x80\x99ll try\r\nthe Earl\xe2\x80\x99s plan for the Dramatisation of Life, and fancy that figure to\r\nbe Lady Muriel; and I won\xe2\x80\x99t undeceive myself too soon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I strolled along the platform, resolutely \xe2\x80\x98making-believe\xe2\x80\x99 (as\r\nchildren say) that the casual passenger, seated on that bench, was the\r\nLady Muriel I remembered so well. She was facing away from me, which\r\naided the elaborate cheatery I was practising on myself: but, though I\r\nwas careful, in passing the spot, to look the other way, in order to\r\nprolong the pleasant illusion, it was inevitable that, when I turned to\r\nwalk back again, I should see who it was. It was Lady Muriel herself!\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98SPEND IT ALL FOR MINNIE\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\nThe whole scene now returned vividly to my memory; and, to make this\r\nrepetition of it stranger still, there was the same old man, whom I\r\nremembered seeing so roughly ordered off, by the Station-Master, to make\r\nroom for his titled passenger. The same, but \xe2\x80\x98with a difference\xe2\x80\x99: no\r\nlonger tottering feebly along the platform, but actually seated at Lady\r\nMuriel\xe2\x80\x99s side, and in conversation with her! \xe2\x80\x9cYes, put it in your\r\npurse,\xe2\x80\x9d she was saying, \xe2\x80\x9cand remember you\xe2\x80\x99re to spend it all for\r\n_Minnie_. And mind you bring her something nice, that\xe2\x80\x99ll do her real\r\ngood! And give her my love!\xe2\x80\x9d So intent was she on saying these words,\r\nthat, although the sound of my footstep had made her lift her head and\r\nlook at me, she did not at first recognise me.\r\n\r\nI raised my hat as I approached, and then there flashed across her face\r\na genuine look of joy, which so exactly recalled the sweet face of\r\nSylvie, when last we met in Kensington Gardens, that I felt quite\r\nbewildered.\r\n\r\nRather than disturb the poor old man at her side, she rose from her\r\nseat, and joined me in my walk up and down the platform, and for a\r\nminute or two our conversation was as utterly trivial and commonplace as\r\nif we were merely two casual guests in a London drawing-room. Each of us\r\nseemed to shrink, just at first, from touching on the deeper interests\r\nwhich linked our lives together.\r\n\r\nThe Elveston train had drawn up at the platform, while we talked; and,\r\nin obedience to the Station-Master\xe2\x80\x99s obsequious hint of \xe2\x80\x9cThis way, my\r\nLady! Time\xe2\x80\x99s up!\xe2\x80\x9d, we were making the best of our way towards the end\r\nwhich contained the sole first-class carriage, and were just passing the\r\nnow-empty bench, when Lady Muriel noticed, lying on it, the purse in\r\nwhich her gift had just been so carefully bestowed, the owner of which,\r\nall unconscious of his loss, was being helped into a carriage at the\r\nother end of the train. She pounced on it instantly. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor old man!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ncried. \xe2\x80\x9cHe mustn\xe2\x80\x99t go off, and think he\xe2\x80\x99s lost it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet _me_ run with it! I can go quicker than you!\xe2\x80\x9d I said. But she was\r\nalready half-way down the platform, flying (\xe2\x80\x98running\xe2\x80\x99 is much too\r\nmundane a word for such fairy-like motion) at a pace that left all\r\npossible efforts of _mine_ hopelessly in the rear.\r\n\r\nShe was back again before I had well completed my audacious boast of\r\nspeed in running, and was saying, quite demurely, as we entered our\r\ncarriage, \xe2\x80\x9cand you really think _you_ could have done it quicker?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d I replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI plead \xe2\x80\x98Guilty\xe2\x80\x99 of gross exaggeration, and\r\nthrow myself on the mercy of the Court!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Court will overlook it\xe2\x80\x94for this once!\xe2\x80\x9d Then her manner suddenly\r\nchanged from playfulness to an anxious gravity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not looking your best!\xe2\x80\x9d she said with an anxious glance. \xe2\x80\x9cIn\r\nfact, I think you look _more_ of an invalid than when you left us. I\r\nvery much doubt if London agrees with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _may_ be the London air,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cor it may be the hard work\xe2\x80\x94or my\r\nrather lonely life: anyhow, I\xe2\x80\x99ve _not_ been feeling very well, lately.\r\nBut Elveston will soon set me up again. Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s prescription\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s my\r\ndoctor, you know, and I heard from him this morning\xe2\x80\x94is \xe2\x80\x98plenty of ozone,\r\nand new milk, and _pleasant society_\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleasant society?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, with a pretty make-believe of\r\nconsidering the question. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, really I don\xe2\x80\x99t know where we can find\r\n_that_ for you! We have so few neighbours. But new milk we _can_ manage.\r\nDo get it of my old friend Mrs. Hunter, up there, on the hill-side. You\r\nmay rely upon the _quality_. And her little Bessie comes to school every\r\nday, and passes your lodgings. So it would be very easy to send it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll follow your advice, with pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and\r\narrange about it tomorrow. I know Arthur will want a walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find it quite an easy walk\xe2\x80\x94under three miles, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now that we\xe2\x80\x99ve settled that point, let me retort your own remark\r\nupon yourself. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think _you\xe2\x80\x99re_ looking quite your best!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI daresay not,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied in a low voice; and a sudden shadow seemed\r\nto overspread her face. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had some troubles lately. It\xe2\x80\x99s a matter\r\nabout which I\xe2\x80\x99ve been long wishing to consult you, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t easily\r\nwrite about it. I\xe2\x80\x99m _so_ glad to have this opportunity!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think,\xe2\x80\x9d she began again, after a minute\xe2\x80\x99s silence, and with a\r\nvisible embarrassment of manner most unusual in her, \xe2\x80\x9cthat a promise,\r\ndeliberately and solemnly given, is _always_ binding\xe2\x80\x94except, of course,\r\nwhere its fulfilment would involve some actual _sin_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t think of any other exception at this moment,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nbranch of casuistry is usually, I believe, treated as a question of\r\ntruth and untruth\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely that _is_ the principle?\xe2\x80\x9d she eagerly interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cI always\r\nthought the Bible-teaching about it consisted of such texts as \xe2\x80\x98_lie not\r\none to another_\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have thought about that point,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied; \xe2\x80\x9cand it seems to me that\r\nthe essence of _lying_ is the intention of _deceiving_. If you give a\r\npromise, fully _intending_ to fulfil it, you are certainly acting\r\ntruthfully _then_; and, if you afterwards break it, that does not\r\ninvolve any _deception_. I cannot call it _untruthful_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother pause of silence ensued. Lady Muriel\xe2\x80\x99s face was hard to read:\r\nshe looked pleased, I thought, but also puzzled; and I felt curious to\r\nknow whether her question had, as I began to suspect, some bearing on\r\nthe breaking off of her engagement with Captain (now Major) Lindon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have relieved me from a great fear,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut the thing is of\r\ncourse _wrong_, somehow. What texts would _you_ quote, to prove it\r\nwrong?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny that enforce the payment of _debts_. If _A_ promises something to\r\n_B_, _B_ has a claim upon _A_. And _A_\xe2\x80\x99s sin, if he breaks his promise,\r\nseems to me more analogous to _stealing_ than to _lying_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a new way of looking at it\xe2\x80\x94to me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it seems a\r\n_true_ way, also. However, I won\xe2\x80\x99t deal in generalities, with an old\r\nfriend like you! For we _are_ old friends, somehow. Do you know, I think\r\nwe _began_ as old friends?\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a playfulness of tone that ill\r\naccorded with the tears that glistened in her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you very much for saying so,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI like to think of you\r\nas an _old_ friend,\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94though you don\xe2\x80\x99t look it!\xe2\x80\x9d would have been the\r\nalmost necessary sequence, with any other lady; but she and I seemed to\r\nhave long passed out of the time when compliments, or any such\r\ntrivialities, were possible.)\r\n\r\nHere the train paused at a station, where two or three passengers\r\nentered the carriage; so no more was said till we had reached our\r\njourney\xe2\x80\x99s end.\r\n\r\nOn our arrival at Elveston, she readily adopted my suggestion that we\r\nshould walk up together; so, as soon as our luggage had been duly taken\r\ncharge of\xe2\x80\x94hers by the servant who met her at the station, and mine by\r\none of the porters\xe2\x80\x94we set out together along the familiar lanes, now\r\nlinked in my memory with so many delightful associations. Lady Muriel at\r\nonce recommenced the conversation at the point where it had been\r\ninterrupted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou knew of my engagement to my cousin Eric. Did you also hear\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted, anxious to spare her the pain of giving any\r\ndetails. \xe2\x80\x9cI heard it had all come to an end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would like to tell you how it happened,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cas that is the\r\nvery point I want your advice about. I had long realised that we were\r\nnot in sympathy in religious belief. His ideas of Christianity are very\r\nshadowy; and even as to the existence of a God he lives in a sort of\r\ndreamland. But it has not affected his life! I feel sure, now, that the\r\nmost absolute Atheist _may_ be leading, though walking blindfold, a pure\r\nand noble life. And if you knew half the good deeds\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she broke off\r\nsuddenly, and turned away her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI entirely agree with you,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd have we not our Saviour\xe2\x80\x99s own\r\npromise that such a life shall surely lead to the light?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a broken voice, still keeping her head\r\nturned away. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so I told him. He said he would believe, for _my_\r\nsake, if he could. And he wished, for _my_ sake, he could see things as\r\nI did. But that is all wrong!\xe2\x80\x9d she went on passionately. \xe2\x80\x9cGod _cannot_\r\napprove such low motives as that! Still it was not _I_ that broke it\r\noff. I knew he loved me; and I had _promised_; and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it was _he_ that broke it off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe released me unconditionally.\xe2\x80\x9d She faced me again now, having quite\r\nrecovered her usual calmness of manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what difficulty remains?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is _this_, that I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe he did it of his own free will. Now,\r\nsupposing he did it _against_ his will, merely to satisfy my scruples,\r\nwould not his claim on me remain just as strong as ever? And would not\r\nmy promise be as binding as ever? My father says \xe2\x80\x98no\xe2\x80\x99; but I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nfearing he is biased by his love for me. And I\xe2\x80\x99ve asked no one else. I\r\nhave many friends\xe2\x80\x94friends for the bright sunny weather; not friends for\r\nthe clouds and storms of life; not _old_ friends like you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me think a little,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: and for some minutes we walked on in\r\nsilence, while, pained to the heart at seeing the bitter trial that had\r\ncome upon this pure and gentle soul, I strove in vain to see my way\r\nthrough the tangled skein of conflicting motives.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she loves him truly,\xe2\x80\x9d (I seemed at last to grasp the clue to the\r\nproblem) \xe2\x80\x9cis not _that_, for her, the voice of God? May she not hope\r\nthat she is sent to him, even as Ananias was sent to Saul in his\r\nblindness, that he may receive his sight?\xe2\x80\x9d Once more I seemed to hear\r\nArthur whispering \xe2\x80\x9c_What knowest thou, O wife, whether thou shalt save\r\nthy husband?_\xe2\x80\x9d and I broke the silence with the words \xe2\x80\x9cIf you still love\r\nhim truly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do _not_!\xe2\x80\x9d she hastily interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least\xe2\x80\x94not in _that_ way. I\r\n_believe_ I loved him when I promised; but I was very young: it is hard\r\nto know. But, whatever the feeling was, it is dead _now_. The motive on\r\n_his_ side is Love: on _mine_ it is\xe2\x80\x94Duty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain there was a long silence. The whole skein of thought was tangled\r\nworse than ever. This time _she_ broke the silence. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t misunderstand\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I said my heart was not _his_, I did not mean it\r\nwas any one else\xe2\x80\x99s! At present I feel bound to _him_; and, till I know I\r\nam absolutely free, in the sight of God, to love any other than him,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll never even _think_ of any one else\xe2\x80\x94in _that_ way, I mean. I would\r\ndie sooner!\xe2\x80\x9d I had never imagined my gentle friend capable of such\r\npassionate utterances.\r\n\r\nI ventured on no further remark until we had nearly arrived at the\r\nHall-gate; but, the longer I reflected, the clearer it became to me that\r\nno call of Duty demanded the sacrifice\xe2\x80\x94possibly of the happiness of a\r\nlife\xe2\x80\x94which she seemed ready to make. I tried to make this clear to _her_\r\nalso, adding some warnings on the dangers that surely awaited a union in\r\nwhich mutual love was wanting. \xe2\x80\x9cThe only argument for it, worth\r\nconsidering,\xe2\x80\x9d I said in conclusion, \xe2\x80\x9cseems to be his supposed\r\n_reluctance_ in releasing you from your promise. I have tried to give to\r\nthat argument its _full_ weight, and my conclusion is that it does _not_\r\naffect the rights of the case, or invalidate the release he has given\r\nyou. My belief is that you are _entirely_ free to act as _now_ seems\r\nright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am _very_ grateful to you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cBelieve it, please!\r\nI ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t put it into proper words!\xe2\x80\x9d and the subject was dropped by\r\nmutual consent: and I only learned, long afterwards, that our discussion\r\nhad really served to dispel the doubts that had harassed her so long.\r\n\r\nWe parted at the Hall-gate, and I found Arthur eagerly awaiting my\r\narrival; and, before we parted for the night, I had heard the whole\r\nstory\xe2\x80\x94how he had put off his journey from day to day, feeling that he\r\n_could_ not go away from the place till his fate had been irrevocably\r\nsettled by the wedding taking place: how the preparations for the\r\nwedding, and the excitement in the neighbourhood, had suddenly come to\r\nan end, and he had learned (from Major Lindon, who called to wish him\r\ngood-bye) that the engagement had been broken off by mutual consent: how\r\nhe had instantly abandoned all his plans for going abroad, and had\r\ndecided to stay on at Elveston, for a year or two at any rate, till his\r\nnewly-awakened hopes should prove true or false; and how, since that\r\nmemorable day, he had avoided all meetings with Lady Muriel, fearing to\r\nbetray his feelings before he had had any sufficient evidence as to how\r\nshe regarded him. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is nearly six weeks since all that happened,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said in conclusion, \xe2\x80\x9cand we can meet in the ordinary way, now, with\r\nno need for any painful allusions. I would have written to tell you all\r\nthis: only I kept hoping from day to day, that\xe2\x80\x94that there would be\r\n_more_ to tell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how should there be _more_, you foolish fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d I fondly urged,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cif you never even go near her? Do you expect the offer to come from\r\n_her_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur was betrayed into a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI hardly expect\r\n_that_. But I\xe2\x80\x99m a desperate coward. There\xe2\x80\x99s no doubt about it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what _reasons_ have you heard of for breaking off the engagement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good many,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur replied, and proceeded to count them on his\r\nfingers. \xe2\x80\x9cFirst, it was found that she was dying of\xe2\x80\x94something; so _he_\r\nbroke it off. Then it was found that _he_ was dying of\xe2\x80\x94some other thing;\r\nso _she_ broke it off. Then the Major turned out to be a confirmed\r\ngamester; so the _Earl_ broke it off. Then the Earl insulted him; so the\r\n_Major_ broke it off. It got a good deal broken off, all things\r\nconsidered!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have all this on the very best authority, of course?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, certainly! And communicated in the strictest confidence! Whatever\r\ndefects Elveston society suffers from, _want of information_ isn\xe2\x80\x99t one\r\nof them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor _reticence_, either, it seems. But, seriously, do you know the real\r\nreason?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m quite in the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI did not feel that I had any right to enlighten him; so I changed the\r\nsubject, to the less engrossing one of \xe2\x80\x9cnew milk,\xe2\x80\x9d and we agreed that I\r\nshould walk over, next day, to Hunter\xe2\x80\x99s farm, Arthur undertaking to set\r\nme part of the way, after which he had to return to keep a\r\nbusiness-engagement.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER III.\r\n                            STREAKS OF DAWN.\r\n\r\n\r\nNext day proved warm and sunny, and we started early, to enjoy the\r\nluxury of a good long chat before he would be obliged to leave me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis neighbourhood has more than its due proportion of the _very_\r\npoor,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked, as we passed a group of hovels, too dilapidated to\r\ndeserve the name of \xe2\x80\x9ccottages.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the few rich,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur replied, \xe2\x80\x9cgive more than their due proportion\r\nof help in charity. So the balance is kept.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose the _Earl_ does a good deal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe _gives_ liberally; but he has not the health or strength to do more.\r\nLady Muriel does more in the way of school-teaching and cottage-visiting\r\nthan she would like me to reveal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen _she_, at least, is not one of the \xe2\x80\x98idle mouths\xe2\x80\x99 one so often\r\nmeets with among the upper classes. I have sometimes thought they would\r\nhave a hard time of it, if suddenly called on to give their _raison\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99\xc3\xaatre_, and to show cause why they should be allowed to live any\r\nlonger!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe whole subject,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, \xe2\x80\x9cof what we may call \xe2\x80\x98idle mouths\xe2\x80\x99 (I\r\nmean persons who absorb some of the material _wealth_ of a community\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nthe form of food, clothes, and so on\xe2\x80\x94without contributing its equivalent\r\nin the form of productive _labour_) is a complicated one, no doubt. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ntried to think it out. And it seemed to me that the simplest form of the\r\nproblem, to start with, is a community without _money_, who buy and sell\r\nby _barter_ only; and it makes it yet simpler to suppose the food and\r\nother things to be capable of _keeping_ for many years without\r\nspoiling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYours is an excellent plan,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your solution of the\r\nproblem?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe commonest type of \xe2\x80\x98idle mouths,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, \xe2\x80\x9cis no doubt due to\r\nmoney being left by parents to their own children. So I imagined a\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94either exceptionally clever, or exceptionally strong and\r\nindustrious\xe2\x80\x94who had contributed so much valuable labour to the needs of\r\nthe community that its equivalent, in clothes, &c., was (say) five times\r\nas much as he needed for himself. We cannot deny his _absolute_ right to\r\ngive the superfluous wealth as he chooses. So, if he leaves _four_\r\nchildren behind him (say two sons and two daughters), with enough of all\r\nthe necessaries of life to last them a life-time, I cannot see that the\r\n_community_ is in any way wronged if they choose to do nothing in life\r\nbut to \xe2\x80\x98eat, drink, and be merry.\xe2\x80\x99 Most certainly, the community could\r\nnot fairly say, in reference to _them_, \xe2\x80\x98_if a man will not work,\r\nneither let him eat_.\xe2\x80\x99 Their reply would be crushing. \xe2\x80\x98The labour has\r\nalready been _done_, which is a fair equivalent for the food we are\r\neating; and you have had the benefit of it. On what principle of justice\r\ncan you demand _two_ quotas of work for _one_ quota of food?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet surely,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is something wrong _somewhere_, if these\r\nfour people are well able to do useful work, and if that work is\r\nactually _needed_ by the community, and they elect to sit idle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think there _is_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur: \xe2\x80\x9cbut it seems to me to arise from a\r\nLaw of God\xe2\x80\x94that every one shall do as much as he can to help others\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nnot from any _rights_, on the part of the community, to exact labour as\r\nan equivalent for food that has already been fairly earned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose the _second_ form of the problem is where the \xe2\x80\x98idle mouths\xe2\x80\x99\r\npossess _money_ instead of _material_ wealth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Arthur: \xe2\x80\x9cand I think the simplest case is that of\r\n_paper_-money. _Gold_ is itself a form of material wealth; but a\r\nbank-note is merely a _promise_ to hand over so much _material_ wealth\r\nwhen called upon to do so. The father of these four \xe2\x80\x98idle mouths,\xe2\x80\x99 had\r\ndone (let us say) five thousand pounds\xe2\x80\x99 worth of useful work for the\r\ncommunity. In return for this, the community had given him what amounted\r\nto a written promise to hand over, whenever called upon to do so, five\r\nthousand pounds\xe2\x80\x99 worth of food, &c. Then, if he only uses _one_ thousand\r\npounds\xe2\x80\x99 worth himself, and leaves the rest of the notes to his children,\r\nsurely they have a full right to _present_ these written promises, and\r\nto say \xe2\x80\x98hand over the food, for which the equivalent labour has been\r\nalready done.\xe2\x80\x99 Now I think _this_ case well worth stating, publicly and\r\nclearly. I should like to drive it into the heads of those Socialists\r\nwho are priming our ignorant paupers with such sentiments as \xe2\x80\x98Look at\r\nthem bloated haristocrats! Doing not a stroke o\xe2\x80\x99 work for theirselves,\r\nand living on the sweat of _our_ brows!\xe2\x80\x99 I should like to _force_ them\r\nto see that the _money_, which those \xe2\x80\x98haristocrats\xe2\x80\x99 are spending,\r\nrepresents so much labour _already done_ for the community, and whose\r\nequivalent, in _material_ wealth, is _due from the community_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight not the Socialists reply \xe2\x80\x98Much of this money does not represent\r\n_honest_ labour _at all_. If you could trace it back, from owner to\r\nowner, though you might begin with several legitimate steps, such as\r\ngift, or bequeathing by will, or \xe2\x80\x98value received,\xe2\x80\x99 you would soon reach\r\nan owner who had no moral right to it, but had got it by fraud or other\r\ncrimes; and of course his successors in the line would have no better\r\nright to it than _he_ had.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt, no doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut surely that involves the\r\nlogical fallacy of _proving too much_? It is _quite_ as applicable to\r\n_material_ wealth, as it is to _money_. If we once begin to go back\r\nbeyond the fact that the _present_ owner of certain property came by it\r\nhonestly, and to ask whether any previous owner, in past ages, got it by\r\nfraud, would _any_ property be secure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a minute\xe2\x80\x99s thought, I felt obliged to admit the truth of this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy general conclusion,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur continued, \xe2\x80\x9cfrom the mere standpoint of\r\nhuman _rights_, man against man, was this\xe2\x80\x94that if some wealthy \xe2\x80\x98idle\r\nmouth,\xe2\x80\x99 who has come by his money in a lawful way, even though not one\r\natom of the labour it represents has been his own doing, chooses to\r\nspend it on his own needs, without contributing any labour to the\r\ncommunity from whom he buys his food and clothes, that community has no\r\n_right_ to interfere with him. But it\xe2\x80\x99s quite another thing, when we\r\ncome to consider the _divine_ law. Measured by _that_ standard, such a\r\nman is undoubtedly doing wrong, if he fails to use, for the good of\r\nthose in need, the strength or the skill, that God has given him. That\r\nstrength and skill do _not_ belong to the community, to be paid to\r\n_them_ as a _debt_: they do _not_ belong to the man _himself_, to be\r\nused for his _own_ enjoyment: they _do_ belong to God, to be used\r\naccording to _His_ will; and we are not left in doubt as to what that\r\nwill is. \xe2\x80\x98_Do good, and lend, hoping for nothing again._\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9can \xe2\x80\x98idle mouth\xe2\x80\x99 very often gives away a great deal in\r\ncharity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn _so-called_ \xe2\x80\x98charity,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d he corrected me. \xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me if I seem to\r\nspeak _un_charitably. I would not dream of _applying_ the term to any\r\n_individual_. But I would say, _generally_, that a man who gratifies\r\nevery fancy that occurs to him\xe2\x80\x94denying himself in _nothing_\xe2\x80\x94and merely\r\ngives to the poor some part, or even _all_, of his _superfluous_ wealth,\r\nis only deceiving himself if he calls it _charity_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, even in giving away _superfluous_ wealth, he _may_ be denying\r\nhimself the miser\xe2\x80\x99s pleasure in hoarding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI grant you that, gladly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cGiven that he _has_ that\r\nmorbid craving, he is doing a good deed in restraining it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, even in spending on _himself_,\xe2\x80\x9d I persisted, \xe2\x80\x9cour typical rich man\r\noften does good, by employing people who would otherwise be out of work:\r\nand that is often better than pauperising them by _giving_ the money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you\xe2\x80\x99ve said that!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cI would not like to quit the\r\nsubject without exposing the _two_ fallacies of that statement\xe2\x80\x94which\r\nhave gone so long uncontradicted that Society now accepts it as an\r\naxiom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are they?\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t even see _one_, myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne is merely the fallacy of _ambiguity_\xe2\x80\x94the assumption that \xe2\x80\x98_doing\r\ngood_\xe2\x80\x99 (that is, benefiting somebody) is necessarily _a good thing to\r\ndo_ (that is, a _right_ thing). The other is the assumption that, if one\r\nof two specified acts is _better_ than another, it is necessarily a\r\n_good_ act in itself. I should like to call this the fallacy of\r\n_comparison_\xe2\x80\x94meaning that it assumes that what is _comparatively_ good\r\nis therefore _positively_ good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what is _your_ test of a good act?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat it shall be _our best_,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur confidently replied. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd even\r\n_then_ \xe2\x80\x98_we are unprofitable servants_.\xe2\x80\x99 But let me illustrate the two\r\nfallacies. Nothing illustrates a fallacy so well as an extreme case,\r\nwhich fairly comes under it. Suppose I find two children drowning in a\r\npond. I rush in, and save one of the children, and then walk away,\r\nleaving the other to drown. Clearly I have \xe2\x80\x98_done good_,\xe2\x80\x99 in saving a\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x99s life? But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94. Again, supposing I meet an inoffensive stranger,\r\nand knock him down, and walk on. Clearly that is \xe2\x80\x98_better_\xe2\x80\x99 than if I\r\nhad proceeded to jump upon him and break his ribs? But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose \xe2\x80\x98buts\xe2\x80\x99 are quite unanswerable,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I should like an\r\ninstance from _real_ life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let us take one of those abominations of modern Society, a\r\nCharity-Bazaar. It\xe2\x80\x99s an interesting question to think out\xe2\x80\x94how much of\r\nthe money, that reaches the object in view, is _genuine_ charity; and\r\nwhether even _that_ is spent in the _best_ way. But the subject needs\r\nregular classification, and analysis, to understand it properly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be glad to _have_ it analysed,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cit has often puzzled\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if I am really not boring you. Let us suppose our Charity-Bazaar\r\nto have been organised to aid the funds of some Hospital: and that A, B,\r\nC _give_ their services in making articles to sell, and in acting as\r\nsalesmen, while X, Y, Z buy the articles, and the money so paid goes to\r\nthe Hospital.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are two distinct species of such Bazaars: one, where the payment\r\nexacted is merely the _market-value_ of the goods supplied, that is,\r\nexactly what you would have to pay at a shop: the other, where\r\n_fancy-prices_ are asked. We must take these separately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst, the \xe2\x80\x98market-value\xe2\x80\x99 case. Here A, B, C are exactly in the same\r\nposition as ordinary shopkeepers; the only difference being that they\r\ngive the proceeds to the Hospital. Practically, they are _giving their\r\nskilled labour_ for the benefit of the Hospital. This seems to me to be\r\ngenuine charity. And I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how they could use it better. But X, Y,\r\nZ, are exactly in the same position as any ordinary purchasers of goods.\r\nTo talk of \xe2\x80\x98charity\xe2\x80\x99 in connection with _their_ share of the business,\r\nis sheer nonsense. Yet they are very likely to do so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSecondly, the case of \xe2\x80\x98fancy-prices.\xe2\x80\x99 Here I think the simplest plan is\r\nto divide the payment into two parts, the \xe2\x80\x98market-value\xe2\x80\x99 and the excess\r\nover that. The \xe2\x80\x98market-value\xe2\x80\x99 part is on the same footing as in the\r\nfirst case: the _excess_ is all we have to consider. Well, A, B, C do\r\nnot _earn_ it; so we may put _them_ out of the question: it is a _gift_,\r\nfrom X, Y, Z, to the Hospital. And my opinion is that it is not given in\r\nthe best way: far better buy what they choose to _buy_, and give what\r\nthey choose to _give_, as two _separate_ transactions: then there is\r\n_some_ chance that their motive in giving may be real charity, instead\r\nof a mixed motive\xe2\x80\x94half charity, half self-pleasing. \xe2\x80\x98The trail of the\r\nserpent is over it all.\xe2\x80\x99 And _therefore_ it is that I hold all such\r\nspurious \xe2\x80\x98Charities\xe2\x80\x99 in _utter_ abomination!\xe2\x80\x9d He ended with unusual\r\nenergy, and savagely beheaded, with his stick, a tall thistle at the\r\nroad-side, behind which I was startled to see Sylvie and Bruno standing.\r\nI caught at his arm, but too late to stop him.
