Whether the stick reached\r\nthem, or not, I could not feel sure: at any rate they took not the\r\nsmallest notice of it, but smiled gaily, and nodded to me; and I saw at\r\nonce that they were only visible to _me_: the \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 influence had not\r\nreached to _Arthur_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you try to save it?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9c_That\xe2\x80\x99s_ not the wheedling\r\nSecretary of a Charity-Bazaar! I only wish it were!\xe2\x80\x9d he added grimly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoos oo know, that stick went right froo my head!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. (They\r\nhad run round to me by this time, and each had secured a hand.) \xe2\x80\x9cJust\r\nunder my chin! I _are_ glad I aren\xe2\x80\x99t a thistle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we\xe2\x80\x99ve threshed _that_ subject out, anyhow!\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nafraid I\xe2\x80\x99ve been talking too much, for _your_ patience and for my\r\nstrength. I must be turning soon. This is about the end of my tether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cTake, O boatman, thrice thy fee;\r\n  Take, I give it willingly;\r\n  For, invisible to thee,\r\n  Spirits twain have crossed with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI quoted, involuntarily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor utterly inappropriate and irrelevant quotations,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Arthur,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou are \xe2\x80\x98ekalled by few, and excelled by none\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d And we strolled on.\r\n\r\nAs we passed the head of the lane that led down to the beach, I noticed\r\na single figure, moving slowly along it, seawards. She was a good way\r\noff, and had her back to us: but it was Lady Muriel, unmistakably.\r\nKnowing that Arthur had not seen her, as he had been looking, in the\r\nother direction, at a gathering rain-cloud, I made no remark, but tried\r\nto think of some plausible pretext for sending him back by the sea.\r\n\r\nThe opportunity instantly presented itself. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m getting tired,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it would be prudent to go further. I had better\r\nturn here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI turned with him, for a few steps, and as we again approached the head\r\nof the lane, I said, as carelessly as I could, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t go back by the\r\nroad. It\xe2\x80\x99s too hot and dusty. Down this lane, and along the beach, is\r\nnearly as short; and you\xe2\x80\x99ll get a breeze off the sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think I will,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur began; but at that moment we came into\r\nsight of Lady Muriel, and he checked himself. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s too far round.\r\nYet it certainly _would_ be cooler\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He stood, hesitating, looking\r\nfirst one way and then the other\xe2\x80\x94a melancholy picture of utter infirmity\r\nof purpose!\r\n\r\nHow long this humiliating scene would have continued, if _I_ had been\r\nthe only external influence, it is impossible to say; for at this moment\r\nSylvie, with a swift decision worthy of Napoleon himself, took the\r\nmatter into her own hands. \xe2\x80\x9cYou go and drive _her_, up this way,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid to Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll get _him_ along!\xe2\x80\x9d And she took hold of the stick\r\nthat Arthur was carrying, and gently pulled him down the lane.\r\n\r\nHe was totally unconscious that any will but his own was acting on the\r\nstick, and appeared to think it had taken a horizontal position simply\r\nbecause he was pointing with it. \xe2\x80\x9cAre not those _orchises_ under the\r\nhedge there?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI think that decides me. I\xe2\x80\x99ll gather some as I\r\ngo along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98ARE NOT THOSE ORCHISES?\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Bruno had run on beyond Lady Muriel, and, with much jumping\r\nabout and shouting (shouts audible to no one but Sylvie and myself),\r\nmuch as if he were driving sheep, he managed to turn her round and make\r\nher walk, with eyes demurely cast upon the ground, in our direction.\r\n\r\nThe victory was ours! And, since it was evident that the lovers, thus\r\nurged together, _must_ meet in another minute, I turned and walked on,\r\nhoping that Sylvie and Bruno would follow my example, as I felt sure\r\nthat the fewer the spectators the better it would be for Arthur and his\r\ngood angel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what sort of meeting was it?\xe2\x80\x9d I wondered, as I paced dreamily on.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER IV.\r\n                             THE DOG-KING.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey shooked hands,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, who was trotting at my side, in answer\r\nto the unspoken question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they looked _ever_ so pleased!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie added from the other side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we must get on, now, as quick as we can,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cIf only I knew\r\nthe best way to Hunter\xe2\x80\x99s farm!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll be sure to know in this cottage,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose they will. Bruno, would you run in and ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie stopped him, laughingly, as he ran off. \xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI must make you _visible_ first, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd _audible_ too, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as she took the jewel, that hung\r\nround her neck, and waved it over his head, and touched his eyes and\r\nlips with it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie: \xe2\x80\x9cand _once_, do you know, I made him _audible_, and\r\nforgot to make him _visible_! And he went to buy some sweeties in a\r\nshop. And the man _was_ so frightened! A voice seemed to come out of the\r\nair, \xe2\x80\x98Please, I want two ounces of barley-sugar drops!\xe2\x80\x99 And a shilling\r\ncame _bang_ down upon the counter! And the man said \xe2\x80\x98I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t _see_\r\nyou!\xe2\x80\x99 And Bruno said \xe2\x80\x98It doosn\xe2\x80\x99t sinnify seeing _me_, so long as oo can\r\nsee the _shilling_!\xe2\x80\x99 But the man said he never sold barley-sugar drops\r\nto people he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t _see_. So we had to\xe2\x80\x94_Now_, Bruno, you\xe2\x80\x99re ready!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd away he trotted.\r\n\r\nSylvie spent the time, while we were waiting for him, in making\r\n_herself_ visible also. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s rather awkward, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained\r\nto me, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen we meet people, and they can see _one_ of us, and ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsee the _other_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a minute or two Bruno returned, looking rather disconsolate. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ngot friends with him, and he were _cross_!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe asked me who I\r\nwere. And I said \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m Bruno: who is _these_ peoples?\xe2\x80\x99 And he said \xe2\x80\x98One\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmy half-brother, and t\xe2\x80\x99other\xe2\x80\x99s my half-sister: and I don\xe2\x80\x99t want no more\r\ncompany! Go along with yer!\xe2\x80\x99 And I said \xe2\x80\x98I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t go along _wizout_ mine\r\nself!\xe2\x80\x99 And I said \xe2\x80\x98Oo shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have _bits_ of peoples lying about like\r\nthat! It\xe2\x80\x99s welly untidy!\xe2\x80\x99 And he said \xe2\x80\x98Oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t talk to _me_!\xe2\x80\x99 And he\r\npushted me outside! And he shutted the door!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you never asked where Hunter\xe2\x80\x99s farm was?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHadn\xe2\x80\x99t room for any questions,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cThe room were so crowded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree people _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ crowd a room,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey _did_, though,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno persisted. \xe2\x80\x9c_He_ crowded it most. He\xe2\x80\x99s such\r\na welly _thick_ man\xe2\x80\x94so as oo couldn\xe2\x80\x99t knock him down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI failed to see the drift of Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s argument. \xe2\x80\x9cSurely _anybody_ could\r\nbe knocked down,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cthick or thin wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOo couldn\xe2\x80\x99t knock _him_ down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s more wider than he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhigh: so, when he\xe2\x80\x99s lying down, he\xe2\x80\x99s more higher than when he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstanding: so a-course oo couldn\xe2\x80\x99t knock him _down_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s another cottage,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ ask the way, _this_ time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no need to go in, this time, as the woman was standing in the\r\ndoorway, with a baby in her arms, talking to a respectably dressed man\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nfarmer, as I guessed\xe2\x80\x94who seemed to be on his way to the town.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94and when there\xe2\x80\x99s _drink_ to be had,\xe2\x80\x9d he was saying, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s just the\r\nworst o\xe2\x80\x99 the lot, is your Willie. So they tell me. He gets fairly mad\r\nwi\xe2\x80\x99 it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d have given \xe2\x80\x99em the lie to their faces, a twelvemonth back!\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nwoman said in a broken voice. \xe2\x80\x9cBut a\xe2\x80\x99 canna noo! A\xe2\x80\x99 canna noo!\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nchecked herself, on catching sight of us, and hastily retreated into the\r\nhouse, shutting the door after her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you can tell me where Hunter\xe2\x80\x99s farm is?\xe2\x80\x9d I said to the man, as\r\nhe turned away from the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can _that_, Sir!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m John Hunter hissel,\r\nat your sarvice. It\xe2\x80\x99s nobbut half a mile further\xe2\x80\x94the only house in\r\nsight, when you get round bend o\xe2\x80\x99 the road yonder. You\xe2\x80\x99ll find my good\r\nwoman within, if so be you\xe2\x80\x99ve business wi\xe2\x80\x99 _her_. Or mebbe I\xe2\x80\x99ll do as\r\nwell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to order some milk. Perhaps I had better\r\narrange it with your wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man. \xe2\x80\x9c_She_ minds all _that_. Good day t\xe2\x80\x99ye, Master\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nto your bonnie childer, as well!\xe2\x80\x9d And he trudged on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe should have said \xe2\x80\x98_child_,\xe2\x80\x99 not \xe2\x80\x98_childer_\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cSylvie\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnot a _childer_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe meant _both_ of us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno persisted. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99cause he said \xe2\x80\x98bonnie\xe2\x80\x99, oo know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, at any rate he _looked_ at us both,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie maintained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then he _must_ have seen we\xe2\x80\x99re not _both_ bonnie!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno\r\nretorted. \xe2\x80\x9cA-_course_ I\xe2\x80\x99m much uglier than _oo_! Didn\xe2\x80\x99t he mean\r\n_Sylvie_, Mister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted over his shoulder, as he ran off.\r\n\r\nBut there was no use in replying, as he had already vanished round the\r\nbend of the road. When we overtook him he was climbing a gate, and was\r\ngazing earnestly into the field, where a horse, a cow, and a kid were\r\nbrowsing amicably together. \xe2\x80\x9cFor its father, a _Horse_,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured to\r\nhimself. \xe2\x80\x9cFor its mother, a _Cow_. For their dear little child, a\r\n_little_ Goat, is the most curiousest thing I ever seen in my world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBruno\xe2\x80\x99s World!\xe2\x80\x9d I pondered. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose every child has a world of\r\nhis own\xe2\x80\x94and every man, too, for the matter of that. I wonder if _that\xe2\x80\x99s_\r\nthe cause for all the misunderstanding there is in Life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat _must_ be Hunter\xe2\x80\x99s farm!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie, pointing to a house on the\r\nbrow of the hill, led up to by a cart-road. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no other farm in\r\nsight, _this_ way; and you _said_ we must be nearly there by this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had _thought_ it, while Bruno was climbing the gate, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nremember having _said_ it. However, Sylvie was evidently in the right.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet down, Bruno,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cand open the gate for us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a good thing we\xe2\x80\x99s with oo, _isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ it, Mister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, as\r\nwe entered the field. \xe2\x80\x9cThat big dog might have bited oo, if oo\xe2\x80\x99d been\r\nalone! Oo needn\xe2\x80\x99t be _flightened_ of it!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered, clinging tight\r\nto my hand to encourage me. \xe2\x80\x9cIt aren\xe2\x80\x99t fierce!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFierce!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie scornfully echoed, as the dog\xe2\x80\x94a magnificent\r\nNewfoundland\xe2\x80\x94that had come galloping down the field to meet us, began\r\ncurveting round us, in gambols full of graceful beauty, and welcoming us\r\nwith short joyful barks. \xe2\x80\x9cFierce! Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s as gentle as a lamb!\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94why, Bruno, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know it? It\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it _are_!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno, rushing forwards and throwing his arms round\r\nits neck. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you _dear_ dog!\xe2\x80\x9d And it seemed as if the two children\r\nwould never have done hugging and stroking it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how _ever_ did he get _here_?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cAsk him, Sylvie. I\r\ndoosn\xe2\x80\x99t know how.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then began an eager talk in Doggee, which of course was lost upon\r\n_me_; and I could only _guess_, when the beautiful creature, with a sly\r\nglance at me, whispered something in Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s ear, that _I_ was now the\r\nsubject of conversation. Sylvie looked round laughingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe asked me who you are,\xe2\x80\x9d she explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I said \xe2\x80\x98He\xe2\x80\x99s our\r\n_friend_.\xe2\x80\x99 And he said \xe2\x80\x98What\xe2\x80\x99s his name?\xe2\x80\x99 And I said \xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s _Mister\r\nSir_.\xe2\x80\x99 And he said \xe2\x80\x98Bosh!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is \xe2\x80\x98Bosh!\xe2\x80\x99 in Doggee?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the same as in English,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly, when a _dog_ says it,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s a sort of a whisper, that\xe2\x80\x99s half a _cough_ and half a _bark_. Nero,\r\nsay \xe2\x80\x98_Bosh!_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Nero, who had now begun gamboling round us again, said \xe2\x80\x9c_Bosh!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nseveral times; and I found that Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s description of the sound was\r\nperfectly accurate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what\xe2\x80\x99s behind this long wall?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as we walked on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the _Orchard_,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie replied, after a consultation with Nero.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee, there\xe2\x80\x99s a boy getting down off the wall, at that far corner. And\r\nnow he\xe2\x80\x99s running away across the field. I do believe he\xe2\x80\x99s been stealing\r\nthe apples!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno set off after him, but returned to us in a few moments, as he had\r\nevidently no chance of overtaking the young rascal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t catch him!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI wiss I\xe2\x80\x99d started a little sooner.\r\nHis pockets _was_ full of apples!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Dog-King looked up at Sylvie, and said something in Doggee.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of _course_ you can!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cHow stupid not to think\r\nof it! _Nero_\xe2\x80\x99ll hold him for us, Bruno! But I\xe2\x80\x99d better make him\r\ninvisible, first.\xe2\x80\x9d And she hastily got out the Magic Jewel, and began\r\nwaving it over Nero\xe2\x80\x99s head, and down along his back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99ll do!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno, impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter him, good Doggie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie exclaimed reproachfully. \xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have sent\r\nhim off so quick! I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t done the tail!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Nero was coursing like a greyhound down the field: so at least\r\nI concluded from all _I_ could see of him\xe2\x80\x94the long feathery tail, which\r\nfloated like a meteor through the air\xe2\x80\x94and in a very few seconds he had\r\ncome up with the little thief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s got him safe, by one foot!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sylvie, who was eagerly watching\r\nthe chase. \xe2\x80\x9cNow there\xe2\x80\x99s no hurry, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo we walked, quite leisurely, down the field, to where the frightened\r\nlad stood. A more curious sight I had seldom seen, in all my \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99\r\nexperiences. Every bit of him was in violent action, except the left\r\nfoot, which was apparently glued to the ground\xe2\x80\x94there being nothing\r\nvisibly holding it: while, at some little distance, the long feathery\r\ntail was waving gracefully from side to side, showing that Nero, at\r\nleast, regarded the whole affair as nothing but a magnificent game of\r\nplay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as gravely as I could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot the crahmp in me ahnkle!\xe2\x80\x9d the thief groaned in reply. \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 me fut\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngone to sleep!\xe2\x80\x9d And he began to blubber aloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, look here!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said in a commanding tone, getting in front of\r\nhim. \xe2\x80\x9cOo\xe2\x80\x99ve got to give up those apples!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lad glanced at me, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to reckon _my_ interference as\r\nworth anything. Then he glanced at Sylvie: _she_ clearly didn\xe2\x80\x99t count\r\nfor very much, either. Then he took courage. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll take a better man\r\nthan any of _yer_ to get \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d he retorted defiantly.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: A ROYAL THIEF-TAKER]\r\n\r\nSylvie stooped and patted the invisible Nero. \xe2\x80\x9cA _little_ tighter!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwhispered. And a sharp yell from the ragged boy showed how promptly the\r\nDog-King had taken the hint.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter _now_?\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cIs your ankle worse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it\xe2\x80\x99ll get worse, and worse, and worse,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno solemnly assured him,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctill oo gives up those apples!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nApparently the thief was convinced of this at last, and he sulkily began\r\nemptying his pockets of the apples. The children watched from a little\r\ndistance, Bruno dancing with delight at every fresh yell extracted from\r\nNero\xe2\x80\x99s terrified prisoner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all,\xe2\x80\x9d the boy said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ all!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s three more in that pocket!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother hint from Sylvie to the Dog-King\xe2\x80\x94another sharp yell from the\r\nthief, now convicted of lying also\xe2\x80\x94and the remaining three apples were\r\nsurrendered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him go, please,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said in Doggee, and the lad limped away at\r\na great pace, stooping now and then to rub the ailing ankle, in fear,\r\nseemingly, that the \xe2\x80\x98crahmp\xe2\x80\x99 might attack it again.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98SUMMAT WRONG WI\xe2\x80\x99 MY SPECTACLES!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\nBruno ran back, with his booty, to the orchard wall, and pitched the\r\napples over it one by one. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99s welly afraid _some_ of them\xe2\x80\x99s gone under\r\nthe wrong trees!\xe2\x80\x9d he panted, on overtaking us again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _wrong_ trees!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cTrees _ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_ do wrong! There\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno such things as _wrong_ trees!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen there\xe2\x80\x99s no such things as _right_ trees, neither!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno.\r\nAnd Sylvie gave up the point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute, please!\xe2\x80\x9d she said to me. \xe2\x80\x9cI must make Nero _visible_,\r\nyou know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, _please_ don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno, who had by this time mounted on the\r\nRoyal back, and was twisting the Royal hair into a bridle. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\n_such_ fun to have him like this!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it _does_ look funny,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie admitted, and led the way to the\r\nfarm-house, where the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s wife stood, evidently much perplexed at\r\nthe weird procession now approaching her. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s summat gone wrong wi\xe2\x80\x99 my\r\nspectacles, I doubt!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, as she took them off, and began\r\ndiligently rubbing them with a corner of her apron.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile Sylvie had hastily pulled Bruno down from his steed, and had\r\njust time to make His Majesty wholly visible before the spectacles were\r\nresumed.\r\n\r\nAll was natural, now; but the good woman still looked a little uneasy\r\nabout it. \xe2\x80\x9cMy eyesight\xe2\x80\x99s getting bad,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I see you _now_,\r\nmy darlings! You\xe2\x80\x99ll give me a kiss, wo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno got behind me, in a moment: however Sylvie put up _her_ face, to\r\nbe kissed, as representative of _both_, and we all went in together.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CHAPTER V.\r\n                             MATILDA JANE.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome to me, my little gentleman,\xe2\x80\x9d said our hostess, lifting Bruno into\r\nher lap, \xe2\x80\x9cand tell me everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cThere wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be time. Besides, I don\xe2\x80\x99t _know_\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe good woman looked a little puzzled, and turned to Sylvie for help.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes he like _riding_?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I _think_ so,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie gently replied. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s just had a ride on\r\n_Nero_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Nero\xe2\x80\x99s a grand dog, isn\xe2\x80\x99t he? Were you ever outside a _horse_, my\r\nlittle man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Always!_\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said with great decision. \xe2\x80\x9cNever was _inside_ one. Was\r\n_oo_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere I thought it well to interpose, and to mention the business on\r\nwhich we had come, and so relieved her, for a few minutes, from Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nperplexing questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd those dear children will like a bit of cake, _I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ warrant!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe farmer\xe2\x80\x99s hospitable wife, when the business was concluded, as she\r\nopened her cupboard, and brought out a cake. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd don\xe2\x80\x99t you waste the\r\ncrust, little gentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d she added, as she handed a good slice of it to\r\nBruno. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know what the poetry-book says about wilful waste?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat doos he say about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him, Bessie!\xe2\x80\x9d And the mother looked down, proudly and lovingly, on\r\na rosy little maiden, who had just crept shyly into the room, and was\r\nleaning against her knee. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that your poetry-book says about\r\nwilful waste?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_For wilful waste makes woeful want_,\xe2\x80\x9d Bessie recited, in an almost\r\ninaudible whisper: \xe2\x80\x9c_and you may live to say \xe2\x80\x98How much I wish I had the\r\ncrust that then I threw away!\xe2\x80\x99_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow try if _you_ can say it, my dear! _For wilful_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_For wifful_\xe2\x80\x94sumfinoruvver\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno began, readily enough; and then\r\nthere came a dead pause. \xe2\x80\x9cCa\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t remember no more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you _learn_ from it, then? You can tell us _that_, at any\r\nrate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno ate a little more cake, and considered: but the moral did not seem\r\nto him to be a very obvious one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie prompted him in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno softly repeated: and then, with sudden inspiration,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9calways to look where it goes to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere _what_ goes to, darling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy the _crust_, a course!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cThen, if I lived to say \xe2\x80\x98_How\r\nmuch I wiss I had the crust_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99 (and all that), I\xe2\x80\x99d know where I frew it\r\nto!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis new interpretation quite puzzled the good woman. She returned to\r\nthe subject of \xe2\x80\x98Bessie.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you like to see Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s doll, my\r\ndears! Bessie, take the little lady and gentleman to see Matilda Jane!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie\xe2\x80\x99s shyness thawed away in a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cMatilda Jane has just woke\r\nup,\xe2\x80\x9d she stated, confidentially, to Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cWo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t you help me on with\r\nher frock? Them strings _is_ such a bother to tie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can tie _strings_,\xe2\x80\x9d we heard, in Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s gentle voice, as the two\r\nlittle girls left the room together. Bruno ignored the whole proceeding,\r\nand strolled to the window, quite with the air of a fashionable\r\ngentleman. Little girls, and dolls, were not at all in his line.\r\n\r\nAnd forthwith the fond mother proceeded to tell me (as what mother is\r\nnot ready to do?) of all Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s virtues (and vices too, for the matter\r\nof that) and of the many fearful maladies which, notwithstanding those\r\nruddy cheeks and that plump little figure, had nearly, time and again,\r\nswept her from the face of the earth.\r\n\r\nWhen the full stream of loving memories had nearly run itself out, I\r\nbegan to question her about the working men of that neighbourhood, and\r\nspecially the \xe2\x80\x98Willie,\xe2\x80\x99 whom we had heard of at his cottage. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was a\r\ngood fellow once,\xe2\x80\x9d said my kind hostess: \xe2\x80\x9cbut it\xe2\x80\x99s the drink has ruined\r\nhim! Not that I\xe2\x80\x99d rob them of the drink\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s good for the most of\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94but there\xe2\x80\x99s some as is too weak to stand agin\xe2\x80\x99 temptations: it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nthousand pities, for _them_, as they ever built the Golden Lion at the\r\ncorner there!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Golden Lion?\xe2\x80\x9d I repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the new Public,\xe2\x80\x9d my hostess explained. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it stands right in the\r\nway, and handy for the workmen, as they come back from the brickfields,\r\nas it might be to-day, with their week\xe2\x80\x99s wages. A deal of money gets\r\nwasted that way. And some of \xe2\x80\x99em gets drunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf only they could have it in their own houses\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I mused, hardly\r\nknowing I had said the words out loud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it!\xe2\x80\x9d she eagerly exclaimed. It was evidently a solution, of the\r\nproblem, that she had already thought out. \xe2\x80\x9cIf only you could manage,\r\nso\xe2\x80\x99s each man to have his own little barrel in his own house\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nhardly be a drunken man in the length and breadth of the land!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then I told her the old story\xe2\x80\x94about a certain cottager who bought\r\nhimself a little barrel of beer, and installed his wife as bar-keeper:\r\nand how, every time he wanted his mug of beer, he regularly paid her\r\nover the counter for it: and how she never would let him go on \xe2\x80\x98tick,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand was a perfectly inflexible bar-keeper in never letting him have more\r\nthan his proper allowance: and how, every time the barrel needed\r\nrefilling, she had plenty to do it with, and something over for her\r\nmoney-box: and how, at the end of the year, he not only found himself in\r\nfirst-rate health and spirits, with that undefinable but quite\r\nunmistakeable air which always distinguishes the sober man from the one\r\nwho takes \xe2\x80\x98a drop too much,\xe2\x80\x99 but had quite a box full of money, all\r\nsaved out of his own pence!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf only they\xe2\x80\x99d all do like that!\xe2\x80\x9d said the good woman, wiping her eyes,\r\nwhich were overflowing with kindly sympathy. \xe2\x80\x9cDrink hadn\xe2\x80\x99t need to be\r\nthe curse it is to some\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a _curse_,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen it is used wrongly. Any of God\xe2\x80\x99s gifts\r\nmay be turned into a curse, unless we use it wisely. But we must be\r\ngetting home. Would you call the little girls? Matilda Jane has seen\r\nenough of company, for _one_ day, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll find \xe2\x80\x99em in a minute,\xe2\x80\x9d said my hostess, as she rose to leave the\r\nroom. \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe that young gentleman saw which way they went?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are they, Bruno?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey ain\xe2\x80\x99t in the field,\xe2\x80\x9d was Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s rather evasive reply, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99cause\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing but _pigs_ there, and Sylvie isn\xe2\x80\x99t a pig. Now don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nimperrupt me any more, \xe2\x80\x99cause I\xe2\x80\x99m telling a story to this fly; and it\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t attend!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re among the apples, I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant \xe2\x80\x99em!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Farmer\xe2\x80\x99s wife. So\r\nwe left Bruno to finish his story, and went out into the orchard, where\r\nwe soon came upon the children, walking sedately side by side, Sylvie\r\ncarrying the doll, while little Bess carefully shaded its face, with a\r\nlarge cabbage-leaf for a parasol.\r\n\r\nAs soon as they caught sight of us, little Bess dropped her cabbage-leaf\r\nand came running to meet us, Sylvie following more slowly, as her\r\nprecious charge evidently needed great care and attention.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m its Mamma, and Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s the Head-Nurse,\xe2\x80\x9d Bessie explained: \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nSylvie\xe2\x80\x99s taught me ever such a pretty song, for me to sing to Matilda\r\nJane!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s hear it once more, Sylvie,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, delighted at getting the\r\nchance I had long wished for, of hearing her sing. But Sylvie turned shy\r\nand frightened in a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, _please_ not!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in an earnest\r\n\xe2\x80\x98aside\xe2\x80\x99 to me. \xe2\x80\x9cBessie knows it quite perfect now. Bessie can sing it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye! Let Bessie sing it!\xe2\x80\x9d said the proud mother. \xe2\x80\x9cBessie has a\r\nbonny voice of her own,\xe2\x80\x9d (this again was an \xe2\x80\x98aside\xe2\x80\x99 to me) \xe2\x80\x9cthough I say\r\nit as shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBessie was only too happy to accept the \xe2\x80\x98encore.\xe2\x80\x99 So the plump little\r\nMamma sat down at our feet, with her hideous daughter reclining stiffly\r\nacross her lap (it was one of a kind that wo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t sit down, under _any_\r\namount of persuasion), and, with a face simply beaming with delight,\r\nbegan the lullaby, in a shout that _ought_ to have frightened the poor\r\nbaby into fits. The Head-Nurse crouched down behind her, keeping herself\r\nrespectfully in the back-ground, with her hands on the shoulders of her\r\nlittle mistress, so as to be ready to act as Prompter, if required, and\r\nto supply \xe2\x80\x98_each gap in faithless memory void_.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n[Illustration: BESSIE\xe2\x80\x99S SONG]\r\n\r\nThe shout, with which she began, proved to be only a momentary effort.\r\nAfter a very few notes, Bessie toned down, and sang on in a small but\r\nvery sweet voice. At first her great black eyes were fixed on her\r\nmother, but soon her gaze wandered upwards, among the apples, and she\r\nseemed to have quite forgotten that she had any other audience than her\r\nBaby, and her Head-Nurse, who once or twice supplied, almost inaudibly,\r\nthe right note, when the singer was getting a little \xe2\x80\x98flat.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cMatilda Jane, you never look\r\n  At any toy or picture-book:\r\n  I show you pretty things in vain\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  You must be blind, Matilda Jane!\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cI ask you riddles, tell you tales,\r\n  But _all_ our conversation fails:\r\n  You _never_ answer me again\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  I fear you\xe2\x80\x99re dumb, Matilda Jane!\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cMatilda, darling, when I call,\r\n  You never seem to hear at all:\r\n  I shout with all my might and main\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  But you\xe2\x80\x99re _so_ deaf, Matilda Jane!\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cMatilda Jane, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t mind;\r\n  For, though you\xe2\x80\x99re deaf, and dumb, and blind,\r\n  There\xe2\x80\x99s _some one_ loves you, it is plain\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  And that is _me,_ Matilda Jane!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe sang three of the verses in a rather perfunctory style, but the last\r\nstanza evidently excited the little maiden. Her voice rose, ever clearer\r\nand louder: she had a rapt look on her face, as if suddenly inspired,\r\nand, as she sang the last few words, she clasped to her heart the\r\ninattentive Matilda Jane.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKiss it now!\xe2\x80\x9d prompted the Head-Nurse. And in a moment the simpering\r\nmeaningless face of the Baby was covered with a shower of passionate\r\nkisses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a bonny song!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Farmer\xe2\x80\x99s wife. \xe2\x80\x9cWho made the words,\r\ndearie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll look for Bruno,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said demurely, and left us\r\nhastily. The curious child seemed always afraid of being praised, or\r\neven noticed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSylvie planned the words,\xe2\x80\x9d Bessie informed us, proud of her superior\r\ninformation: \xe2\x80\x9cand Bruno planned the music\xe2\x80\x94and _I_ sang it!\xe2\x80\x9d (this last\r\ncircumstance, by the way, we did not need to be told).\r\n\r\nSo we followed Sylvie, and all entered the parlour together. Bruno was\r\nstill standing at the window, with his elbows on the sill. He had,\r\napparently, finished the story that he was telling to the fly, and had\r\nfound a new occupation. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t imperrupt!\xe2\x80\x9d he said as we came in. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ncounting the Pigs in the field!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many are there?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout a thousand and four,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean \xe2\x80\x98about a thousand,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie corrected him. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no good\r\nsaying \xe2\x80\x98_and four_\xe2\x80\x99: you _ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_ be sure about the four!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you\xe2\x80\x99re as wrong as ever!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno exclaimed triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just\r\nthe _four_ I _can_ be sure about; \xe2\x80\x99cause they\xe2\x80\x99re here, grubbling under\r\nthe window! It\xe2\x80\x99s the _thousand_ I isn\xe2\x80\x99t pruffickly sure about!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut some of them have gone into the sty,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, leaning over him\r\nto look out of the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno; \xe2\x80\x9cbut they went so slowly and so fewly, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nto count _them_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must be going, children,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWish Bessie good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie\r\nflung her arms round the little maiden\xe2\x80\x99s neck, and kissed her: but Bruno\r\nstood aloof, looking unusually shy. (\xe2\x80\x9cI never kiss _nobody_ but Sylvie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe explained to me afterwards.) The farmer\xe2\x80\x99s wife showed us out: and we\r\nwere soon on our way back to Elveston.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that\xe2\x80\x99s the new public-house that we were talking about, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nI said, as we came in sight of a long low building, with the words \xe2\x80\x98The\r\nGolden Lion\xe2\x80\x99 over the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if _her_ Willie\xe2\x80\x99s inside? Run\r\nin, Bruno, and see if he\xe2\x80\x99s there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI interposed, feeling that Bruno was, in a sort of way, in _my_ care.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s not a place to send a child into.\xe2\x80\x9d For already the revelers were\r\ngetting noisy: and a wild discord of singing, shouting, and meaningless\r\nlaughter came to us through the open windows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey wo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t _see_ him, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie explained. \xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute,\r\nBruno!\xe2\x80\x9d She clasped the jewel, that always hung round her neck, between\r\nthe palms of her hands, and muttered a few words to herself. What they\r\nwere I could not at all make out, but some mysterious change seemed\r\ninstantly to pass over us. My feet seemed to me no longer to press the\r\nground, and the dream-like feeling came upon me, that I was suddenly\r\nendowed with the power of floating in the air. I could still just _see_\r\nthe children: but their forms were shadowy and unsubstantial, and their\r\nvoices sounded as if they came from some distant place and time, they\r\nwere so unreal. However, I offered no further opposition to Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngoing into the house. He was back again in a few moments. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, he isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncome yet,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re talking about him inside, and saying how\r\ndrunk he was last week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile he was speaking, one of the men lounged out through the door, a\r\npipe in one hand and a mug of beer in the other, and crossed to where we\r\nwere standing, so as to get a better view along the road. Two or three\r\nothers leaned out through the open window, each holding his mug of beer,\r\nwith red faces and sleepy eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cCanst see him, lad?\xe2\x80\x9d one of them asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dunnot know,\xe2\x80\x9d the man said, taking a step forwards, which brought us\r\nnearly face to face. Sylvie hastily pulled me out of his way. \xe2\x80\x9cThanks,\r\nchild,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see us. What would have\r\nhappened if I had staid in his way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t matter to _us_; but\r\n_you_ may be different.\xe2\x80\x9d She said this in her usual voice, but the man\r\ntook no sort of notice, though she was standing close in front of him,\r\nand looking up into his face as she spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s coming now!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno, pointing down the road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe be a-coomin noo!\xe2\x80\x9d echoed the man, stretching out his arm exactly\r\nover Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s head, and pointing with his pipe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen _chorus_ agin!\xe2\x80\x9d was shouted out by one of the red-faced men in the\r\nwindow: and forthwith a dozen voices yelled, to a harsh discordant\r\nmelody, the refrain:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s him, an\xe2\x80\x99 yo\xe2\x80\x99 an\xe2\x80\x99 me,\r\n            Roarin\xe2\x80\x99 laddies!\r\n  We loves a bit o spree,\r\n  Roarin\xe2\x80\x99 laddies we,\r\n            Roarin\xe2\x80\x99 laddies\r\n            Roarin\xe2\x80\x99 laddies!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man lounged back again to the house, joining lustily in the chorus\r\nas he went: so that only the children and I were in the road when\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Willie\xe2\x80\x99 came up.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER VI.\r\n                             WILLIE\xe2\x80\x99S WIFE.\r\n\r\n\r\nHe made for the door of the public-house, but the children intercepted\r\nhim. Sylvie clung to one arm; while Bruno, on the opposite side, was\r\npushing him with all his strength, with many inarticulate cries of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGee-up! Gee-back! Woah then!\xe2\x80\x9d which he had picked up from the\r\nwaggoners.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Willie\xe2\x80\x99 took not the least notice of them: he was simply conscious that\r\n_something_ had checked him: and, for want of any other way of\r\naccounting for it, he seemed to regard it as his own act.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: THE RESCUE OF WILLIE]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wunnut coom in,\xe2\x80\x9d he said: \xe2\x80\x9cnot to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA mug o\xe2\x80\x99 beer wunnut hurt \xe2\x80\x99ee!\xe2\x80\x9d his friends shouted in chorus. \xe2\x80\x9c_Two_\r\nmugs wunnut hurt \xe2\x80\x99ee! Nor a dozen mugs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Willie. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m agoan whoam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, withouten thy drink, Willie man?\xe2\x80\x9d shouted the others. But \xe2\x80\x98Willie\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99 would have no more discussion, and turned doggedly away, the\r\nchildren keeping one on each side of him, to guard him against any\r\nchange in his sudden resolution.\r\n\r\nFor a while he walked on stoutly enough, keeping his hands in his\r\npockets, and softly whistling a tune, in time to his heavy tread: his\r\nsuccess, in appearing entirely at his ease, was _almost_ complete; but a\r\ncareful observer would have noted that he had forgotten the second part\r\nof the air, and that, when it broke down, he instantly began it again,\r\nbeing too nervous to think of another, and too restless to endure\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\nIt was not the old fear that possessed him now\xe2\x80\x94the old fear, that had\r\nbeen his dreary companion every Saturday night he could remember, as he\r\nhad reeled along, steadying himself against gates and garden-palings,\r\nand when the shrill reproaches of his wife had seemed to his dazed brain\r\nonly the echo of a yet more piercing voice within, the intolerable wail\r\nof a hopeless remorse: it was a wholly new fear that had come to him\r\nnow: life had taken on itself a new set of colours, and was lighted up\r\nwith a new and dazzling radiance, and he did not see, as yet, how his\r\nhome-life, and his wife and child, would fit into the new order of\r\nthings: the very novelty of it all was, to his simple mind, a perplexity\r\nand an overwhelming terror.\r\n\r\nAnd now the tune died into sudden silence on the trembling lips, as he\r\nturned a sharp corner, and came in sight of his own cottage, where his\r\nwife stood, leaning with folded arms on the wicket-gate, and looking up\r\nthe road with a pale face, that had in it no glimmer of the light of\r\nhope\xe2\x80\x94only the heavy shadow of a deep stony despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine an\xe2\x80\x99 early, lad! Fine an\xe2\x80\x99 early!\xe2\x80\x9d The words might have been words\r\nof welcoming, but oh, the bitterness of the tone in which she said it!\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat brings thee from thy merry mates, and all the fiddling and the\r\njigging? Pockets empty, I doubt? Or thou\xe2\x80\x99st come, mebbe, for to see thy\r\nlittle one die? The bairnie\xe2\x80\x99s clemmed, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve nor bite nor sup to gie\r\nher. But what does _thou_ care?\xe2\x80\x9d She flung the gate open, and met him\r\nwith blazing eyes of fury.\r\n\r\nThe man said no word. Slowly, and with downcast eyes, he passed into the\r\nhouse, while she, half terrified at his strange silence, followed him in\r\nwithout another word; and it was not till he had sunk into a chair, with\r\nhis arms crossed on the table and with drooping head, that she found her\r\nvoice again.\r\n\r\nIt seemed entirely natural for us to go in with them: at another time\r\none would have asked leave for this, but I felt, I knew not why, that we\r\nwere in some mysterious way invisible, and as free to come and to go as\r\ndisembodied spirits.\r\n\r\nThe child in the cradle woke up, and raised a piteous cry, which in a\r\nmoment brought the children to its side: Bruno rocked the cradle, while\r\nSylvie tenderly replaced the little head on the pillow from which it had\r\nslipped. But the mother took no heed of the cry, nor yet of the\r\nsatisfied \xe2\x80\x98coo\xe2\x80\x99 that it set up when Sylvie had made it happy again: she\r\nonly stood gazing at her husband, and vainly trying, with white\r\nquivering lips (I believe she thought he was mad), to speak in the old\r\ntones of shrill upbraiding that he knew so well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd thou\xe2\x80\x99st spent all thy wages\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll swear thou hast\xe2\x80\x94on the devil\xe2\x80\x99s own\r\ndrink\xe2\x80\x94and thou\xe2\x80\x99st been and made thysen a beast again\xe2\x80\x94as thou allus\r\ndost\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHasna!\xe2\x80\x9d the man muttered, his voice hardly rising above a whisper, as\r\nhe slowly emptied his pockets on the table. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s th\xe2\x80\x99 wage, Missus,\r\nevery penny on\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman gasped, and put one hand to her heart, as if under some great\r\nshock of surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cThen _how_\xe2\x80\x99s thee gotten th\xe2\x80\x99 drink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hasna_ gotten it,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered her, in a tone more sad than sullen. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhanna touched a drop this blessed day. No!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried aloud, bringing his\r\nclenched fist heavily down upon the table, and looking up at her with\r\ngleaming eyes, \xe2\x80\x9cnor I\xe2\x80\x99ll never touch another drop o\xe2\x80\x99 the cursed\r\ndrink\xe2\x80\x94till I die\xe2\x80\x94so help me God my Maker!\xe2\x80\x9d His voice, which had suddenly\r\nrisen to a hoarse shout, dropped again as suddenly: and once more he\r\nbowed his head, and buried his face in his folded arms.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: WILLIE\xe2\x80\x99S WIFE]\r\n\r\nThe woman had dropped upon her knees by the cradle, while he was\r\nspeaking. She neither looked at him nor seemed to hear him. With hands\r\nclasped above her head, she rocked herself wildly to and fro. \xe2\x80\x9cOh my\r\nGod! Oh my God!\xe2\x80\x9d was all she said, over and over again.\r\n\r\nSylvie and Bruno gently unclasped her hands and drew them down\xe2\x80\x94till she\r\nhad an arm round each of them, though she took no notice of them, but\r\nknelt on with eyes gazing upwards, and lips that moved as if in silent\r\nthanksgiving. The man kept his face hidden, and uttered no sound: but\r\none could _see_ the sobs that shook him from head to foot.\r\n\r\nAfter a while he raised his head\xe2\x80\x94his face all wet with tears. \xe2\x80\x9cPolly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said softly; and then, louder, \xe2\x80\x9cOld Poll!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she rose from her knees and came to him, with a dazed look, as if\r\nshe were walking in her sleep. \xe2\x80\x9cWho was it called me old Poll?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked: her voice took on it a tender playfulness: her eyes sparkled; and\r\nthe rosy light of Youth flushed her pale cheeks, till she looked more\r\nlike a happy girl of seventeen than a worn woman of forty. \xe2\x80\x9cWas that my\r\nown lad, my Willie, a-waiting for me at the stile?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis face too was transformed, in the same magic light, to the likeness\r\nof a bashful boy: and boy and girl they seemed, as he wound an arm about\r\nher, and drew her to his side, while with the other hand he thrust from\r\nhim the heap of money, as though it were something hateful to the touch.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTak it, lass,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9ctak it all! An\xe2\x80\x99 fetch us summat to eat: but\r\nget a sup o\xe2\x80\x99 milk, first, for t\xe2\x80\x99 bairn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy _little_ bairn!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured as she gathered up the coins. \xe2\x80\x9cMy own\r\nlittle lassie!\xe2\x80\x9d Then she moved to the door, and was passing out, but a\r\nsudden thought seemed to arrest her: she hastily returned\xe2\x80\x94first to kneel\r\ndown and kiss the sleeping child, and then to throw herself into her\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s arms and be strained to his heart. The next moment she was on\r\nher way, taking with her a jug that hung on a peg near the door: we\r\nfollowed close behind.\r\n\r\nWe had not gone far before we came in sight of a swinging sign-board\r\nbearing the word \xe2\x80\x98DAIRY\xe2\x80\x99 on it, and here she went in, welcomed by a\r\nlittle curly white dog, who, not being under the \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 influence, saw\r\nthe children, and received them with the most effusive affection. When I\r\ngot inside, the dairyman was in the act of taking the money. \xe2\x80\x9cIs\xe2\x80\x99t for\r\nthysen, Missus, or for t\xe2\x80\x99 bairn?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, when he had filled the jug,\r\npausing with it in his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor t\xe2\x80\x99 _bairn_!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, almost reproachfully. \xe2\x80\x9cThink\xe2\x80\x99st tha I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ntouch a drop _mysen_, while as _she_ hadna got her fill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, Missus,\xe2\x80\x9d the man replied, turning away with the jug in his\r\nhand. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s just mak sure it\xe2\x80\x99s good measure.\xe2\x80\x9d He went back among his\r\nshelves of milk-bowls, carefully keeping his back towards her while he\r\nemptied a little measure of cream into the jug, muttering to himself\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmebbe it\xe2\x80\x99ll hearten her up a bit, the little lassie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman never noticed the kind deed, but took back the jug with a\r\nsimple \xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, Master,\xe2\x80\x9d and went her way: but the children had\r\nbeen more observant, and, as we followed her out, Bruno remarked \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nwere _welly_ kind: and I loves that man: and if I was welly rich I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ngive him a hundred pounds\xe2\x80\x94and a bun. That little grummeling dog doosn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow its business!\xe2\x80\x9d He referred to the dairyman\xe2\x80\x99s little dog, who had\r\napparently quite forgotten the affectionate welcome he had given us on\r\nour arrival, and was now following at a respectful distance, doing his\r\nbest to \xe2\x80\x98_speed the parting guest_\xe2\x80\x99 with a shower of little shrill\r\nbarks, that seemed to tread on one another\xe2\x80\x99s heels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _is_ a dog\xe2\x80\x99s business?\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cDogs ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t keep shops\r\nand give change!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSisters\xe2\x80\x99 businesses _isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ to laugh at their brothers,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno replied\r\nwith perfect gravity. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd dogs\xe2\x80\x99 businesses is to _bark_\xe2\x80\x94not like that:\r\nit should finish one bark before it begins another: and it should\xe2\x80\x94Oh\r\nSylvie, there\xe2\x80\x99s some dindledums!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd in another moment the happy children were flying across the common,\r\nracing for the patch of dandelions.\r\n\r\nWhile I stood watching them, a strange dreamy feeling came upon me: a\r\nrailway-platform seemed to take the place of the green sward, and,\r\ninstead of the light figure of Sylvie bounding along, I seemed to see\r\nthe flying form of Lady Muriel; but whether Bruno had also undergone a\r\ntransformation, and had become the old man whom she was running to\r\novertake, I was unable to judge, so instantaneously did the feeling come\r\nand go.\r\n\r\nWhen I re-entered the little sitting-room which I shared with Arthur, he\r\nwas standing with his back to me, looking out of the open window, and\r\nevidently had not heard me enter. A cup of tea, apparently just tasted\r\nand pushed aside, stood on the table, on the opposite side of which was\r\na letter, just begun, with the pen lying across it: an open book lay on\r\nthe sofa: the London paper occupied the easy chair; and on the little\r\ntable, which stood by it, I noticed an unlighted cigar and an open box\r\nof cigar-lights: all things betokened that the Doctor, usually so\r\nmethodical and so self-contained, had been trying every form of\r\noccupation, and could settle to none!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is very unlike _you_, Doctor!\xe2\x80\x9d I was beginning, but checked\r\nmyself, as he turned at the sound of my voice, in sheer amazement at the\r\nwonderful change that had taken place in his appearance. Never had I\r\nseen a face so radiant with happiness, or eyes that sparkled with such\r\nunearthly light! \xe2\x80\x9cEven thus,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought, \xe2\x80\x9cmust the herald-angel have\r\nlooked, who brought to the shepherds, watching over their flocks by\r\nnight, that sweet message of \xe2\x80\x98_peace on earth, good-will to men_\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, dear friend!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as if in answer to the question that I\r\nsuppose he read in my face. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is true! It is true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo need to ask _what_ was true. \xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you both!\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as I felt\r\nthe happy tears brimming to my eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cYou were made for each other!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, simply, \xe2\x80\x9cI believe we were. And _what_ a change it makes\r\nin one\xe2\x80\x99s Life! This isn\xe2\x80\x99t the same world! That isn\xe2\x80\x99t the sky I saw\r\nyesterday! Those clouds\xe2\x80\x94I never saw such clouds in all my life before!\r\nThey look like troops of hovering angels!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo _me_ they looked very ordinary clouds indeed: but then _I_ had not\r\nfed \xe2\x80\x98_on honey-dew, And drunk the milk of Paradise_\xe2\x80\x99!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe wants to see you\xe2\x80\x94at once,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, descending suddenly to the\r\nthings of earth. \xe2\x80\x9cShe says _that_ is the _one_ drop yet wanting in her\r\ncup of happiness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go at once,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as I turned to leave the room. \xe2\x80\x9cWo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\ncome with me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Doctor, with a sudden effort\xe2\x80\x94which proved an utter\r\nfailure\xe2\x80\x94to resume his professional manner. \xe2\x80\x9cDo I _look_ like coming with\r\nyou? Have you never heard that two is company, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI _have_ heard it: and I\xe2\x80\x99m painfully aware that_ I_ am\r\n_Number Three_! But, _when_ shall we three meet again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_When the hurly-burly\xe2\x80\x99s done!_\xe2\x80\x9d he answered with a happy laugh, such as\r\nI had not heard from him for many a year.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER VII.\r\n                               MEIN HERR.\r\n\r\n\r\nSo I went on my lonely way, and, on reaching the Hall, I found Lady\r\nMuriel standing at the garden-gate waiting for me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo need to _give_ you joy, or to _wish_ you joy?\xe2\x80\x9d I began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone _whatever_!\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, with the joyous laugh of a child. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\n_give_ people what they haven\xe2\x80\x99t got: we _wish_ for something that is yet\r\nto come. For _me_, it\xe2\x80\x99s all _here_! It\xe2\x80\x99s all _mine_! Dear friend,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsuddenly broke off, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think Heaven ever begins on _Earth_, for any\r\nof us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor _some_,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cFor some, perhaps, who are simple and childlike.\r\nYou know He said \xe2\x80\x98of such is the Kingdom of Heaven.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel clasped her hands, and gazed up into the cloudless sky, with\r\na look I had often seen in Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel as if it had begun for\r\n_me_,\xe2\x80\x9d she almost whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel as if _I_ were one of the happy\r\nchildren, whom He bid them bring near to Him, though the people would\r\nhave kept them back. Yes, He has seen me in the throng. He has read the\r\nwistful longing in my eyes. He has beckoned me to Him. They have _had_\r\nto make way for me. He has taken me up in His arms. He has put His hands\r\nupon me and blessed me!\xe2\x80\x9d She paused, breathless in her perfect\r\nhappiness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI think He has!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must come and speak to my father,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, as we stood side by\r\nside at the gate, looking down the shady lane. But, even as she said the\r\nwords, the \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 sensation came over me like a flood: I saw the dear\r\nold Professor approaching us, and also saw, what was stranger still,\r\nthat he was visible to _Lady Muriel_!\r\n\r\nWhat was to be done? Had the fairy-life been merged in the real life? Or\r\nwas Lady Muriel \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 also, and thus able to enter into the\r\nfairy-world along with me? The words were on my lips (\xe2\x80\x9cI see an old\r\nfriend of mine in the lane: if you don\xe2\x80\x99t know him, may I introduce him\r\nto you?\xe2\x80\x9d) when the strangest thing of all happened: Lady Muriel spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see an old friend of mine in the lane,\xe2\x80\x9d she said: \xe2\x80\x9cif you don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nhim, may I introduce him to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI seemed to wake out of a dream: for the \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 feeling was still\r\nstrong upon me, and the figure outside seemed to be changing at every\r\nmoment, like one of the shapes in a kaleidoscope: now he was the\r\n_Professor_, and now he was somebody else! By the time he had reached\r\nthe gate, he certainly was somebody else: and I felt that the proper\r\ncourse was for _Lady Muriel_, not for _me_, to introduce him. She\r\ngreeted him kindly, and, opening the gate, admitted the venerable old\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94a German, obviously\xe2\x80\x94who looked about him with dazed eyes, as if\r\n_he_, too, had but just awaked from a dream!\r\n\r\nNo, it was certainly _not_ the Professor! My old friend _could_ not have\r\ngrown that magnificent beard since last we met: moreover, he would have\r\nrecognised _me_, for I was certain that _I_ had not changed much in the\r\ntime.\r\n\r\nAs it was, he simply looked at me vaguely, and took off his hat in\r\nresponse to Lady Muriel\xe2\x80\x99s words \xe2\x80\x9cLet me introduce Mein Herr to you\xe2\x80\x9d;\r\nwhile in the words, spoken in a strong German accent, \xe2\x80\x9cproud to make\r\nyour acquaintance, Sir!\xe2\x80\x9d I could detect no trace of an idea that we had\r\never met before.\r\n\r\nLady Muriel led us to the well-known shady nook, where preparations for\r\nafternoon tea had already been made, and, while she went in to look for\r\nthe Earl, we seated ourselves in two easy-chairs, and \xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr\xe2\x80\x99 took\r\nup Lady Muriel\xe2\x80\x99s work, and examined it through his large spectacles (one\r\nof the adjuncts that made him so provokingly like the Professor).\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHemming pocket-handkerchiefs?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, musingly. \xe2\x80\x9cSo _that_ is what\r\nthe English miladies occupy themselves with, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the one accomplishment,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cin which Man has never yet\r\nrivaled Woman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere Lady Muriel returned with her father; and, after he had exchanged\r\nsome friendly words with \xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr,\xe2\x80\x99 and we had all been supplied with\r\nthe needful \xe2\x80\x98creature-comforts,\xe2\x80\x99 the newcomer returned to the suggestive\r\nsubject of Pocket-handkerchiefs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have heard of Fortunatus\xe2\x80\x99s Purse, Miladi? Ah, so! Would you be\r\nsurprised to hear that, with three of these leetle handkerchiefs, you\r\nshall make the Purse of Fortunatus, quite soon, quite easily?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I indeed?\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel eagerly replied, as she took a heap of\r\nthem into her lap, and threaded her needle. \xe2\x80\x9c_Please_ tell me how, Mein\r\nHerr! I\xe2\x80\x99ll make one before I touch another drop of tea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shall first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr, possessing himself of two of the\r\nhandkerchiefs, spreading one upon the other, and holding them up by two\r\ncorners, \xe2\x80\x9cyou shall first join together these upper corners, the right\r\nto the right, the left to the left; and the opening between them shall\r\nbe the _mouth_ of the Purse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA very few stitches sufficed to carry out _this_ direction. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, if I\r\nsew the other three edges together,\xe2\x80\x9d she suggested, \xe2\x80\x9cthe bag is\r\ncomplete?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so, Miladi: the _lower_ edges shall _first_ be joined\xe2\x80\x94ah, not so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(as she was beginning to sew them together). \xe2\x80\x9cTurn one of them over, and\r\njoin the _right_ lower corner of the one to the _left_ lower corner of\r\nthe other, and sew the lower edges together in what you would call _the\r\nwrong way_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ see!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, as she deftly executed the order. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd a\r\nvery twisted, uncomfortable, uncanny-looking bag it makes! But the\r\n_moral_ is a lovely one. Unlimited wealth can only be attained by doing\r\nthings _in the wrong way_! And how are we to join up these\r\nmysterious\xe2\x80\x94no, I mean _this_ mysterious opening?\xe2\x80\x9d (twisting the thing\r\nround and round with a puzzled air.) \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it _is_ one opening. I\r\nthought it was _two_, at first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have seen the puzzle of the Paper Ring?\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr said, addressing\r\nthe Earl. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere you take a slip of paper, and join its ends together,\r\nfirst twisting one, so as to join the _upper_ corner of _one_ end to the\r\n_lower_ corner of the _other_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw one made, only yesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl replied. \xe2\x80\x9cMuriel, my child,\r\nwere you not making one, to amuse those children you had to tea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know that Puzzle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Ring has only _one_\r\nsurface, and only _one_ edge. It\xe2\x80\x99s very mysterious!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _bag_ is just like that, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested. \xe2\x80\x9cIs not the\r\n_outer_ surface of one side of it continuous with the _inner_ surface of\r\nthe other side?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly it _isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ a bag, just yet. How shall\r\nwe fill up this opening, Mein Herr?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThus!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man impressively, taking the bag from her, and\r\nrising to his feet in the excitement of the explanation. \xe2\x80\x9cThe edge of\r\nthe opening consists of _four_ handkerchief-edges, and you can trace it\r\ncontinuously, round and round the opening: down the right edge of _one_\r\nhandkerchief, up the left edge of the _other_, and then down the left\r\nedge of the _one_, and up the right edge of the _other_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you can!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel murmured thoughtfully, leaning her head on her\r\nhand, and earnestly watching the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd that _proves_ it to be\r\nonly _one_ opening!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: FORTUNATUS\xe2\x80\x99 PURSE]\r\n\r\nShe looked so strangely like a child, puzzling over a difficult lesson,\r\nand Mein Herr had become, for the moment, so strangely like the old\r\nProfessor, that I felt utterly bewildered: the \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 feeling was on me\r\nin its full force, and I felt almost _impelled_ to say \xe2\x80\x9cDo you\r\nunderstand it, Sylvie?\xe2\x80\x9d However I checked myself by a great effort, and\r\nlet the dream (if indeed it _was_ a dream) go on to its end.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, this _third_ handkerchief,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr proceeded, \xe2\x80\x9chas _also_ four\r\nedges, which you can trace continuously round and round: all you need do\r\nis to join its four edges to the four edges of the opening. The Purse is\r\nthen complete, and its outer surface\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ see!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel eagerly interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cIts _outer_ surface will be\r\ncontinuous with its _inner_ surface! But it will take time. I\xe2\x80\x99ll sew it\r\nup after tea.\xe2\x80\x9d She laid aside the bag and resumed her cup of tea. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nwhy do you call it Fortunatus\xe2\x80\x99s Purse, Mein Herr?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe dear old man beamed upon her, with a jolly smile, looking more\r\nexactly like the Professor than ever. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you see, my child\xe2\x80\x94I should\r\nsay Miladi? Whatever is _inside_ that Purse, is _outside_ it; and\r\nwhatever is _outside_ it, is _inside_ it. So you have all the wealth of\r\nthe world in that leetle Purse!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis pupil clapped her hands, in unrestrained delight. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll certainly\r\nsew the third handkerchief in\xe2\x80\x94_some_ time,\xe2\x80\x9d she said: \xe2\x80\x9cbut I wo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t take\r\nup your time by trying it now. Tell us some more wonderful things,\r\nplease!\xe2\x80\x9d And her face and her voice so _exactly_ recalled Sylvie, that I\r\ncould not help glancing round, half-expecting to see _Bruno_ also!\r\n\r\nMein Herr began thoughtfully balancing his spoon on the edge of his\r\nteacup, while he pondered over this request. \xe2\x80\x9cSomething wonderful\xe2\x80\x94like\r\n Fortunatus\xe2\x80\x99s Purse? _That_ will give you\xe2\x80\x94when it is made\xe2\x80\x94wealth beyond\r\nyour wildest dreams: but it will not give you _Time_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA pause of silence ensued\xe2\x80\x94utilised by Lady Muriel for the very practical\r\npurpose of refilling the teacups.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn _your_ country,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr began with a startling abruptness, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\r\nbecomes of all the wasted Time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel looked grave. \xe2\x80\x9cWho can tell?\xe2\x80\x9d she half-whispered to herself.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll one knows is that it is gone\xe2\x80\x94past recall!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, in _my_\xe2\x80\x94I mean in a country _I_ have visited,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthey store it up: and it comes in _very_ useful, years afterwards! For\r\nexample, suppose you have a long tedious evening before you: nobody to\r\ntalk to: nothing you care to do: and yet hours too soon to go to bed.\r\nHow do _you_ behave then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI get _very_ cross,\xe2\x80\x9d she frankly admitted: \xe2\x80\x9cand I want to throw things\r\nabout the room!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen that happens to\xe2\x80\x94to the people I have visited, they never act _so_.\r\nBy a short and simple process\xe2\x80\x94which I cannot explain to you\xe2\x80\x94they store\r\nup the useless hours: and, on some _other_ occasion, when they happen to\r\n_need_ extra time, they get them out again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Earl was listening with a slightly incredulous smile. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy cannot\r\nyou _explain_ the process?\xe2\x80\x9d he enquired.\r\n\r\nMein Herr was ready with a quite unanswerable reason. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause you have\r\nno _words_, in _your_ language, to convey the ideas which are needed. I\r\ncould explain it in\xe2\x80\x94in\xe2\x80\x94but you would not understand it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, graciously dispensing with the _name_ of\r\nthe unknown language. \xe2\x80\x9cI never learnt it\xe2\x80\x94at least, not to speak it\r\n_fluently_, you know. _Please_ tell us some more wonderful things!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey run their railway-trains without any engines\xe2\x80\x94nothing is needed but\r\nmachinery to _stop_ them with. Is _that_ wonderful enough, Miladi?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where does the _force_ come from?\xe2\x80\x9d I ventured to ask.\r\n\r\nMein Herr turned quickly round, to look at the new speaker. Then he took\r\noff his spectacles, and polished them, and looked at me again, in\r\nevident bewilderment. I could see he was thinking\xe2\x80\x94as indeed _I_ was\r\nalso\xe2\x80\x94that we _must_ have met before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey use the force of _gravity_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a force known also in\r\n_your_ country, I believe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that would need a railway going _down-hill_,\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl remarked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t have _all_ your railways going down-hill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey _all_ do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot from _both_ ends?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom _both_ ends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I give it up!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you explain the process?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cWithout using that\r\nlanguage, that I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t speak fluently?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEasily,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9cEach railway is in a long tunnel, perfectly\r\nstraight: so of course the _middle_ of it is nearer the centre of the\r\nglobe than the two ends: so every train runs half-way _down_-hill, and\r\nthat gives it force enough to run the _other_ half _up_-hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. I understand that perfectly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the\r\nvelocity, in the _middle_ of the tunnel, must be something _fearful_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr\xe2\x80\x99 was evidently much gratified at the intelligent interest\r\nLady Muriel took in his remarks. At every moment the old man seemed to\r\ngrow more chatty and more fluent. \xe2\x80\x9cYou would like to know our methods of\r\n_driving_?\xe2\x80\x9d he smilingly enquired. \xe2\x80\x9cTo us, a run-away horse is of no\r\nimport at all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel slightly shuddered. \xe2\x80\x9cTo _us_ it is a very real danger,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is because your carriage is wholly _behind_ your horse. Your horse\r\nruns. Your carriage follows. Perhaps your horse has the bit in his\r\nteeth. Who shall stop him? You fly, ever faster and faster! Finally\r\ncomes the inevitable upset!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut suppose _your_ horse manages to get the bit in his teeth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter! We would not concern ourselves. Our horse is harnessed in\r\nthe very centre of our carriage. Two wheels are in front of him, and two\r\nbehind. To the roof is attached one end of a broad belt. This goes under\r\nthe horse\xe2\x80\x99s body, and the other end is attached to a leetle\xe2\x80\x94what you\r\ncall a \xe2\x80\x98windlass,\xe2\x80\x99 I think. The horse takes the bit in his teeth. He\r\nruns away. We are flying at ten miles an hour! We turn our little\r\nwindlass, five turns, six turns, seven turns, and\xe2\x80\x94poof! Our horse is off\r\nthe ground! _Now_ let him gallop in the air, as much as he pleases: our\r\n_carriage_ stands still. We sit round him, and watch him till he is\r\ntired. Then we let him down. Our horse is glad, very much glad, when his\r\nfeet once more touch the ground!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapital!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl, who had been listening attentively. \xe2\x80\x9cAre there\r\nany other peculiarities in your carriages?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the _wheels_, sometimes, my Lord. For your health, _you_ go to sea:\r\nto be pitched, to be rolled, occasionally to be drowned. _We_ do all\r\nthat on land: we are pitched, as you; we are rolled, as you; but\r\n_drowned_, no! There is no water!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are the wheels like, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are _oval_, my Lord. Therefore the carriages rise and fall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and pitch the carriage backwards and forwards: but how do they\r\nmake it _roll_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey do not match, my Lord. The _end_ of one wheel answers to the\r\n_side_ of the opposite wheel. So first one side of the carriage rises,\r\nthen the other. And it pitches all the while. Ah, you must be a good\r\nsailor, to drive in our boat-carriages!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can easily believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl.\r\n\r\nMein Herr rose to his feet. \xe2\x80\x9cI must leave you now, Miladi,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\nconsulting his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cI have another engagement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI only wish we had stored up some extra time!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel said, as she\r\nshook hands with him. \xe2\x80\x9cThen we could have kept you a little longer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn _that_ case I would gladly stay,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9cAs it is\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nfear I must say good-bye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you first meet him?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked Lady Muriel, when Mein Herr had\r\nleft us. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd where does he live? And what is his real name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe first\xe2\x80\x94met\xe2\x80\x94him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she musingly replied, \xe2\x80\x9creally, I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t remember\r\n_where_! And I\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea where he lives! And I never heard any other\r\nname! It\xe2\x80\x99s very curious. It never occurred to me before to consider what\r\na mystery he is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope we shall meet again,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9che interests me very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will be at our farewell-party, this day fortnight,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you will come? Muriel is anxious to gather all our friends\r\naround us once more, before we leave the place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he explained to me\xe2\x80\x94as Lady Muriel had left us together\xe2\x80\x94that he\r\nwas so anxious to get his daughter away from a place full of so many\r\npainful memories connected with the now-canceled engagement with Major\r\nLindon, that they had arranged to have the wedding in a months time,\r\nafter which Arthur and his wife were to go on a foreign tour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t forget Tuesday week!\xe2\x80\x9d he said as we shook hands at parting. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nonly wish you could bring with you those charming children, that you\r\nintroduced to us in the summer. Talk of the mystery of Mein Herr! That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_nothing_ to the mystery that seems to attend _them_! I shall never\r\nforget those marvellous flowers!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will bring them if I possibly can,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. But how to _fulfil_ such\r\na promise, I mused to myself on my way back to our lodgings, was a\r\nproblem entirely beyond my skill!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER VIII.\r\n                           IN A SHADY PLACE.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe ten days glided swiftly away: and, the day before the great party\r\nwas to take place, Arthur proposed that we should stroll down to the\r\nHall, in time for afternoon-tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHadn\xe2\x80\x99t you better go _alone_?\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested. \xe2\x80\x9cSurely _I_ shall be very\r\nmuch _de trop_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99ll be a kind of _experiment_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9c_Fiat experimentum in\r\ncorpore vili!_\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with a graceful bow of mock politeness towards\r\nthe unfortunate victim. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see I shall have to bear the sight,\r\nto-morrow night, of my lady-love making herself agreable to everybody\r\n_except_ the right person, and I shall bear the agony all the better if\r\nwe have a dress-rehearsal beforehand!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_My_ part in the play being, apparently, that of the sample _wrong_\r\nperson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur said musingly, as we set forth: \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s no such part\r\nin a regular company. \xe2\x80\x98Heavy Father\xe2\x80\x99? _That_ won\xe2\x80\x99t do: that\xe2\x80\x99s filled\r\nalready. \xe2\x80\x98Singing Chambermaid\xe2\x80\x99? Well, the \xe2\x80\x98First Lady\xe2\x80\x99 doubles _that_\r\npart. \xe2\x80\x98Comic Old Man\xe2\x80\x99? You\xe2\x80\x99re not comic enough. After all, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s no part for you but the \xe2\x80\x98Well-dressed Villain\xe2\x80\x99: only,\xe2\x80\x9d with a\r\ncritical side-glance, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a _leetle_ uncertain about the dress!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe found Lady Muriel alone, the Earl having gone out to make a call, and\r\nat once resumed old terms of intimacy, in the shady arbour where the\r\ntea-things seemed to be always waiting. The only novelty in the\r\narrangements (one which Lady Muriel seemed to regard as _entirely_ a\r\nmatter of course), was that two of the chairs were placed _quite_ close\r\ntogether, side by side. Strange to say, _I_ was not invited to occupy\r\n_either_ of them!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have been arranging, as we came along, about letter-writing,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur\r\nbegan. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will want to know how we\xe2\x80\x99re enjoying our Swiss tour: and of\r\ncourse we must pretend we _are_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d she meekly assented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the skeleton-in-the-cupboard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94is always a difficulty,\xe2\x80\x9d she quickly put in, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you\xe2\x80\x99re traveling\r\nabout, and when there are no cupboards in the hotels. However, _ours_ is\r\na _very_ portable one; and will be neatly packed, in a nice leather\r\ncase\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut please don\xe2\x80\x99t think about _writing_,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you\xe2\x80\x99ve anything\r\nmore attractive on hand. I delight in _reading_ letters, but I know well\r\nhow tiring it is to _write_ them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _is_, sometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur assented. \xe2\x80\x9cFor instance, when you\xe2\x80\x99re very\r\nshy of the person you have to write to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes that show itself in the _letter_?\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel enquired. \xe2\x80\x9cOf\r\ncourse, when I hear any one _talking_\xe2\x80\x94_you_, for instance\xe2\x80\x94I can see how\r\n_desperately_ shy he is!
