But can you see that in a _letter_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, of course, when you hear any one talk _fluently_\xe2\x80\x94_you_, for\r\ninstance\xe2\x80\x94you can see how desperately _un_-shy she is\xe2\x80\x94not to say saucy!\r\nBut the shyest and most intermittent talker must _seem_ fluent in\r\nletter-writing. He may have taken half-an-hour to _compose_ his second\r\nsentence; but there it is, close after the first!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen letters don\xe2\x80\x99t express all that they _might_ express?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s merely because our system of letter-writing is incomplete. A shy\r\nwriter _ought_ to be able to show that he is so. Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t he make\r\n_pauses_ in writing, just as he would do in speaking? He might leave\r\nblank spaces\xe2\x80\x94say half a page at a time. And a _very_ shy girl\xe2\x80\x94if there\r\n_is_ such a thing\xe2\x80\x94might write a sentence on the _first_ sheet of her\r\nletter\xe2\x80\x94then put in a couple of _blank_ sheets\xe2\x80\x94then a sentence on the\r\n_fourth_ sheet: and so on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite foresee that _we_\xe2\x80\x94I mean this clever little boy and myself\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nLady Muriel said to me, evidently with the kind wish to bring me into\r\nthe conversation, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94are going to become famous\xe2\x80\x94of course all our\r\ninventions are common property now\xe2\x80\x94for a new Code of Rules for\r\nLetter-writing! Please invent some more, little boy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, another thing _greatly_ needed, little girl, is some way of\r\nexpressing that we _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ mean anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExplain yourself, little boy! Surely _you_ can find no difficulty in\r\nexpressing a _total_ absence of meaning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that you should be able, when you _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ mean a thing to be\r\ntaken seriously, to express that wish. For human nature is so\r\nconstituted that whatever you write seriously is taken as a joke, and\r\nwhatever you mean as a joke is taken seriously! At any rate, it is so in\r\nwriting to a _lady_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you\xe2\x80\x99re not used to writing to ladies!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel remarked,\r\nleaning back in her chair, and gazing thoughtfully into the sky. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nshould try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cHow many ladies may I begin writing to? As\r\nmany as I can count on the fingers of both hands?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs many as you can count on the _thumbs_ of _one_ hand!\xe2\x80\x9d his lady-love\r\nreplied with much severity. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a _very_ naughty little boy he is!\r\n_Isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ he?\xe2\x80\x9d (with an appealing glance at me).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a little fractious,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he\xe2\x80\x99s cutting a tooth.\xe2\x80\x9d While\r\nto myself I said \xe2\x80\x9cHow _exactly_ like Sylvie talking to Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wants his tea.\xe2\x80\x9d (The naughty little boy volunteered the\r\ninformation.) \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s getting very tired, at the mere _prospect_ of the\r\ngreat party to-morrow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he shall have a good rest beforehand!\xe2\x80\x9d she soothingly replied.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tea isn\xe2\x80\x99t made yet. Come, little boy, lean well back in your chair,\r\nand think about nothing\xe2\x80\x94or about _me_, whichever you prefer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the same, all the same!\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur sleepily murmured, watching her\r\nwith loving eyes, as she moved her chair away to the tea table, and\r\nbegan to make the tea. \xe2\x80\x9cThen he\xe2\x80\x99ll wait for his tea, like a good,\r\npatient little boy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I bring you the London Papers?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw them\r\nlying on the table as I came out, but my father said there was nothing\r\nin them, except that horrid murder-trial.\xe2\x80\x9d (Society was just then\r\nenjoying its daily thrill of excitement in studying the details of a\r\nspecially sensational murder in a thieves\xe2\x80\x99 den in the East of London.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no appetite for horrors,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I hope we have\r\nlearned the lesson they should teach us\xe2\x80\x94though we are very apt to read\r\nit backwards!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98I AM SITTING AT YOUR FEET\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak in riddles,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease explain yourself. See\r\nnow,\xe2\x80\x9d suiting the action to the word, \xe2\x80\x9cI am sitting at your feet, just\r\nas if you were a second Gamaliel! Thanks, no.\xe2\x80\x9d (This was to me, who had\r\nrisen to bring her chair back to its former place.) \xe2\x80\x9cPray don\xe2\x80\x99t disturb\r\nyourself. This tree and the grass make a very nice easy-chair. _What_ is\r\nthe lesson that one always reads wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur was silent for a minute. \xe2\x80\x9cI would like to be clear what it _is_ I\r\nmean,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, slowly and thoughtfully, \xe2\x80\x9cbefore I say anything to\r\n_you_\xe2\x80\x94because you _think_ about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnything approaching to a compliment was so unusual an utterance for\r\nArthur, that it brought a flush of pleasure to her cheek, as she replied\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is _you_, that give me the ideas to think about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne\xe2\x80\x99s first thought,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur proceeded, \xe2\x80\x9cin reading of anything\r\nspecially vile or barbarous, as done by a fellow-creature, is apt to be\r\nthat we see a new depth of Sin revealed _beneath_ us: and we seem to\r\ngaze down into that abyss from some higher ground, far apart from it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I understand you now. You mean that one ought to think\xe2\x80\x94not\r\n\xe2\x80\x98God, I thank Thee that I am not as other men are\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94but \xe2\x80\x98God, be merciful\r\nto me also, who might be, but for Thy grace, a sinner as vile as he!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cI meant a great deal more than that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked up quickly, but checked herself, and waited in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne must begin further back, I think. Think of some other man, the same\r\nage as this poor wretch. Look back to the time when they both began\r\nlife\xe2\x80\x94before they had sense enough to know Right from Wrong. _Then_, at\r\nany rate, they were equal in God\xe2\x80\x99s sight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nodded assent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have, then, two distinct epochs at which we may contemplate the two\r\nmen whose lives we are comparing. At the first epoch they are, so far as\r\nmoral responsibility is concerned, on precisely the same footing: they\r\nare alike incapable of doing right or wrong. At the second epoch the one\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94I am taking an extreme case, for contrast\xe2\x80\x94has won the esteem and\r\nlove of all around him: his character is stainless, and his name will be\r\nheld in honour hereafter: the other man\xe2\x80\x99s history is one unvaried record\r\nof crime, and his life is at last forfeited to the outraged laws of his\r\ncountry. Now what have been the causes, in each case, of each man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncondition being what it is at the second epoch? They are of two\r\nkinds\xe2\x80\x94one acting from within, the other from without. These two kinds\r\nneed to be discussed separately\xe2\x80\x94that is, if I have not already tired you\r\nwith my prosing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, \xe2\x80\x9cit is a special delight to me to\r\nhave a question discussed in this way\xe2\x80\x94analysed and arranged, so that one\r\ncan understand it. Some books, that profess to argue out a question, are\r\nto me intolerably wearisome, simply because the ideas are all arranged\r\nhap-hazard\xe2\x80\x94a sort of \xe2\x80\x98first come, first served.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very encouraging,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur replied, with a pleased look. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\ncauses, acting from _within_, which make a man\xe2\x80\x99s character what it is at\r\nany given moment, are his successive acts of volition\xe2\x80\x94that is, his acts\r\nof choosing whether he will do this or that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are to assume the existence of Free-Will?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, in order to have\r\nthat point made quite clear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf not,\xe2\x80\x9d was the quiet reply, \xe2\x80\x9c_cadit quaestio_: and I have no more to\r\nsay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe _will_ assume it!\xe2\x80\x9d the rest of the audience\xe2\x80\x94the majority, I may say,\r\nlooking at it from Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s point of view\xe2\x80\x94imperiously proclaimed. The\r\norator proceeded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe causes, acting from _without_, are his surroundings\xe2\x80\x94what Mr.\r\nHerbert Spencer calls his \xe2\x80\x98environment.\xe2\x80\x99 Now the point I want to make\r\nclear is this, that a man is responsible for his acts of choosing, but\r\n_not_ responsible for his environment. Hence, if these two men make, on\r\nsome given occasion, when they are exposed to equal temptation, equal\r\nefforts to resist and to choose the right, their condition, in the sight\r\nof God, must be the same. If He is pleased in the one case, so will He\r\nbe in the other; if displeased in the one case, so also in the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so, no doubt: I see it quite clearly,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel put in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet, owing to their different environments, the one may win a great\r\nvictory over the temptation, while the other falls into some black abyss\r\nof crime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely you would not say those men were equally guilty in the sight\r\nof God?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEither that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, \xe2\x80\x9cor else I must give up my belief in God\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nperfect justice. But let me put one more case, which will show my\r\nmeaning even more forcibly. Let the one man be in a high social\r\nposition\xe2\x80\x94the other, say, a common thief. Let the one be tempted to some\r\ntrivial act of unfair dealing\xe2\x80\x94something which he can do with the\r\nabsolute certainty that it will never be discovered\xe2\x80\x94something which he\r\ncan with perfect ease forbear from doing\xe2\x80\x94and which he distinctly knows\r\nto be a sin. Let the other be tempted to some terrible crime\xe2\x80\x94as men\r\nwould consider it\xe2\x80\x94but under an almost overwhelming pressure of\r\nmotives\xe2\x80\x94of course not _quite_ overwhelming, as that would destroy all\r\nresponsibility. Now, in this case, let the second man make a _greater_\r\neffort at resistance than the first. Also suppose _both_ to fall under\r\nthe temptation\xe2\x80\x94I say that the second man is, in God\xe2\x80\x99s sight, _less_\r\nguilty than the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel drew a long breath. \xe2\x80\x9cIt upsets all one\xe2\x80\x99s ideas of Right and\r\nWrong\xe2\x80\x94just at first! Why, in that dreadful murder-trial, you would say,\r\nI suppose, that it was possible that the least guilty man in the Court\r\nwas the murderer, and that possibly the judge who tried him, by yielding\r\nto the temptation of making one unfair remark, had committed a crime\r\noutweighing the criminal\xe2\x80\x99s whole career!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I should,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur firmly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIt sounds like a paradox,\r\nI admit. But just think what a grievous sin it must be, in God\xe2\x80\x99s sight,\r\nto yield to some very slight temptation, which we could have resisted\r\nwith perfect ease, and to do it deliberately, and in the full light of\r\nGod\xe2\x80\x99s Law. What penance can atone for a sin like _that_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t reject your theory,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut how it seems to widen the\r\npossible area of Sin in the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that so?\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel anxiously enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, not so, not so!\xe2\x80\x9d was the eager reply. \xe2\x80\x9cTo me it seems to clear away\r\nmuch of the cloud that hangs over the world\xe2\x80\x99s history. When this view\r\nfirst made itself clear to me, I remember walking out into the fields,\r\nrepeating to myself that line of Tennyson \xe2\x80\x98_There seemed no room for\r\nsense of wrong!_\xe2\x80\x99 The thought, that perhaps the real guilt of the human\r\nrace was infinitely less than I fancied it\xe2\x80\x94that the millions, whom I had\r\nthought of as sunk in hopeless depths of sin, were perhaps, in God\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsight, scarcely sinning at all\xe2\x80\x94was more sweet than words can tell! Life\r\nseemed more bright and beautiful, when once that thought had come! \xe2\x80\x98_A\r\nlivelier emerald twinkles in the grass, A purer sapphire melts into the\r\nsea!_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d His voice trembled as he concluded, and the tears stood in his\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nLady Muriel shaded her face with her hand, and was silent for a minute.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a beautiful thought,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking up at last. \xe2\x80\x9cThank\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94Arthur, for putting it into my head!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Earl returned in time to join us at tea, and to give us the very\r\nunwelcome tidings that a fever had broken out in the little harbour-town\r\nthat lay below us\xe2\x80\x94a fever of so malignant a type that, though it had\r\nonly appeared a day or two ago, there were already more than a dozen\r\ndown in it, two or three of whom were reported to be in imminent danger.\r\n\r\nIn answer to the eager questions of Arthur\xe2\x80\x94who of course took a deep\r\nscientific interest in the matter\xe2\x80\x94he could give very few _technical_\r\ndetails, though he had met the local doctor. It appeared, however, that\r\nit was an almost _new_ disease\xe2\x80\x94at least in _this_ century, though it\r\n_might_ prove to be identical with the \xe2\x80\x98Plague\xe2\x80\x99 recorded in\r\nHistory\xe2\x80\x94_very_ infectious, and frightfully rapid in its action. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will\r\nnot, however, prevent our party to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in conclusion. \xe2\x80\x9cNone\r\nof the guests belong to the infected district, which is, as you know,\r\nexclusively peopled by fishermen: so you may come without any fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur was very silent, all the way back, and, on reaching our lodgings,\r\nimmediately plunged into medical studies, connected with the alarming\r\nmalady of whose arrival we had just heard.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER IX.\r\n                          THE FAREWELL-PARTY.\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the following day, Arthur and I reached the Hall in good time, as\r\nonly a few of the guests\xe2\x80\x94it was to be a party of eighteen\xe2\x80\x94had as yet\r\narrived; and these were talking with the Earl, leaving us the\r\nopportunity of a few words apart with our hostess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that _very_ learned-looking man with the large spectacles?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nArthur enquired. \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t met him here before, have I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he\xe2\x80\x99s a new friend of ours,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel: \xe2\x80\x9ca German, I believe.\r\nHe _is_ such a dear old thing! And quite the most learned man I ever\r\nmet\xe2\x80\x94with _one_ exception, of course!\xe2\x80\x9d she added humbly, as Arthur drew\r\nhimself up with an air of offended dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the young lady in blue, just beyond him, talking to that\r\nforeign-looking man. Is _she_ learned, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99m told she\xe2\x80\x99s a wonderful\r\npiano-forte-player. I hope you\xe2\x80\x99ll hear her to-night. I asked that\r\nforeigner to take her in, because _he\xe2\x80\x99s_ very musical, too. He\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nFrench Count, I believe; and he sings _splendidly_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cScience\xe2\x80\x94music\xe2\x80\x94singing\xe2\x80\x94you have indeed got a complete party!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nArthur. \xe2\x80\x9cI feel quite a privileged person, meeting all these stars. I\r\n_do_ love music!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the party isn\xe2\x80\x99t _quite_ complete!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cYou haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbrought us those two beautiful children,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, turning to me.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe brought them here to tea, you know, one day last summer,\xe2\x80\x9d again\r\naddressing Arthur; \xe2\x80\x9cand they _are_ such darlings!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are, _indeed_,\xe2\x80\x9d I assented.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why haven\xe2\x80\x99t you brought them with you? You promised my father you\r\n_would_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut really it was impossible to bring them\r\nwith me.\xe2\x80\x9d Here I most certainly _meant_ to conclude the sentence: and it\r\nwas with a feeling of utter amazement, which I cannot adequately\r\ndescribe, that I heard myself _going on speaking_. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94but they are to\r\njoin me here in the course of the evening\xe2\x80\x9d were the words, uttered in\r\n_my_ voice, and seeming to come from _my_ lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m _so_ glad!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel joyfully replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI _shall_ enjoy\r\nintroducing them to some of my friends here! When do you expect them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI took refuge in silence. The only _honest_ reply would have been \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nwas not _my_ remark. _I_ didn\xe2\x80\x99t say it, and _it isn\xe2\x80\x99t true_!\xe2\x80\x9d But I had\r\nnot the moral courage to make such a confession. The character of a\r\n\xe2\x80\x98lunatic\xe2\x80\x99 is not, I believe, very difficult to _acquire_: but it is\r\namazingly difficult to _get rid of_: and it seemed quite certain that\r\nany such speech as _that_ would _quite_ justify the issue of a writ \xe2\x80\x98_de\r\nlunatico inquirendo_.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLady Muriel evidently thought I had failed to hear her question, and\r\nturned to Arthur with a remark on some other subject; and I had time to\r\nrecover from my shock of surprise\xe2\x80\x94or to awake out of my momentary\r\n\xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 condition, whichever it was.\r\n\r\nWhen things around me seemed once more to be real, Arthur was saying\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid there\xe2\x80\x99s no help for it: they _must_ be finite in number.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be sorry to have to believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cYet, when\r\none comes to think of it, there _are_ no new melodies, now-a-days. What\r\npeople talk of as \xe2\x80\x98the last new song\xe2\x80\x99 always recalls to _me_ some tune\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve known as a child!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe day must come\xe2\x80\x94if the world lasts long enough\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen\r\nevery possible tune will have been composed\xe2\x80\x94every possible pun\r\nperpetrated\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (Lady Muriel wrung her hands, like a tragedy-queen) \xe2\x80\x9cand,\r\nworse than that, every possible _book_ written! For the number of\r\n_words_ is finite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll make very little difference to the _authors_,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInstead of saying \xe2\x80\x98_what_ book shall I write?\xe2\x80\x99 an author will ask\r\nhimself \xe2\x80\x98_which_ book shall I write?\xe2\x80\x99 A mere verbal distinction!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel gave me an approving smile. \xe2\x80\x9cBut _lunatics_ would always\r\nwrite new books, surely?\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cThey _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ write the sane\r\nbooks over again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cBut _their_ books would come to an end, also. The\r\nnumber of lunatic _books_ is as finite as the number of lunatics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd _that_ number is becoming greater every year,\xe2\x80\x9d said a pompous man,\r\nwhom I recognised as the self-appointed showman on the day of the\r\npicnic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo they say,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, when ninety per cent. of us are\r\nlunatics,\xe2\x80\x9d (he seemed to be in a wildly nonsensical mood) \xe2\x80\x9cthe asylums\r\nwill be put to their proper use.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d the pompous man gravely enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_To shelter the sane!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9c_We_ shall bar ourselves in. The\r\nlunatics will have it all their own way, _outside_. They\xe2\x80\x99ll do it a\r\nlittle queerly, no doubt. Railway-collisions will be always happening:\r\nsteamers always blowing up: most of the towns will be burnt down: most\r\nof the ships sunk\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd most of the men _killed_!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the pompous man, who was\r\nevidently hopelessly bewildered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur assented. \xe2\x80\x9cTill at last there will be _fewer_\r\nlunatics than sane men. Then _we_ come out: _they_ go in: and things\r\nreturn to their normal condition!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pompous man frowned darkly, and bit his lip, and folded his arms,\r\nvainly trying to think it out. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is _jesting_!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered to himself\r\nat last, in a tone of withering contempt, as he stalked away.\r\n\r\nBy this time the other guests had arrived; and dinner was announced.\r\nArthur of course took down Lady Muriel: and _I_ was pleased to find\r\nmyself seated at her other side, with a severe-looking old lady (whom I\r\nhad not met before, and whose name I had, as is usual in introductions,\r\nentirely failed to catch, merely gathering that it sounded like a\r\ncompound-name) as my partner for the banquet.\r\n\r\nShe appeared, however, to be acquainted with Arthur, and confided to me\r\nin a low voice her opinion that he was \xe2\x80\x9ca very argumentative young man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nArthur, for his part, seemed well inclined to show himself worthy of the\r\ncharacter she had given him, and, hearing her say \xe2\x80\x9cI never take wine\r\nwith my soup!\xe2\x80\x9d (this was _not_ a confidence to me, but was launched upon\r\nSociety, as a matter of general interest), he at once challenged a\r\ncombat by asking her \xe2\x80\x9c_when_ would you say that property _commence_ in a\r\nplate of soup?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is _my_ soup,\xe2\x80\x9d she sternly replied: \xe2\x80\x9cand what is before you is\r\n_yours_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur: \xe2\x80\x9cbut _when_ did I begin to own it? Up to the\r\nmoment of its being put into the plate, it was the property of our host:\r\nwhile being offered round the table, it was, let us say, held in trust\r\nby the waiter: did it become mine when I accepted it? Or when it was\r\nplaced before me? Or when I took the first spoonful?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a _very_ argumentative young man!\xe2\x80\x9d was all the old lady would\r\nsay: but she said it audibly, this time, feeling that Society had a\r\nright to know it.\r\n\r\nArthur smiled mischievously. \xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind betting you a shilling,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the Eminent Barrister next you\xe2\x80\x9d (It certainly _is_\r\npossible to say words so as to make them begin with capitals!) \xe2\x80\x9cca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nanswer me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _never_ bet,\xe2\x80\x9d she sternly replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot even sixpenny points at _whist_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Never!_\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated. \xe2\x80\x9c_Whist_ is innocent enough: but whist played\r\nfor _money_!\xe2\x80\x9d She shuddered.\r\n\r\nArthur became serious again. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t take that view,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI consider that the introduction of small stakes for card-playing\r\nwas one of the most _moral_ acts Society ever did, _as_ Society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow was it so?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it took Cards, once for all, out of the category of games at\r\nwhich _cheating_ is possible. Look at the way Croquet is demoralising\r\nSociety. Ladies are beginning to cheat at it, terribly: and, if they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nfound out, they only laugh, and call it fun. But when there\xe2\x80\x99s _money_ at\r\nstake, that is out of the question. The swindler is _not_ accepted as a\r\nwit. When a man sits down to cards, and cheats his friends out of their\r\nmoney, he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t get much _fun_ out of it\xe2\x80\x94unless he thinks it fun to be\r\nkicked down stairs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf all gentlemen thought as badly of ladies as _you_ do,\xe2\x80\x9d my neighbour\r\nremarked with some bitterness, \xe2\x80\x9cthere would be very few\xe2\x80\x94very few\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nseemed doubtful how to end her sentence, but at last took \xe2\x80\x9choneymoons\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nas a safe word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, the mischievous smile returning to his\r\nface, \xe2\x80\x9cif only people would adopt _my_ theory, the number of\r\nhoneymoons\xe2\x80\x94quite of a new kind\xe2\x80\x94would be greatly increased!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay we hear about this new kind of honeymoon?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet _X_ be the gentleman,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur began, in a slightly raised voice, as\r\nhe now found himself with an audience of _six_, including \xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwho was seated at the other side of my polynomial partner. \xe2\x80\x9cLet _X_ be\r\nthe gentleman, and _Y_ the lady to whom he thinks of proposing. He\r\napplies for an Experimental Honeymoon. It is granted. Forthwith the\r\nyoung couple\xe2\x80\x94accompanied by the great-aunt of _Y_, to act as\r\nchaperone\xe2\x80\x94start for a month\xe2\x80\x99s tour, during which they have many a\r\nmoonlight-walk, and many a _t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_ conversation, and each can form\r\na more correct estimate of the other\xe2\x80\x99s character, in four _weeks_, than\r\nwould have been possible in as many _years_, when meeting under the\r\nordinary restrictions of Society. And it is only after their _return_\r\nthat _X_ finally decides whether he will, or will not, put the momentous\r\nquestion to _Y_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn nine cases out of ten,\xe2\x80\x9d the pompous man proclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9che would decide\r\nto break it off!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, in nine cases out of ten,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur rejoined, \xe2\x80\x9can unsuitable match\r\nwould be prevented, and _both_ parties saved from misery!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe only really _unsuitable_ matches,\xe2\x80\x9d the old lady remarked, \xe2\x80\x9care\r\nthose made without sufficient _Money_. Love may come _afterwards_. Money\r\nis needed _to begin with_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis remark was cast loose upon Society, as a sort of general challenge;\r\nand, as such, it was at once accepted by several of those within\r\nhearing: _Money_ became the key-note of the conversation for some time;\r\nand a fitful echo of it was again heard, when the dessert had been\r\nplaced upon the table, the servants had left the room, and the Earl had\r\nstarted the wine in its welcome progress round the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very glad to see you keep up the old customs,\xe2\x80\x9d I said to Lady\r\nMuriel as I filled her glass. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s really delightful to experience,\r\nonce more, the peaceful feeling that comes over one when the waiters\r\nhave left the room\xe2\x80\x94when one can converse without the feeling of being\r\noverheard, and without having dishes constantly thrust over one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshoulder. How much more sociable it is to be able to pour out the wine\r\nfor the ladies, and to hand the dishes to those who wish for them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case, kindly send those peaches down here,\xe2\x80\x9d said a fat\r\nred-faced man, who was seated beyond our pompous friend. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\nwishing for them\xe2\x80\x94diagonally\xe2\x80\x94for some time!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it _is_ a ghastly innovation,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel replied, \xe2\x80\x9cletting the\r\nwaiters carry round the wine at dessert. For one thing, they _always_\r\ntake it the wrong way round\xe2\x80\x94which of course brings bad luck to\r\n_everybody_ present!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter go the _wrong_ way than not go _at all_!\xe2\x80\x9d said our host. \xe2\x80\x9cWould\r\nyou kindly help yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d (This was to the fat red-faced man.) \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\r\nnot a teetotaler, I think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed but I _am_!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, as he pushed on the bottles. \xe2\x80\x9cNearly\r\ntwice as much money is spent in England on _Drink_, as on any other\r\narticle of food. Read this card.\xe2\x80\x9d (What faddist ever goes about without\r\na pocketful of the appropriate literature?) \xe2\x80\x9cThe stripes of different\r\ncolours represent the amounts spent on various articles of food. Look at\r\nthe highest three. Money spent on butter and on cheese, thirty-five\r\nmillions: on bread, seventy millions: on _intoxicating liquors_, one\r\nhundred and thirty-six millions! If I had my way, I would close every\r\npublic-house in the land! Look at that card, and read the motto. _That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhere all the money goes to!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you seen the _Anti-Teetotal Card_?\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur innocently enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Sir, I have not!\xe2\x80\x9d the orator savagely replied. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlmost exactly like this one. The coloured stripes are the same. Only,\r\ninstead of the words \xe2\x80\x98Money spent on,\xe2\x80\x99 it has \xe2\x80\x98Incomes derived from sale\r\nof\xe2\x80\x99; and, instead of \xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s where all the money goes to,\xe2\x80\x99 its motto is\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_That\xe2\x80\x99s where all the money comes from!_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe red-faced man scowled, but evidently considered Arthur beneath his\r\nnotice. So Lady Muriel took up the cudgels. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you hold the theory,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe enquired, \xe2\x80\x9cthat people can preach teetotalism more effectually by\r\nbeing teetotalers themselves?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I do!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the red-faced man. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, here is a case in\r\npoint,\xe2\x80\x9d unfolding a newspaper-cutting: \xe2\x80\x9clet me read you this letter from\r\na teetotaler. _To the Editor. Sir, I was once a moderate drinker, and\r\nknew a man who drank to excess. I went to him. \xe2\x80\x98Give up this drink,\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x98It will ruin your health!\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98You drink,\xe2\x80\x99 he said: \xe2\x80\x98why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nI?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98but I know when to leave off.\xe2\x80\x99 He turned away from\r\nme. \xe2\x80\x98You drink in your way,\xe2\x80\x99 he said: \xe2\x80\x98let me drink in mine. Be off!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nThen I saw that, to do any good with him, I must forswear drink. From\r\nthat hour I haven\xe2\x80\x99t touched a drop!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere! What do you say to _that_?\xe2\x80\x9d He looked round triumphantly, while\r\nthe cutting was handed round for inspection.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow very curious!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Arthur, when it had reached him. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you\r\nhappen to see a letter, last week, about early rising? It was strangely\r\nlike this one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe red-faced man\xe2\x80\x99s curiosity was roused. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere did it appear?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me read it to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. He took some papers from his\r\npocket, opened one of them, and read as follows. \xe2\x80\x9c_To the Editor. Sir, I\r\nwas once a moderate sleeper, and knew a man who slept to excess. I\r\npleaded with him. \xe2\x80\x98Give up this lying in bed,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98It will ruin\r\nyour health!\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98You go to bed,\xe2\x80\x99 he said: \xe2\x80\x98why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x98but I know when to get up in the morning.\xe2\x80\x99 He turned away from\r\nme. \xe2\x80\x98You sleep in your way,\xe2\x80\x99 he said: \xe2\x80\x98let me sleep in mine. Be off!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nThen I saw that to do any good with him, I must forswear sleep. From\r\nthat hour I haven\xe2\x80\x99t been to bed!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur folded and pocketed his paper, and passed on the\r\nnewspaper-cutting. None of us dared to laugh, the red-faced man was\r\nevidently so angry. \xe2\x80\x9cYour parallel doesn\xe2\x80\x99t run on all fours!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsnarled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Moderate_ drinkers never do so!\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur quietly replied. Even the\r\nstern old lady laughed at this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it needs many other things to make a _perfect_ dinner!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady\r\nMuriel, evidently anxious to change the subject. \xe2\x80\x9cMein Herr! What is\r\n_your_ idea of a perfect dinner-party?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man looked round smilingly, and his gigantic spectacles seemed\r\nmore gigantic than ever. \xe2\x80\x9cA _perfect_ dinner-party?\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst, it must be presided over by our present hostess!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat, of _course_!\xe2\x80\x9d she gaily interposed. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what _else_, Mein Herr?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can but tell you what I have seen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr, \xe2\x80\x9cin mine own\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nthe country I have traveled in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe paused for a full minute, and gazed steadily at the ceiling\xe2\x80\x94with so\r\ndreamy an expression on his face, that I feared he was going off into a\r\nreverie, which seemed to be his normal state. However, after a minute,\r\nhe suddenly began again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat which chiefly causes the failure of a dinner-party, is the\r\nrunning-short\xe2\x80\x94not of meat, nor yet of drink, but of _conversation_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn an _English_ dinner-party,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cI have never known\r\n_small-talk_ run short!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr respectfully replied, \xe2\x80\x9cI did not say\r\n\xe2\x80\x98small-talk.\xe2\x80\x99 I said \xe2\x80\x98conversation.\xe2\x80\x99 All such topics as the weather, or\r\npolitics, or local gossip, are unknown among us. They are either vapid\r\nor controversial. What we need for _conversation_ is a topic of\r\n_interest_ and of _novelty_. To secure these things we have tried\r\nvarious plans\xe2\x80\x94Moving-Pictures, Wild-Creatures, Moving-Guests, and a\r\nRevolving-Humorist. But this last is only adapted to _small_ parties.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us have it in four separate Chapters, please!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel,\r\nwho was evidently deeply interested\xe2\x80\x94as, indeed, most of the party were,\r\nby this time: and, all down the table, talk had ceased, and heads were\r\nleaning forwards, eager to catch fragments of Mein Herr\xe2\x80\x99s oration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChapter One! Moving-Pictures!\xe2\x80\x9d was proclaimed in the silvery voice of\r\nour hostess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dining-table is shaped like a circular ring,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr began, in\r\nlow dreamy tones, which, however, were perfectly audible in the silence.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe guests are seated at the inner side as well as the outer, having\r\nascended to their places by a winding-staircase, from the room below.\r\nAlong the middle of the table runs a little railway; and there is an\r\nendless train of trucks, worked round by machinery; and on each truck\r\nthere are two pictures, leaning back to back. The train makes two\r\ncircuits during dinner; and, when it has been _once_ round, the waiters\r\nturn the pictures round in each truck, making them face the other way.\r\nThus _every_ guest sees _every_ picture!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe paused, and the silence seemed deader than ever. Lady Muriel looked\r\naghast. \xe2\x80\x9cReally, if this goes on,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cI shall have to drop\r\na pin! Oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s _my_ fault, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d (In answer to an appealing look from\r\nMein Herr.) \xe2\x80\x9cI was forgetting my duty. Chapter Two! Wild-Creatures!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe found the Moving-Pictures a _little_ monotonous,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople didn\xe2\x80\x99t care to talk Art through a whole dinner; so we tried\r\nWild-Creatures. Among the flowers, which we laid (just as _you_ do)\r\nabout the table, were to be seen, here a mouse, there a beetle; here a\r\nspider,\xe2\x80\x9d (Lady Muriel shuddered) \xe2\x80\x9cthere a wasp; here a toad, there a\r\nsnake;\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cFather!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, plaintively. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear\r\n_that_?\xe2\x80\x9d) \xe2\x80\x9cso we had plenty to talk about!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when you got stung\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d the old lady began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were all chained-up, dear Madam!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd the old lady gave a satisfied nod.\r\n\r\nThere was no silence to follow, _this_ time. \xe2\x80\x9cThird Chapter!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady\r\nMuriel proclaimed at once, \xe2\x80\x9cMoving-Guests!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven the Wild-Creatures proved monotonous,\xe2\x80\x9d the orator proceeded. \xe2\x80\x9cSo\r\nwe left the guests to choose their own subjects; and, to avoid monotony,\r\nwe changed _them_. We made the table of _two_ rings; and the inner ring\r\nmoved slowly round, all the time, along with the floor in the middle and\r\nthe inner row of guests. Thus _every_ inner guest was brought\r\nface-to-face with _every_ outer guest. It was a little confusing,\r\nsometimes, to have to _begin_ a story to one friend and _finish_ it to\r\nanother; but _every_ plan has its faults, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFourth Chapter!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel hastened to announce. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nRevolving-Humorist!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a _small_ party we found it an excellent plan to have a round\r\ntable, with a hole cut in the middle large enough to hold _one_ guest.\r\nHere we placed our _best_ talker. He revolved slowly, facing every other\r\nguest in turn: and he told lively anecdotes the whole time!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the pompous man. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would make me\r\ngiddy, revolving like that! I should decline to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d here it appeared to\r\ndawn upon him that perhaps the assumption he was making was not\r\nwarranted by the circumstances: he took a hasty gulp of wine, and choked\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\nBut Mein Herr had relapsed into reverie, and made no further remark.\r\nLady Muriel gave the signal, and the ladies left the room.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                               CHAPTER X.\r\n                           JABBERING AND JAM.\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen the last lady had disappeared, and the Earl, taking his place at\r\nthe head of the table, had issued the military order \xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen! Close\r\nup the ranks, if you please!\xe2\x80\x9d, and when, in obedience to his command, we\r\nhad gathered ourselves compactly round him, the pompous man gave a deep\r\nsigh of relief, filled his glass to the brim, pushed on the wine, and\r\nbegan one of his favorite orations. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are charming, no doubt!\r\nCharming, but very frivolous. They drag us down, so to speak, to a lower\r\nlevel. They\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not all pronouns require antecedent _nouns_?\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl gently\r\nenquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the pompous man, with lofty condescension. \xe2\x80\x9cI had\r\noverlooked the noun. The ladies. We regret their absence. Yet we console\r\nourselves. _Thought is free._ With them, we are limited to _trivial_\r\ntopics\xe2\x80\x94Art, Literature, Politics, and so forth. One can bear to discuss\r\n_such_ paltry matters with a lady. But no man, in his senses\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (he\r\nlooked sternly round the table, as if defying contradiction) \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94ever yet\r\ndiscussed _WINE_ with a lady!\xe2\x80\x9d He sipped his glass of port, leaned back\r\nin his chair, and slowly raised it up to his eye, so as to look through\r\nit at the lamp. \xe2\x80\x9cThe vintage, my Lord?\xe2\x80\x9d he enquired, glancing at his\r\nhost.\r\n\r\nThe Earl named the date.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I had supposed. But one likes to be certain. The _tint_ is, perhaps,\r\nslightly pale. But the _body_ is unquestionable. And as for the\r\n_bouquet_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAh, that magic Bouquet! How vividly that single word recalled the scene!\r\nThe little beggar-boy turning his somersault in the road\xe2\x80\x94the sweet\r\nlittle crippled maiden in my arms\xe2\x80\x94the mysterious evanescent\r\nnurse-maid\xe2\x80\x94all rushed tumultuously into my mind, like the creatures of a\r\ndream: and through this mental haze there still boomed on, like the\r\ntolling of a bell, the solemn voice of the great connoisseur of _WINE_!\r\n\r\nEven _his_ utterances had taken on themselves a strange and dream-like\r\nform. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed\xe2\x80\x94and _why_ is it, I pause to ask, that, in taking\r\nup the broken thread of a dialogue, one _always_ begins with this\r\ncheerless monosyllable? After much anxious thought, I have come to the\r\nconclusion that the object in view is the same as that of the schoolboy,\r\nwhen the sum he is working has got into a hopeless muddle, and when in\r\ndespair he takes the sponge, washes it all out, and begins again. Just\r\nin the same way the bewildered orator, by the simple process of denying\r\n_everything_ that has been hitherto asserted, makes a clean sweep of the\r\nwhole discussion, and can \xe2\x80\x98start fair\xe2\x80\x99 with a fresh theory. \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nresumed: \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing like cherry-jam, after all. That\xe2\x80\x99s what _I_\r\nsay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for _all_ qualities!\xe2\x80\x9d an eager little man shrilly interposed. \xe2\x80\x9cFor\r\n_richness_ of general tone I don\xe2\x80\x99t say that it _has_ a rival. But for\r\n_delicacy_ of modulation\xe2\x80\x94for what one may call the \xe2\x80\x98_harmonics_\xe2\x80\x99 of\r\nflavour\xe2\x80\x94give _me_ good old _raspberry_-jam!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllow me one word!\xe2\x80\x9d The fat red-faced man, quite hoarse with\r\nexcitement, broke into the dialogue. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too important a question to\r\nbe settled by Amateurs! I can give you the views of a\r\n_Professional_\xe2\x80\x94perhaps the most experienced jam-taster now living. Why,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve known him fix the age of strawberry-jam, to a _day_\xe2\x80\x94and we all know\r\nwhat a difficult jam it is to give a date to\xe2\x80\x94on a single tasting! Well,\r\nI put to him the _very_ question you are discussing. His words were\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_cherry_-jam is best, for mere _chiaroscuro_ of flavour:\r\n_raspberry_-jam lends itself best to those resolved discords that linger\r\nso lovingly on the tongue: but, for rapturous _utterness_ of saccharine\r\nperfection, it\xe2\x80\x99s _apricot-jam first and the rest nowhere_!\xe2\x80\x99 That was\r\nwell put, _wasn\xe2\x80\x99t_ it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConsummately put!\xe2\x80\x9d shrieked the eager little man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know your friend well,\xe2\x80\x9d said the pompous man. \xe2\x80\x9cAs a jam-taster, he\r\nhas no rival! Yet I scarcely think\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut here the discussion became general: and his words were lost in a\r\nconfused medley of names, every guest sounding the praises of his own\r\nfavorite jam. At length, through the din, our host\xe2\x80\x99s voice made itself\r\nheard. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us join the ladies!\xe2\x80\x9d These words seemed to recall me to\r\nwaking life; and I felt sure that, for the last few minutes, I had\r\nrelapsed into the \xe2\x80\x98eerie\xe2\x80\x99 state.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA strange dream!\xe2\x80\x9d I said to myself as we trooped upstairs. \xe2\x80\x9cGrown men\r\ndiscussing, as seriously as if they were matters of life and death, the\r\nhopelessly trivial details of mere _delicacies_, that appeal to no\r\nhigher human function than the nerves of the tongue and palate! What a\r\nhumiliating spectacle such a discussion would be in waking life!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen, on our way to the drawing-room, I received from the housekeeper my\r\nlittle friends, clad in the daintiest of evening costumes, and looking,\r\nin the flush of expectant delight, more radiantly beautiful than I had\r\never seen them before, I felt no shock of surprise, but accepted the\r\nfact with the same unreasoning apathy with which one meets the events of\r\na dream, and was merely conscious of a vague anxiety as to how they\r\nwould acquit themselves in so novel a scene\xe2\x80\x94forgetting that Court-life\r\nin Outland was as good training as they could need for Society in the\r\nmore substantial world.\r\n\r\nIt would be best, I thought, to introduce them as soon as possible to\r\nsome good-natured lady-guest, and I selected the young lady whose\r\npiano-forte-playing had been so much talked of. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure you like\r\nchildren,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cMay I introduce two little friends of mine? This is\r\nSylvie\xe2\x80\x94and this is Bruno.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young lady kissed Sylvie very graciously. She would have done the\r\nsame for _Bruno_, but he hastily drew back out of reach. \xe2\x80\x9cTheir faces\r\nare new to me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you come from, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had not anticipated so inconvenient a question; and, fearing that it\r\nmight embarrass Sylvie, I answered for her. \xe2\x80\x9cThey come from some\r\ndistance. They are only here just for this one evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow far have you come, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d the young lady persisted.\r\n\r\nSylvie looked puzzled. \xe2\x80\x9cA mile or two, I _think_,\xe2\x80\x9d she said doubtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA mile or _three_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say \xe2\x80\x98a mile or _three_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie corrected him.\r\n\r\nThe young lady nodded approval. \xe2\x80\x9cSylvie\xe2\x80\x99s quite right. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t usual to\r\nsay \xe2\x80\x98a mile or _three_.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be usual\xe2\x80\x94if we said it often enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\nIt was the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s turn to look puzzled now. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s very quick, for\r\nhis age!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not more than seven, are you, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nadded aloud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not so many as _that_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m _one_. Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s _one_.\r\nSylvie and me is _two_. _Sylvie_ taught me to count.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t _counting_ you, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d the young lady laughingly\r\nreplied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHasn\xe2\x80\x99t oo _learnt_ to count?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\nThe young lady bit her lip. \xe2\x80\x9cDear! What embarrassing questions he _does_\r\nask!\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a half-audible \xe2\x80\x98aside.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBruno, you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said reprovingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t _what_?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask\xe2\x80\x94that sort of questions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_ sort of questions?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno mischievously persisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _she_ told you not,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie replied, with a shy glance at the\r\nyoung lady, and losing all sense of grammar in her confusion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOo ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t pronounce it!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno triumphantly cried. And he turned to the\r\nyoung lady, for sympathy in his victory. \xe2\x80\x9cI _knewed_ she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\npronounce \xe2\x80\x98umbrella-sting\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young lady thought it best to return to the arithmetical problem.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I asked if you were _seven_, you know, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean \xe2\x80\x98how many\r\n_children_?\xe2\x80\x99 I meant \xe2\x80\x98how many _years_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly got _two_ ears,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody\xe2\x80\x99s got _seven_ ears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you belong to this little girl?\xe2\x80\x9d the young lady continued,\r\nskilfully evading the anatomical problem.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I doosn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to _her_!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cSylvie belongs to _me_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd he clasped his arms round her as he added \xe2\x80\x9cShe are my very mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, do you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young lady, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve a little sister at home,\r\nexactly like _your_ sister? I\xe2\x80\x99m sure they\xe2\x80\x99d love each other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99d be very extremely useful to each other,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said,\r\nthoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want no looking-glasses to brush their\r\nhair wiz.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, my child?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, each one would do for the other one\xe2\x80\x99s looking-glass, a-course!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncried Bruno.\r\n\r\nBut here Lady Muriel, who had been standing by, listening to this\r\nbewildering dialogue, interrupted it to ask if the young lady would\r\nfavour us with some music; and the children followed their new friend to\r\nthe piano.\r\n\r\nArthur came and sat down by me. \xe2\x80\x9cIf rumour speaks truly,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwe are to have a real treat!\xe2\x80\x9d And then, amid a breathless silence, the\r\nperformance began.\r\n\r\nShe was one of those players whom Society talks of as \xe2\x80\x98brilliant,\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\nshe dashed into the loveliest of Haydn\xe2\x80\x99s Symphonies in a style that was\r\nclearly the outcome of years of patient study under the best masters. At\r\nfirst it seemed to be the perfection of piano-forte-playing; but in a\r\nfew minutes I began to ask myself, wearily, \xe2\x80\x9c_What_ is it that is\r\nwanting? _Why_ does one get no pleasure from it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen I set myself to listen intently to every note; and the mystery\r\nexplained itself. There _was_ an almost-perfect mechanical\r\n_correctness_\xe2\x80\x94and there was nothing else! False notes, of course, did\r\nnot occur: she knew the piece too well for _that_; but there was just\r\nenough irregularity of _time_ to betray that the player had no real\r\n\xe2\x80\x98ear\xe2\x80\x99 for music\xe2\x80\x94just enough inarticulateness in the more elaborate\r\npassages to show that she did not think her audience worth taking real\r\npains for\xe2\x80\x94just enough mechanical monotony of accent to take all _soul_\r\nout of the heavenly modulations she was profaning\xe2\x80\x94in short, it was\r\nsimply irritating; and, when she had rattled off the finale and had\r\nstruck the final chord as if, the instrument being now done with, it\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t matter how many wires she broke, I could not even _affect_ to\r\njoin in the stereotyped \xe2\x80\x9cOh, _thank_ you!\xe2\x80\x9d which was chorused around me.\r\n\r\nLady Muriel joined us for a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it _beautiful_?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwhispered, to Arthur, with a mischievous smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. But the gentle sweetness of his face quite\r\nneutralised the apparent rudeness of the reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch execution, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d she persisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what she _deserves_,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur doggedly replied: \xe2\x80\x9cbut people are\r\nso prejudiced against capital\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99re beginning to talk nonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel cried. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you\r\n_do_ like Music, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? You said so just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I like _Music_?\xe2\x80\x9d the Doctor repeated softly to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear\r\nLady Muriel, there is Music and Music. Your question is painfully vague.\r\nYou might as well ask \xe2\x80\x98Do you like _People_?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel bit her lip, frowned, and stamped with one tiny foot. As a\r\ndramatic representation of ill-temper, it was distinctly _not_ a\r\nsuccess. However, it took in _one_ of her audience, and Bruno hastened\r\nto interpose, as peacemaker in a rising quarrel, with the remark \xe2\x80\x9c_I_\r\nlikes Peoples!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur laid a loving hand on the little curly head. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat? _All_\r\nPeoples?\xe2\x80\x9d he enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot _all_ Peoples,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno explained. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly but Sylvie\xe2\x80\x94and Lady\r\nMuriel\xe2\x80\x94and him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (pointing to the Earl) \xe2\x80\x9cand oo\xe2\x80\x94and oo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t point at people,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very rude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s World,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cthere are only _four_ People\xe2\x80\x94worth\r\nmentioning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s World!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel repeated thoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cA bright and\r\nflowery world. Where the grass is always green, where the breezes always\r\nblow softly, and the rain-clouds never gather; where there are no wild\r\nbeasts, and no deserts\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere _must_ be deserts,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur decisively remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least if it\r\nwas _my_ ideal world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what possible use is there in a _desert_?\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Surely_ you would have no wilderness in your ideal world?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cBut indeed I _would_!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cA wilderness would be\r\nmore necessary than a railway; and _far_ more conducive to general\r\nhappiness than church-bells!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what would you use it for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_To practise music in_,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cAll the young ladies, that have\r\nno ear for music, but insist on learning it, should be conveyed, every\r\nmorning, two or three miles into the wilderness. There each would find a\r\ncomfortable room provided for her, and also a cheap second-hand\r\npiano-forte, on which she might play for hours, without adding one\r\nneedless pang to the sum of human misery!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel glanced round in alarm, lest these barbarous sentiments\r\nshould be overheard. But the fair musician was at a safe distance. \xe2\x80\x9cAt\r\nany rate you must allow that she\xe2\x80\x99s a sweet girl?\xe2\x80\x9d she resumed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, certainly. As sweet as _eau sucr\xc3\xa9e_, if you choose\xe2\x80\x94and nearly as\r\ninteresting!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are incorrigible!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, and turned to me. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you\r\nfound Mrs. Mills an interesting companion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _that\xe2\x80\x99s_ her name, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI fancied there was _more_ of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo there is: and it will be \xe2\x80\x98at your proper peril\xe2\x80\x99 (whatever that may\r\nmean) if you ever presume to address her as \xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Mills.\xe2\x80\x99 She is \xe2\x80\x98Mrs.\r\nErnest\xe2\x80\x94Atkinson\xe2\x80\x94Mills\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is one of those would-be grandees,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, \xe2\x80\x9cwho think that,\r\nby tacking on to their surname all their spare Christian-names, with\r\nhyphens between, they can give it an aristocratic flavour. As if it\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t trouble enough to remember _one_ surname!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time the room was getting crowded, as the guests, invited for\r\nthe evening-party, were beginning to arrive, and Lady Muriel had to\r\ndevote herself to the task of welcoming them, which she did with the\r\nsweetest grace imaginable. Sylvie and Bruno stood by her, deeply\r\ninterested in the process.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you like my friends?\xe2\x80\x9d she said to them. \xe2\x80\x9cSpecially my dear old\r\nfriend, Mein Herr (What\xe2\x80\x99s become of him, I wonder? Oh, there he is!),\r\nthat old gentleman in spectacles, with a long beard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a grand old gentleman!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, gazing admiringly at \xe2\x80\x98Mein\r\nHerr,\xe2\x80\x99 who had settled down in a corner, from which his mild eyes beamed\r\non us through a gigantic pair of spectacles. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what a lovely beard!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does he call his-self?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe calls himself \xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie whispered in reply.\r\n\r\nBruno shook his head impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what he calls his _hair_, not\r\nhis _self_, oo silly!\xe2\x80\x9d He appealed to me. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat doos he call his _self_,\r\nMister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the only name _I_ know of,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut he looks very lonely.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you pity his grey hairs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pities his _self_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, still harping on the misnomer; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\r\ndoosn\xe2\x80\x99t pity his _hair_, one bit. His _hair_ ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t feel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe met him this afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99d been to see Nero, and\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99d had _such_ fun with him, making him invisible again! And we saw\r\nthat nice old gentleman as we came back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let\xe2\x80\x99s go and talk to him, and cheer him up a little,\xe2\x80\x9d I said:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand perhaps we shall find out what he calls himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XI.\r\n                          THE MAN IN THE MOON.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe children came willingly. With one of them on each side of me, I\r\napproached the corner occupied by \xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t object to\r\n_children_, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d I began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Crabbed age and youth cannot live together!_\xe2\x80\x9d the old man cheerfully\r\nreplied, with a most genial smile. \xe2\x80\x9cNow take a good look at me, my\r\nchildren! You would guess me to be an _old_ man, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt first sight, though his face had reminded me so mysteriously of \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nProfessor,\xe2\x80\x9d he had seemed to be decidedly a _younger_ man: but, when I\r\ncame to look into the wonderful depth of those large dreamy eyes, I\r\nfelt, with a strange sense of awe, that he was incalculably _older_: he\r\nseemed to gaze at us out of some by-gone age, centuries away.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: MEIN HERR\xe2\x80\x99S FAIRY-FRIENDS]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know if oo\xe2\x80\x99re an _old_ man,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno answered, as the children,\r\nwon over by the gentle voice, crept a little closer to him. \xe2\x80\x9cI thinks\r\noo\xe2\x80\x99re _eighty-three_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is very exact!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he anything like right?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are reasons,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr gently replied, \xe2\x80\x9creasons which I am not\r\nat liberty to explain, for not mentioning _definitely_ any Persons,\r\nPlaces, or Dates. One remark only I will permit myself to make\xe2\x80\x94that the\r\nperiod of life, between the ages of a hundred-and-sixty-five and a\r\nhundred-and-seventy-five, is a specially _safe_ one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you make that out?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThus. You would consider swimming to be a very safe amusement, if you\r\nscarcely ever heard of any one dying of it. Am I not right in thinking\r\nthat you never heard of any one dying between those two ages?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see what you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t prove\r\n_swimming_ to be safe, on the same principle. It is no uncommon thing to\r\nhear of some one being _drowned_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn _my_ country,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr, \xe2\x80\x9cno one is _ever_ drowned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there no water deep enough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlenty! But we ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t _sink_. We are all _lighter than water_. Let me\r\nexplain,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, seeing my look of surprise. \xe2\x80\x9cSuppose you desire a\r\nrace of _pigeons_ of a particular shape or colour, do you not select,\r\nfrom year to year, those that are nearest to the shape or colour you\r\nwant, and keep those, and part with the others?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe do,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied. \xe2\x80\x9cWe call it \xe2\x80\x98Artificial Selection.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, _we_ have practised that for some\r\ncenturies\xe2\x80\x94constantly selecting the _lightest_ people: so that, now,\r\n_everybody_ is lighter than water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you never can be drowned at _sea_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! It is only on the _land_\xe2\x80\x94for instance, when attending a play in\r\na theatre\xe2\x80\x94that we are in such a danger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can that happen at a _theatre_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur theatres are all _underground_. Large tanks of water are placed\r\nabove. If a fire breaks out, the taps are turned, and in one minute the\r\ntheatre is flooded, up to the very roof! Thus the fire is extinguished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_And_ the audience, I presume?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a minor matter,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr carelessly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut they have\r\nthe comfort of knowing that, whether drowned or not, they are all\r\n_lighter than water_. We have not yet reached the standard of making\r\npeople lighter than _air_: but we are _aiming_ at it; and, in another\r\nthousand years or so\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat doos oo do wiz the peoples that\xe2\x80\x99s too heavy?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno solemnly\r\nenquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have applied the same process,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr continued, not noticing\r\nBruno\xe2\x80\x99s question, \xe2\x80\x9cto many other purposes. We have gone on selecting\r\n_walking-sticks_\xe2\x80\x94always keeping those that walked _best_\xe2\x80\x94till we have\r\nobtained some, that can walk by themselves! We have gone on selecting\r\n_cotton-wool_, till we have got some lighter than air! You\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea\r\nwhat a useful material it is! We call it \xe2\x80\x98Imponderal.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you use it for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, chiefly for _packing_ articles, to go by Parcel-Post. It makes\r\nthem weigh _less than nothing_, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how do the Post-Office people know what you have to pay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the beauty of the new system!\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr cried exultingly. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\r\npay _us_: we don\xe2\x80\x99t pay _them_! I\xe2\x80\x99ve often got as much as five shillings\r\nfor sending a parcel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut doesn\xe2\x80\x99t your Government object?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they _do_ object, a little. They say it comes so expensive, in\r\nthe long run. But the thing\xe2\x80\x99s as clear as daylight, by their own rules.\r\nIf I send a parcel, that weighs a pound _more_ than nothing, I _pay_\r\nthree-pence: so, of course, if it weighs a pound _less_ than nothing, I\r\nought to _receive_ three-pence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is _indeed_ a useful article!\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet even \xe2\x80\x98Imponderal\xe2\x80\x99 has its disadvantages,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cI bought\r\nsome, a few days ago, and put it into my _hat_, to carry it home, and\r\nthe hat simply floated away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad oo some of that funny stuff in oor hat _today_?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno enquired.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSylvie and me saw oo in the road, and oor hat were ever so high up!\r\nWeren\xe2\x80\x99t it, Sylvie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that was quite another thing.\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a drop or\r\ntwo of rain falling: so I put my hat on the top of my stick\xe2\x80\x94as an\r\numbrella, you know. As I came along the road,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, turning to\r\nme, \xe2\x80\x9cI was overtaken by\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94a shower of rain?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it _looked_ more like the tail of a dog,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwas the most curious thing! Something rubbed affectionately against my\r\nknee. And I looked down. And I could see _nothing_! Only, about a yard\r\noff, there was a dog\xe2\x80\x99s tail, wagging, all by itself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _Sylvie_!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno murmured reproachfully. \xe2\x80\x9cOo didn\xe2\x80\x99t finish making\r\nhim visible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m _so_ sorry!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, looking very penitent. \xe2\x80\x9cI meant to rub it\r\nalong his back, but we were in such a hurry. We\xe2\x80\x99ll go and finish him\r\ntomorrow. Poor thing! Perhaps he\xe2\x80\x99ll get no supper tonight!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Course_ he won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody never gives bones to a dog\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntail!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMein Herr looked from one to the other in blank astonishment. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not\r\nunderstand you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI had lost my way, and I was consulting a\r\npocket-map, and somehow I had dropped one of my gloves, and this\r\ninvisible _Something_, that had rubbed against my knee, actually brought\r\nit back to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse he did!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s _welly_ fond of fetching things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMein Herr looked so thoroughly bewildered that I thought it best to\r\nchange the subject. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a useful thing a pocket-map is!\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s another thing we\xe2\x80\x99ve learned from _your_ Nation,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmap-making. But we\xe2\x80\x99ve carried it much further than _you_. What do you\r\nconsider the _largest_ map that would be really useful?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout six inches to the mile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly _six inches_!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9cWe very soon got to six\r\n_yards_ to the mile. Then we tried a _hundred_ yards to the mile. And\r\nthen came the grandest idea of all! We actually made a map of the\r\ncountry, on the scale of _a mile to the mile_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you used it much?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has never been spread out, yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr: \xe2\x80\x9cthe farmers\r\nobjected: they said it would cover the whole country, and shut out the\r\nsunlight! So we now use the country itself, as its own map, and I assure\r\nyou it does nearly as well. Now let me ask you _another_ question. What\r\nis the smallest _world_ you would care to inhabit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ know!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno, who was listening intently. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d like a little\r\nteeny-tiny world, just big enough for Sylvie and me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you would have to stand on opposite sides of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so you would never see your sister _at all_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99d have no _lessons_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say you\xe2\x80\x99ve been trying experiments in _that_\r\ndirection!\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not _experiments_ exactly. We do not profess to _construct_\r\nplanets. But a scientific friend of mine, who has made several\r\nballoon-voyages, assures me he has visited a planet so small that he\r\ncould walk right round it in twenty minutes! There had been a great\r\nbattle, just before his visit, which had ended rather oddly: the\r\nvanquished army ran away at full speed, and in a very few minutes found\r\nthemselves face-to-face with the victorious army, who were marching home\r\nagain, and who were so frightened at finding themselves between _two_\r\narmies, that they surrendered at once! Of course that lost them the\r\nbattle, though, as a matter of fact, they had killed _all_ the soldiers\r\non the other side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKilled soldiers _ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_ run away,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno thoughtfully remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Killed\xe2\x80\x99 is a technical word,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9cIn the little planet\r\nI speak of, the bullets were made of soft black stuff, which marked\r\neverything it touched. So, after a battle, all you had to do was to\r\ncount how many soldiers on each side were \xe2\x80\x98killed\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94that means \xe2\x80\x98marked on\r\nthe _back_,\xe2\x80\x99 for marks in _front_ didn\xe2\x80\x99t count.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x98kill\xe2\x80\x99 any, unless they ran away?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy scientific friend found out a better plan than _that_. He pointed\r\nout that, if only the bullets were sent _the other way round the world_,\r\nthey would hit the enemy in the _back_. After that, the _worst_ marksmen\r\nwere considered the _best_ soldiers; and _the very worst of all_ always\r\ngot First Prize.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how did you decide which was _the very worst of all_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEasily. The _best_ possible shooting is, you know, to hit what is\r\nexactly in _front_ of you: so of course the _worst_ possible is to hit\r\nwhat is exactly _behind_ you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were strange people in that little planet!\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were indeed! Perhaps their method of _government_ was the\r\nstrangest of all. In _this_ planet, I am told, a Nation consists of a\r\nnumber of Subjects, and one King: but, in the little planet I speak of,\r\nit consisted of a number of _Kings_, and one _Subject_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say you are \xe2\x80\x98told\xe2\x80\x99 what happens in _this_ planet,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cMay I\r\nventure to guess that you yourself are a visitor from some _other_\r\nplanet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno clapped his hands in his excitement. \xe2\x80\x9cIs oo the Man-in-the-Moon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe cried.\r\n\r\nMein Herr looked uneasy. \xe2\x80\x9cI am _not_ in the Moon, my child,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nevasively. \xe2\x80\x9cTo return to what I was saying. I think _that_ method of\r\ngovernment ought to answer _well_. You see, the Kings would be sure to\r\nmake Laws contradicting each other: so the Subject could never be\r\npunished, because, _whatever_ he did, he\xe2\x80\x99d be obeying _some_ Law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, whatever he did, he\xe2\x80\x99d be _dis_obeying _some_ Law!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he\xe2\x80\x99d _always_ be punished!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel was passing at the moment, and caught the last word.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody\xe2\x80\x99s going to be punished _here_!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, taking Bruno in her\r\narms. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is Liberty-Hall! Would you lend me the children for a\r\nminute?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe children desert us, you see,\xe2\x80\x9d I said to Mein Herr, as she carried\r\nthem off: \xe2\x80\x9cso we old folk must keep each other company!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, well! We\xe2\x80\x99re old folk _now_; and yet I was a\r\nchild myself, once\xe2\x80\x94at least I fancy so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt _did_ seem a rather unlikely fancy, I could not help owning to\r\nmyself\xe2\x80\x94looking at the shaggy white hair, and the long beard\xe2\x80\x94that he\r\ncould _ever_ have been a child. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are fond of young people?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYoung _men_,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cNot of _children_ exactly. I used to teach\r\nyoung men\xe2\x80\x94many a year ago\xe2\x80\x94in my dear old University!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite catch its _name_?\xe2\x80\x9d I hinted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not name it,\xe2\x80\x9d the old man replied mildly. \xe2\x80\x9cNor would you know the\r\nname if I did. Strange tales I could tell you of all the changes I have\r\nwitnessed there! But it would weary you, I fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, _indeed_!\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cPray go on. What kind of changes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the old man seemed to be more in a humour for questions than for\r\nanswers. \xe2\x80\x9cTell me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, laying his hand impressively on my arm,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctell me something. For I am a stranger in your land, and I know little\r\nof _your_ modes of education: yet something tells me _we_ are further on\r\nthan _you_ in the eternal cycle of change\xe2\x80\x94and that many a theory _we_\r\nhave tried and found to fail, _you_ also will try, with a wilder\r\nenthusiasm: you also will find to fail, with a bitterer despair!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was strange to see how, as he talked, and his words flowed more and\r\nmore freely, with a certain rhythmic eloquence, his features seemed to\r\nglow with an inner light, and the whole man seemed to be transformed, as\r\nif he had grown fifty years younger in a moment of time.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XII.\r\n                              FAIRY-MUSIC.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe silence that ensued was broken by the voice of the musical young\r\nlady, who had seated herself near us, and was conversing with one of the\r\nnewly-arrived guests. \xe2\x80\x9cWell!\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a tone of scornful surprise.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe _are_ to have something new in the way of music, it appears!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked round for an explanation, and was nearly as much astonished as\r\nthe speaker herself: it was _Sylvie_ whom Lady Muriel was leading to the\r\npiano!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo try it, my darling!\xe2\x80\x9d she was saying. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure you can play very\r\nnicely!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie looked round at me, with tears in her eyes. I tried to give her\r\nan encouraging smile, but it was evidently a great strain on the nerves\r\nof a child so wholly unused to be made an exhibition of, and she was\r\nfrightened and unhappy. Yet here came out the perfect sweetness of her\r\ndisposition: I could see that she was resolved to forget herself, and do\r\nher best to give pleasure to Lady Muriel and her friends. She seated\r\nherself at the instrument, and began instantly. Time and expression, so\r\nfar as one could judge, were perfect: but her touch was one of such\r\nextraordinary lightness that it was at first scarcely possible, through\r\nthe hum of conversation which still continued, to catch a note of what\r\nshe was playing.\r\n\r\nBut in a minute the hum had died away into absolute silence, and we all\r\nsat, entranced and breathless, to listen to such heavenly music as none\r\nthen present could ever forget.\r\n\r\nHardly touching the notes at first, she played a sort of introduction in\r\na minor key\xe2\x80\x94like an embodied twilight; one felt as though the lights\r\nwere growing dim, and a mist were creeping through the room. Then there\r\nflashed through the gathering gloom the first few notes of a melody so\r\nlovely, so delicate, that one held one\xe2\x80\x99s breath, fearful to lose a\r\nsingle note of it. Ever and again the music dropped into the pathetic\r\nminor key with which it had begun, and, each time that the melody forced\r\nits way, so to speak, through the enshrouding gloom into the light of\r\nday, it was more entrancing, more magically sweet. Under the airy touch\r\nof the child, the instrument actually seemed to _warble_, like a bird.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Rise up, my love, my fair one_,\xe2\x80\x9d it seemed to sing, \xe2\x80\x9c_and come away!\r\nFor lo, the winter is past, the rain is over and gone; the flowers\r\nappear on the earth; the time of the singing of birds is come!_\xe2\x80\x9d One\r\ncould fancy one heard the tinkle of the last few drops, shaken from the\r\ntrees by a passing gust\xe2\x80\x94that one saw the first glittering rays of the\r\nsun, breaking through the clouds.\r\n\r\nThe Count hurried across the room in great excitement. \xe2\x80\x9cI _cannot_\r\nremember myself,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cof the name of this so charming an air!\r\nIt is of an opera, most surely. Yet not even will the _opera_ remind his\r\nname to me! What you call him, dear child?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98HOW CALL YOU THE OPERA?\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\nSylvie looked round at him with a rapt expression of face.
