She had\r\nceased playing, but her fingers still wandered fitfully over the keys.\r\nAll fear and shyness had quite passed away now, and nothing remained but\r\nthe pure joy of the music that had thrilled our hearts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe title of it!\xe2\x80\x9d the Count repeated impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cHow call you the\r\nopera?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what an opera _is_,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie half-whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow, then, call you the _air_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know any name for it,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie replied, as she rose from the\r\ninstrument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is marvellous!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Count, following the child, and\r\naddressing himself to me, as if I were the proprietor of this musical\r\nprodigy, and so _must_ know the origin of her music. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have heard her\r\nplay this, sooner\xe2\x80\x94I would say \xe2\x80\x98before this occasion\xe2\x80\x99? How call you the\r\nair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head; but was saved from more questions by Lady Muriel, who\r\ncame up to petition the Count for a song.\r\n\r\nThe Count spread out his hands apologetically, and ducked his head.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Milady, I have already respected\xe2\x80\x94I would say prospected\xe2\x80\x94all your\r\nsongs; and there shall be none fitted to my voice! They are not for\r\nbasso voices!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t you look at them again?\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel implored.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s help him!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno whispered to Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get him\xe2\x80\x94_you_ know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cShall _we_ look for a song for you?\xe2\x80\x9d she said sweetly to\r\nthe Count.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMais _oui_!\xe2\x80\x9d the little man exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course we may!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, while, each taking a hand of the\r\ndelighted Count, they led him to the music-stand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is still hope!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel over her shoulder, as she\r\nfollowed them.\r\n\r\nI turned to \xe2\x80\x98Mein Herr,\xe2\x80\x99 hoping to resume our interrupted conversation.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were remarking\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I began: but at this moment Sylvie came to call\r\nBruno, who had returned to my side, looking unusually serious. \xe2\x80\x9c_Do_\r\ncome, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d she entreated. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know we\xe2\x80\x99ve nearly found it!\xe2\x80\x9d Then, in\r\na whisper, \xe2\x80\x9cThe locket\xe2\x80\x99s in my _hand_, now. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get it out while\r\nthey were looking!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Bruno drew back. \xe2\x80\x9cThe man called me names,\xe2\x80\x9d he said with dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat names?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired with some curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI asked him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich sort of song he liked. And he said\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_A_ song of _a_ man, not of _a_ lady.\xe2\x80\x99 And I said \xe2\x80\x98Shall Sylvie and me\r\nfind you the song of Mister Tottles?\xe2\x80\x99 And he said \xe2\x80\x98Wait, eel!\xe2\x80\x99 And I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n_not_ an eel, oo know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m _sure_ he didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean it!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s something\r\nFrench\xe2\x80\x94you know he ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t talk English so well as\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno relented visibly. \xe2\x80\x9cCourse he knows no better, if he\xe2\x80\x99s Flench!\r\nFlenchmen _never_ can speak English so goodly as _us_!\xe2\x80\x9d And Sylvie led\r\nhim away, a willing captive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNice children!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, taking off his spectacles and rubbing\r\nthem carefully. Then he put them on again, and watched with an approving\r\nsmile, while the children tossed over the heap of music, and we just\r\ncaught Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s reproving words, \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re _not_ making hay, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis has been a long interruption to our conversation,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cPray\r\nlet us go on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWillingly!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the gentle old man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was much interested in what you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He paused a moment, and passed his\r\nhand uneasily across his brow. \xe2\x80\x9cOne forgets,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat was I\r\nsaying? Oh! Something you were to tell me. Yes. Which of your teachers\r\ndo you value the most highly, those whose words are easily understood,\r\nor those who puzzle you at every turn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI felt obliged to admit that we generally admired most the teachers we\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t quite understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the way it begins. Well, _we_ were at\r\nthat stage some eighty years ago\xe2\x80\x94or was it ninety? Our favourite teacher\r\ngot more obscure every year; and every year we admired him more\xe2\x80\x94just as\r\n_your_ Art-fanciers call _mist_ the fairest feature in a landscape, and\r\nadmire a view with frantic delight when they can see nothing! Now I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ntell you how it ended. It was Moral Philosophy that our idol lectured\r\non. Well, his pupils couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make head or tail of it, but they got it\r\nall by heart; and, when Examination-time came, they wrote it down; and\r\nthe Examiners said \xe2\x80\x98Beautiful! What depth!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what good was it to the young men _afterwards_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mein Herr. \xe2\x80\x9c_They_ became teachers in\r\ntheir turn, and _they_ said all these things over again; and _their_\r\npupils wrote it all down; and the Examiners accepted it; and nobody had\r\nthe ghost of an idea what it all meant!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how did it end?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ended this way. We woke up one fine day, and found there was no one\r\nin the place that knew _anything_ about Moral Philosophy. So we\r\nabolished it, teachers, classes, examiners, and all. And if any one\r\nwanted to learn anything about it, he had to make it out for himself;\r\nand after another twenty years or so there were several men that really\r\nknew something about it! Now tell me another thing. How long do you\r\nteach a youth before you examine him, in your Universities?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI told him, three or four years.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so, just what _we_ did!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe taught \xe2\x80\x99em a bit, and,\r\njust as they were beginning to take it in, we took it all out again! We\r\npumped our wells dry before they were a quarter full\xe2\x80\x94we stripped our\r\norchards while the apples were still in blossom\xe2\x80\x94we applied the severe\r\nlogic of arithmetic to our chickens, while peacefully slumbering in\r\ntheir shells! Doubtless it\xe2\x80\x99s the early bird that picks up the worm\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nif the bird gets up so outrageously early that the worm is still deep\r\nunderground, what _then_ is its chance of a breakfast?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot much, I admitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow see how that works!\xe2\x80\x9d he went on eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to pump your\r\nwells so soon\xe2\x80\x94and I suppose you tell me that is what you _must_ do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cIn an over-crowded country like this, nothing but\r\nCompetitive Examinations\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMein Herr threw up his hands wildly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat, _again_?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthought it was dead, fifty years ago! Oh this Upas tree of Competitive\r\nExaminations! Beneath whose deadly shade all the original genius, all\r\nthe exhaustive research, all the untiring life-long diligence by which\r\nour fore-fathers have so advanced human knowledge, must slowly but\r\nsurely wither away, and give place to a system of Cookery, in which the\r\nhuman mind is a sausage, and all we ask is, how much indigestible stuff\r\ncan be crammed into it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlways, after these bursts of eloquence, he seemed to forget himself for\r\na moment, and only to hold on to the thread of thought by some single\r\nword. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, _crammed_,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cWe went through all that stage of\r\nthe disease\xe2\x80\x94had it bad, I warrant you! Of course, as the Examination was\r\nall in all, we tried to put in just what was wanted\xe2\x80\x94and the _great_\r\nthing to aim at was, that the Candidate should know absolutely _nothing_\r\nbeyond the needs of the Examination! I don\xe2\x80\x99t say it was ever _quite_\r\nachieved: but one of my own pupils (pardon an old man\xe2\x80\x99s egotism) came\r\nvery near it. After the Examination, he mentioned to me the few facts\r\nwhich he knew but had _not_ been able to bring in, and I can assure you\r\nthey were trivial, Sir, absolutely trivial!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI feebly expressed my surprise and delight.\r\n\r\nThe old man bowed, with a gratified smile, and proceeded. \xe2\x80\x9cAt that time,\r\nno one had hit on the much more rational plan of watching for the\r\nindividual scintillations of genius, and rewarding them as they\r\noccurred. As it was, we made our unfortunate pupil into a Leyden-jar,\r\ncharged him up to the eyelids\xe2\x80\x94then applied the knob of a Competitive\r\nExamination, and drew off one magnificent spark, which very often\r\ncracked the jar! What mattered _that_? We labeled it \xe2\x80\x98First Class\r\nSpark,\xe2\x80\x99 and put it away on the shelf.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the more rational system\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes! _that_ came next. Instead of giving the whole reward of\r\nlearning in one lump, we used to pay for every good answer as it\r\noccurred. How well I remember lecturing in those days, with a heap of\r\nsmall coins at my elbow! It was \xe2\x80\x98A _very_ good answer, Mr. Jones!\xe2\x80\x99 (that\r\nmeant a shilling, mostly). \xe2\x80\x98Bravo, Mr. Robinson!\xe2\x80\x99 (that meant\r\nhalf-a-crown). Now I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you how _that_ worked. Not one single fact\r\nwould any of them take in, without a fee! And when a clever boy came up\r\nfrom school, he got paid more for learning than we got paid for teaching\r\nhim! Then came the wildest craze of all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, _another_ craze?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the last one,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cI must have tired you out with\r\nmy long story. Each College wanted to get the clever boys: so we adopted\r\na system which we had heard was very popular in England: the Colleges\r\ncompeted against each other, and the boys let themselves out to the\r\nhighest bidder! What geese we were! Why, they were bound to come to the\r\nUniversity _somehow_. We needn\xe2\x80\x99t have paid \xe2\x80\x99em! And all our money went\r\nin getting clever boys to come to one College rather than another! The\r\ncompetition was so keen, that at last mere money-payments were not\r\nenough. Any College, that wished to secure some specially clever young\r\nman, had to waylay him at the Station, and hunt him through the streets.\r\nThe first who touched him was allowed to have him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat hunting-down of the scholars, as they arrived, must have been a\r\ncurious business,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cCould you give me some idea of what it was\r\nlike?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWillingly!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man. \xe2\x80\x9cI will describe to you the very last\r\nHunt that took place, before that form of Sport (for it was actually\r\nreckoned among the _Sports_ of the day: we called it \xe2\x80\x98Cub-Hunting\xe2\x80\x99) was\r\nfinally abandoned. I witnessed it myself, as I happened to be passing by\r\nat the moment, and was what we called \xe2\x80\x98in at the death.\xe2\x80\x99 I can see it\r\nnow!\xe2\x80\x9d he went on in an excited tone, gazing into vacancy with those\r\nlarge dreamy eyes of his. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems like yesterday; and yet it\r\nhappened\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He checked himself hastily, and the remaining words died\r\naway into a whisper.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: SCHOLAR-HUNTING: THE PURSUED]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_How_ many years ago did you say?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, much interested in the\r\nprospect of at last learning _some_ definite fact in his history.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: SCHOLAR-HUNTING: THE PURSUERS]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Many_ years ago,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cThe scene at the Railway-Station had\r\nbeen (so they told me) one of wild excitement. Eight or nine Heads of\r\nColleges had assembled at the gates (no one was allowed inside), and the\r\nStation-Master had drawn a line on the pavement, and insisted on their\r\nall standing behind it. The gates were flung open! The young man darted\r\nthrough them, and fled like lightning down the street, while the Heads\r\nof Colleges actually _yelled_ with excitement on catching sight of him!\r\nThe Proctor gave the word, in the old statutory form, \xe2\x80\x98_Semel!_ _Bis!_\r\n_Ter!_ _Currite!_\xe2\x80\x99, and the Hunt began! Oh, it was a fine sight, believe\r\nme! At the first corner he dropped his Greek Lexicon: further on, his\r\nrailway-rug: then various small articles: then his umbrella: lastly,\r\nwhat I suppose he prized most, his hand-bag: but the game was up: the\r\nspherical Principal of\xe2\x80\x94of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf _which_ College?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94of _one_ of the Colleges,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed, \xe2\x80\x9chad put into operation the\r\nTheory\xe2\x80\x94his own discovery\xe2\x80\x94of Accelerated Velocity, and captured him just\r\nopposite to where I stood. I shall never forget that wild breathless\r\nstruggle! But it was soon over. Once in those great bony hands, escape\r\nwas impossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I ask why you speak of him as the \xe2\x80\x98_spherical_\xe2\x80\x99 Principal?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe epithet referred to his _shape_, which was a perfect _sphere_. You\r\nare aware that a bullet, another instance of a perfect sphere, when\r\nfalling in a perfectly straight line, moves with Accelerated Velocity?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI bowed assent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my spherical friend (as I am proud to call him) set himself to\r\ninvestigate the _causes_ of this. He found them to be _three_. One; that\r\nit is a perfect _sphere_. Two; that it moves in a _straight line_.\r\nThree; that its direction is _not upwards_. When these three conditions\r\nare fulfilled, you get Accelerated Velocity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHardly,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cif you will excuse my differing from you. Suppose we\r\napply the theory to _horizontal_ motion. If a bullet is fired\r\n_horizontally_, it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94it does _not_ move in a _straight line_,\xe2\x80\x9d he quietly finished my\r\nsentence for me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI yield the point,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did your friend do next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe next thing was to apply the theory, as you rightly suggest, to\r\n_horizontal_ motion. But the moving body, ever tending to _fall_, needs\r\n_constant support_, if it is to move in a true horizontal line. \xe2\x80\x98What,\r\nthen,\xe2\x80\x99 he asked himself, \xe2\x80\x98will _give constant support to a moving\r\nbody_?\xe2\x80\x99 And his answer was \xe2\x80\x98_Human legs!_\xe2\x80\x99 _That_ was the discovery that\r\nimmortalised his name!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis name being\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had not mentioned it,\xe2\x80\x9d was the gentle reply of my most unsatisfactory\r\ninformant. \xe2\x80\x9cHis next step was an obvious one. He took to a diet of\r\nsuet-dumplings, until his body had become a perfect sphere. _Then_ he\r\nwent out for his first experimental run\xe2\x80\x94which nearly cost him his life!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow was _that_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see, he had no idea of the _tremendous_ new Force in Nature\r\nthat he was calling into play. He began too fast. In a very few minutes\r\nhe found himself moving at a hundred miles an hour! And, if he had not\r\nhad the presence of mind to charge into the middle of a haystack (which\r\nhe scattered to the four winds) there can be no doubt that he would have\r\nleft the Planet he belonged to, and gone right away into Space!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how came that to be the _last_ of the Cub-Hunts?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see, it led to a rather scandalous dispute between two of the\r\nColleges. _Another_ Principal had laid his hand on the young man, so\r\nnearly at the same moment as the _spherical_ one, that there was no\r\nknowing which had touched him first. The dispute got into print, and did\r\nus no credit, and, in short, Cub-Hunts came to an end. Now I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you\r\nwhat cured us of that wild craze of ours, the bidding against each\r\nother, for the clever scholars, just as if they were articles to be sold\r\nby auction! Just when the craze had reached its highest point, and when\r\none of the Colleges had actually advertised a Scholarship of one\r\nthousand pounds _per annum_, one of our tourists brought us the\r\nmanuscript of an old African legend\xe2\x80\x94I happen to have a copy of it in my\r\npocket. Shall I translate it for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray go on,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, though I felt I was getting _very_ sleepy.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER XIII.\r\n                          WHAT TOTTLES MEANT.\r\n\r\n\r\nMein Herr unrolled the manuscript, but, to my great surprise, instead of\r\n_reading_ it, he began to _sing_ it, in a rich mellow voice that seemed\r\nto ring through the room.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cOne thousand pounds per annuum\r\n  Is not so bad a figure, come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  Cried Tottles. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I tell you, flat,\r\n  A man may marry well on that!\r\n  To say \xe2\x80\x98the Husband needs the Wife\xe2\x80\x99\r\n  Is not the way to represent it.\r\n  The crowning joy of Woman\xe2\x80\x99s life\r\n  Is _Man_!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\n  The blissful Honey-moon is past:\r\n  The Pair have settled down at last:\r\n  Mamma-in-law their home will share,\r\n  And make their happiness her care.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cYour income is an ample one;\r\n  Go it, my children!\xe2\x80\x9d (And they went it).\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cI rayther think this kind of fun\r\n  Won\xe2\x80\x99t last!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\n  They took a little country-box\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  A box at Covent Garden also:\r\n  They lived a life of double-knocks,\r\n  Acquaintances began to call so:\r\n  Their London house was much the same\r\n  (It took three hundred, clear, to rent it):\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cLife is a very jolly game!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  Cried happy Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98Contented with a frugal lot\xe2\x80\x99\r\n  (He always used that phrase at Gunter\xe2\x80\x99s),\r\n  He bought a handy little yacht\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  A dozen serviceable hunters\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  The fishing of a Highland Loch\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  A sailing-boat to circumvent it\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cThe sounding of that Gaelic \xe2\x80\x98och\xe2\x80\x99\r\n  Beats _me_!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\nHere, with one of those convulsive starts that wake one up in the very\r\nact of dropping off to sleep, I became conscious that the deep musical\r\ntones that thrilled me did _not_ belong to Mein Herr, but to the French\r\nCount. The old man was still conning the manuscript.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _beg_ your pardon for keeping you waiting!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was just\r\nmaking sure that I knew the English for all the words. I am quite ready\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d And he read me the following Legend:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a city that stands in the very centre of Africa, and is rarely\r\nvisited by the casual tourist, the people had always bought eggs\xe2\x80\x94a daily\r\nnecessary in a climate where egg-flip was the usual diet\xe2\x80\x94from a Merchant\r\nwho came to their gates once a week. And the people always bid wildly\r\nagainst each other: so there was quite a lively auction every time the\r\nMerchant came, and the last egg in his basket used to fetch the value of\r\ntwo or three camels, or thereabouts. And eggs got dearer every week. And\r\nstill they drank their egg-flip, and wondered where all their money went\r\nto.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: THE EGG-MERCHANT]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there came a day when they put their heads together. And they\r\nunderstood what donkeys they had been.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd next day, when the Merchant came, only _one_ Man went forth. And he\r\nsaid \xe2\x80\x98Oh, thou of the hook-nose and the goggle-eyes, thou of the\r\nmeasureless beard, how much for that lot of eggs?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Merchant answered him \xe2\x80\x98I _could_ let thee have that lot at ten\r\nthousand piastres the dozen.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Man chuckled inwardly, and said \xe2\x80\x98_Ten_ piastres the dozen I\r\noffer thee, and no more, oh descendant of a distinguished grandfather!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Merchant stroked his beard, and said \xe2\x80\x98Hum! I will await the\r\ncoming of thy friends,\xe2\x80\x99 So he waited. And the Man waited with him. And\r\nthey waited both together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe manuscript breaks off here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mein Herr, as he rolled it up\r\nagain; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it was enough to open our eyes. We saw what simpletons we\r\nhad been\xe2\x80\x94buying our Scholars much as those ignorant savages bought their\r\neggs\xe2\x80\x94and the ruinous system was abandoned. If only we could have\r\nabandoned, along with it, all the _other_ fashions we had borrowed from\r\nyou, instead of carrying them to their logical results! But it was not\r\nto be. What ruined my country, and drove me from my home, was the\r\nintroduction\xe2\x80\x94into the _Army_, of all places\xe2\x80\x94of your theory of Political\r\nDichotomy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I trouble you too much,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cif I ask you to explain what\r\nyou mean by \xe2\x80\x98the Theory of Political Dichotomy\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo trouble at all!\xe2\x80\x9d was Mein Herr\xe2\x80\x99s most courteous reply. \xe2\x80\x9cI quite\r\nenjoy talking, when I get so good a listener. What started the thing,\r\nwith us, was the report brought to us, by one of our most eminent\r\nstatesmen, who had stayed some time in England, of the way affairs were\r\nmanaged there. It was a political necessity (so he assured us, and we\r\nbelieved him, though we had never discovered it till that moment) that\r\nthere should be _two_ Parties, in every affair and on every subject. In\r\n_Politics_, the two Parties, which you had found it necessary to\r\ninstitute, were called, he told us, \xe2\x80\x98Whigs\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Tories\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat must have been some time ago?\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _was_ some time ago,\xe2\x80\x9d he admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd this was the way the affairs\r\nof the British Nation were managed. (You will correct me if I\r\nmisrepresent it. I do but repeat what our traveler told us.) These two\r\nParties\xe2\x80\x94which were in chronic hostility to each other\xe2\x80\x94took turns in\r\nconducting the Government; and the Party, that happened _not_ to be in\r\npower, was called the \xe2\x80\x98Opposition\xe2\x80\x99, I believe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the right name,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere have always been, so long as we\r\nhave had a Parliament at all, _two_ Parties, one \xe2\x80\x98in\xe2\x80\x99, and one \xe2\x80\x98out\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the function of the \xe2\x80\x98Ins\xe2\x80\x99 (if I may so call them) was to do the\r\nbest they could for the national welfare\xe2\x80\x94in such things as making war or\r\npeace, commercial treaties, and so forth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the function of the \xe2\x80\x98Outs\xe2\x80\x99 was (so our traveller assured us, though\r\nwe were very incredulous at first) to _prevent_ the \xe2\x80\x98Ins\xe2\x80\x99 from\r\nsucceeding in any of these things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo _criticize_ and to _amend_ their proceedings,\xe2\x80\x9d I corrected him. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwould be _unpatriotic_ to _hinder_ the Government in doing what was for\r\nthe good of the Nation! We have always held a _Patriot_ to be the\r\ngreatest of heroes, and an _unpatriotic_ spirit to be one of the worst\r\nof human ills!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me for a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d the old gentleman courteously replied, taking\r\nout his pocket-book. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a few memoranda here, of a correspondence I\r\nhad with our tourist, and, if you will allow me, I\xe2\x80\x99ll just refresh my\r\nmemory\xe2\x80\x94although I quite agree with you\xe2\x80\x94it is, as you say, one of the\r\nworst of human ills\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d And, here Mein Herr began singing again:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  But oh, the worst of human ills\r\n  (Poor Tottles found) are \xe2\x80\x98little bills\xe2\x80\x99!\r\n  And, with no balance in the Bank,\r\n  What wonder that his spirits sank?\r\n  Still, as the money flowed away,\r\n  He wondered how on earth she spent it.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cYou cost me twenty pounds a day,\r\n  _At least_!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\n  She sighed. \xe2\x80\x9cThose Drawing Rooms, you know!\r\n  I really never thought about it:\r\n  Mamma declared we ought to go\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  We should be Nobodies without it.\r\n  That diamond-circlet for my brow\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  I quite believed that _she_ had sent it,\r\n  Until the Bill came in just now\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c_Viper_!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\n  Poor Mrs. T. could bear no more,\r\n  But fainted flat upon the floor.\r\n  Mamma-in-law, with anguish wild,\r\n  Seeks, all in vain, to rouse her child.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cQuick! Take this box of smelling-salts!\r\n  Don\xe2\x80\x99t scold her, James, or you\xe2\x80\x99ll repent it,\r\n  She\xe2\x80\x99s a _dear_ girl, with all her faults\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cShe _is_!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cI was a donkey,\xe2\x80\x9d Tottles cried,\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cTo choose your daughter for my bride!\r\n  \xe2\x80\x99Twas _you_ that bid us cut a dash!\r\n  \xe2\x80\x99Tis _you_ have brought us to this smash!\r\n  You don\xe2\x80\x99t suggest one single thing\r\n  That can in any way prevent it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Then what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of arguing?\r\n  _Shut up!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\nOnce more I started into wakefulness, and realised that Mein Herr was\r\nnot the singer. He was still consulting his memoranda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is exactly what my friend told me,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed, after conning over\r\nvarious papers. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_Unpatriotic_\xe2\x80\x99 is the very word I had used, in writing\r\nto him, and \xe2\x80\x98_hinder_\xe2\x80\x99 is the very word he used in his reply! Allow me\r\nto read you a portion of his letter:\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_I can assure you_,\xe2\x80\x99 he writes, \xe2\x80\x98_that, unpatriotic as you may think\r\n  it, the recognised function of the \xe2\x80\x98Opposition\xe2\x80\x99 is to hinder, in every\r\n  manner not forbidden by the Law, the action of the Government. This\r\n  process is called \xe2\x80\x98Legitimate Obstruction\xe2\x80\x99: and the greatest triumph\r\n  the \xe2\x80\x98Opposition\xe2\x80\x99 can ever enjoy, is when they are able to point out\r\n  that, owing to their \xe2\x80\x98Obstruction\xe2\x80\x99, the Government have failed in\r\n  everything they have tried to do for the good of the Nation!_\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour friend has not put it _quite_ correctly,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Opposition\r\nwould no doubt be glad to point out that the Government had failed\r\n_through their own fault_; but _not_ that they had failed on account of\r\n_Obstruction_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think so?\xe2\x80\x9d he gently replied. \xe2\x80\x9cAllow me now to read to you this\r\nnewspaper-cutting, which my friend enclosed in his letter. It is part of\r\nthe report of a public speech, made by a Statesman who was at the time a\r\nmember of the \xe2\x80\x98Opposition\xe2\x80\x99:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_At the close of the Session, he thought they had no reason to be\r\n  discontented with the fortunes of the campaign. They had routed the\r\n  enemy at every point. But the pursuit must be continued. They had only\r\n  to follow up a disordered and dispirited foe._\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow to what portion of your national history would you guess that the\r\nspeaker was referring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, the number of _successful_ wars we have waged during the last\r\ncentury,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, with a glow of British pride, \xe2\x80\x9cis _far_ too great\r\nfor me to guess, with any chance of success, _which_ it was we were then\r\nengaged in. However, I will name \xe2\x80\x98_India_\xe2\x80\x99 as the most probable. The\r\nMutiny was no doubt, all but crushed, at the time that speech was made.\r\nWhat a fine, manly, _patriotic_ speech it must have been!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed\r\nin an outburst of enthusiasm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think so?\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, in a tone of gentle pity. \xe2\x80\x9cYet my friend\r\ntells me that the \xe2\x80\x98_disordered and dispirited foe_\xe2\x80\x99 simply meant the\r\nStatesmen who happened to be in power at the moment; that the\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_pursuit_\xe2\x80\x99 simply meant \xe2\x80\x98Obstruction\xe2\x80\x99; and that the words \xe2\x80\x98_they had\r\nrouted the enemy_\xe2\x80\x99 simply meant that the \xe2\x80\x98Opposition\xe2\x80\x99 had succeeded in\r\nhindering the Government from doing any of the work which the Nation had\r\nempowered them to do!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought it best to say nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seemed queer to _us_, just at first,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed, after courteously\r\nwaiting a minute for me to speak: \xe2\x80\x9cbut, when once we had mastered the\r\nidea, our respect for your Nation was so great that we carried it into\r\nevery department of life! It was \xe2\x80\x98_the beginning of the end_\xe2\x80\x99 with us.\r\nMy country never held up its head again!\xe2\x80\x9d And the poor old gentleman\r\nsighed deeply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us change the subject,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cDo not distress yourself, I beg!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with an effort to recover himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI had rather\r\nfinish my story! The next step (after reducing our Government to\r\nimpotence, and putting a stop to all useful legislation, which did not\r\ntake us long to do) was to introduce what we called \xe2\x80\x98the glorious\r\nBritish Principle of Dichotomy\xe2\x80\x99 into _Agriculture_. We persuaded many of\r\nthe well-to-do farmers to divide their staff of labourers into two\r\nParties, and to set them one against the other. They were called, like\r\nour political Parties, the \xe2\x80\x98Ins\xe2\x80\x99 and the \xe2\x80\x98Outs\xe2\x80\x99: the business of the\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ins\xe2\x80\x99 was to do as much of ploughing, sowing, or whatever might be\r\nneeded, as they could manage in a day, and at night they were paid\r\naccording to the amount they had _done_: the business of the \xe2\x80\x98Outs\xe2\x80\x99 was\r\nto hinder them, and _they_ were paid for the amount they had _hindered_.\r\nThe farmers found they had to pay only _half_ as much wages as they did\r\nbefore, and they didn\xe2\x80\x99t observe that the amount of work done was only a\r\n_quarter_ as much as was done before: so they took it up quite\r\nenthusiastically, _at first_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd _afterwards_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, _afterwards_ they didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it quite so well. In a very short\r\ntime, things settled down into a regular routine. No work _at all_ was\r\ndone. So the \xe2\x80\x98Ins\xe2\x80\x99 got no wages, and the \xe2\x80\x98Outs\xe2\x80\x99 got full pay. And the\r\nfarmers never discovered, till most of them were ruined, that the\r\nrascals had agreed to manage it so, and had shared the pay between them!\r\nWhile the thing lasted, there were funny sights to be seen! Why, I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\noften watched a ploughman, with two horses harnessed to the plough,\r\ndoing his best to get it _forwards_; while the opposition-ploughman,\r\nwith three donkeys harnessed at the _other_ end, was doing _his_ best to\r\nget it _backwards_! And the plough never moving an inch, _either_ way!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _we_ never did anything like _that_!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSimply because you were less _logical_ than we were,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mein\r\nHerr. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is _sometimes_ an advantage in being a donk\xe2\x80\x94Excuse me! No\r\n_personal_ allusion intended. All this happened _long ago_, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid the Dichotomy-Principle succeed in _any_ direction?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn _none_,\xe2\x80\x9d Mein Herr candidly confessed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt had a _very_ short trial\r\nin _Commerce_. The shop-keepers _wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ take it up, after once trying\r\nthe plan of having half the attendants busy in folding up and carrying\r\naway the goods which the other half were trying to spread out upon the\r\ncounters. They said the Public didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder at it,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we tried \xe2\x80\x98the British Principle\xe2\x80\x99 for some years. And the end of\r\nit all was\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d His voice suddenly dropped, almost to a whisper; and large\r\ntears began to roll down his cheeks. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94the end was that we got involved\r\nin a war; and there was a great battle, in which we far out-numbered the\r\nenemy. But what could one expect, when only _half_ of our soldiers were\r\nfighting, and the other half pulling them back? It ended in a crushing\r\ndefeat\xe2\x80\x94an utter rout. This caused a Revolution; and most of the\r\nGovernment were banished. I myself was accused of Treason, for having so\r\nstrongly advocated \xe2\x80\x98the British Principle.\xe2\x80\x99 My property was all\r\nforfeited, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94I was driven into exile! \xe2\x80\x98Now the mischief\xe2\x80\x99s done,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthey said, \xe2\x80\x98perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99ll kindly leave the country?\xe2\x80\x99 It nearly broke my\r\nheart, but I had to go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe melancholy tone became a wail: the wail became a chant: the chant\r\nbecame a song\xe2\x80\x94though whether it was _Mein Herr_ that was singing, this\r\ntime, or somebody else, I could not feel certain.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, now the mischief\xe2\x80\x99s done, perhaps\r\n  You\xe2\x80\x99ll kindly go and pack your traps?\r\n  Since _two_ (your daughter and your son)\r\n  Are Company, but _three_ are none.\r\n  A course of saving we\xe2\x80\x99ll begin:\r\n  When change is needed, _I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ invent it:\r\n  Don\xe2\x80\x99t think to put _your_ finger in\r\n  _This_ pie!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tottles (and he meant it).\r\n\r\nThe music seemed to die away. Mein Herr was again speaking in his\r\nordinary voice. \xe2\x80\x9cNow tell me one thing more,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I right in\r\nthinking that in _your_ Universities, though a man may reside some\r\nthirty or forty years, you examine him, once for all, at the end of the\r\nfirst three or four?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so, undoubtedly,\xe2\x80\x9d I admitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPractically, then, you examine a man at the _beginning_ of his career!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nthe old man said to himself rather than to me. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what guarantee have\r\nyou that he _retains_ the knowledge for which you have rewarded\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94beforehand, as _we_ should say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone,\xe2\x80\x9d I admitted, feeling a little puzzled at the drift of his\r\nremarks. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do _you_ secure that object?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy examining him at the _end_ of his thirty or forty years\xe2\x80\x94not at the\r\nbeginning,\xe2\x80\x9d he gently replied. \xe2\x80\x9cOn an average, the knowledge then found\r\nis about one-fifth of what it was at first\xe2\x80\x94the process of forgetting\r\ngoing on at a very steady uniform rate\xe2\x80\x94and he, who forgets _least_, gets\r\n_most_ honour, and most rewards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you give him the money when he needs it no longer? And you make\r\nhim live most of his life on _nothing_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHardly that. He gives his orders to the tradesmen: they supply him, for\r\nforty, sometimes fifty, years, at their own risk: then he gets his\r\nFellowship\xe2\x80\x94which pays him in _one_ year as much as _your_ Fellowships\r\npay in fifty\xe2\x80\x94and then he can easily pay all his bills, with interest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut suppose he fails to get his Fellowship? That must occasionally\r\nhappen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat occasionally happens.\xe2\x80\x9d It was Mein Herr\xe2\x80\x99s turn, now, to make\r\nadmissions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what becomes of the tradesmen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey calculate accordingly. When a man appears to be getting alarmingly\r\nignorant, or stupid, they will sometimes refuse to supply him any\r\nlonger. You have no idea with what enthusiasm a man will begin to rub up\r\nhis forgotten sciences or languages, when his butcher has cut off the\r\nsupply of beef and mutton!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who are the Examiners?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young men who have just come, brimming over with knowledge. You\r\nwould think it a curious sight,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cto see mere boys examining\r\nsuch old men. I have known a man set to examine his own grandfather. It\r\nwas a little painful for both of them, no doubt. The old gentleman was\r\nas bald as a coot\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow bald would that be?\xe2\x80\x9d I\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea why I asked this question. I felt\r\nI was getting foolish.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XIV.\r\n                            BRUNO\xe2\x80\x99S PICNIC.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs bald as bald,\xe2\x80\x9d was the bewildering reply. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Bruno, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you\r\na story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell _oo_ a story,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, beginning in a great hurry for\r\nfear of Sylvie getting the start of him: \xe2\x80\x9conce there were a Mouse\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlittle tiny Mouse\xe2\x80\x94such a tiny little Mouse! Oo never saw such a tiny\r\nMouse\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid nothing ever happen to it, Bruno?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you anything\r\nmore to tell us, besides its being so tiny?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing never happened to it,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno solemnly replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did nothing never happen to it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie, who was sitting, with\r\nher head on Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, patiently waiting for a chance of\r\nbeginning _her_ story.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt were too tiny,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_That\xe2\x80\x99s_ no reason!\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever tiny it was, things might happen\r\nto it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno looked pityingly at me, as if he thought me very stupid. \xe2\x80\x9cIt were\r\ntoo tiny,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cIf anything happened to it, it would die\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nwere so _very_ tiny!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally that\xe2\x80\x99s enough about its being tiny!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie put in. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\ninvented any more about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t invented no more yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t begin a story till you\xe2\x80\x99ve invented more! Now\r\nbe quiet, there\xe2\x80\x99s a good boy, and listen to _my_ story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Bruno, having quite exhausted all his inventive faculty, by\r\nbeginning in too great a hurry, quietly resigned himself to listening.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell about the other Bruno, please,\xe2\x80\x9d he said coaxingly.\r\n\r\nSylvie put her arms round his neck, and began:\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe wind was whispering among the trees,\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cThat wasn\xe2\x80\x99t good manners!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nBruno interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cNever mind about manners,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie) \xe2\x80\x9cand it was\r\nevening\xe2\x80\x94a nice moony evening, and the Owls were hooting\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretend they weren\xe2\x80\x99t Owls!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno pleaded, stroking her cheek with his\r\nfat little hand. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like Owls. So the\r\nOwls\xe2\x80\x94the Chickens, I mean\xe2\x80\x94were looking to see if they could find a nice\r\nfat Mouse for their supper\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretend it was a nice \xe2\x80\x99abbit!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it _wasn\xe2\x80\x99t_ a nice habit, to kill Mouses,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie argued. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\npretend _that_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t say \xe2\x80\x98_habit_,\xe2\x80\x99 oo silly fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno replied with a merry\r\ntwinkle in his eye. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99_abbits_\xe2\x80\x94that runs about in the fields!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRabbit? A Chicken _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ eat a Rabbit!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it might have wished to see if it could try to eat it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it wished to see if it could try\xe2\x80\x94oh, really, Bruno, that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnonsense! I shall go back to the Owls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, pretend they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t great eyes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they saw a little Boy,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie went on, disdaining to make any\r\nfurther corrections. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he asked them to tell him a story. And the\r\nOwls hooted and flew away\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cOo shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say \xe2\x80\x98_flewed_;\xe2\x80\x99 oo should\r\nsay \xe2\x80\x98_flied_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno whispered. But Sylvie wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear.) \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he met\r\na Lion. And he asked the Lion to tell him a story. And the Lion said\r\n\xe2\x80\x98yes,\xe2\x80\x99 it would. And, while the Lion was telling him the story, it\r\nnibbled some of his head off\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say \xe2\x80\x98nibbled\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno entreated. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly little things\r\nnibble\xe2\x80\x94little thin sharp things, with edges\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then, it \xe2\x80\x98_nubbled_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd when it had nubbled _all_\r\nhis head off, he went away, and he never said \xe2\x80\x98thank you\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat were very rude,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cIf he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t speak, he might have\r\nnodded\xe2\x80\x94no, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t nod. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n\xe2\x80\x99cause \xe2\x80\x98nubbly\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99s such a grumbly word to say\xe2\x80\x94when one person\xe2\x80\x99s got her\r\nhead on another person\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder. And he kept his birthday\r\non the second shelf.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long did he keep his birthday?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI never can keep _mine_\r\nmore than twenty-four hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, a birthday _stays_ that long by itself!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cOo doosn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow how to keep birthdays! This Boy kept _his_ a whole year!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then the next birthday would begin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cSo it would be\r\nhis birthday _always_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it were,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cDoos _oo_ have treats on _oor_ birthday,\r\nMister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen oo\xe2\x80\x99re _good_, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it _is_ a sort of treat, being good, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA sort of _treat_!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a sort of _punishment_, _I_\r\nthink!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie interrupted, almost sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cHow _can_ you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, but it _is_,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno persisted. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, look here, Mister Sir!\r\n_This_ is being good!\xe2\x80\x9d And he sat bolt upright, and put on an absurdly\r\nsolemn face. \xe2\x80\x9cFirst oo must sit up as straight as pokers\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94as _a_ poker,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie corrected him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94as straight as _pokers_,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno firmly repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cThen oo must clasp\r\noor hands\xe2\x80\x94_so_. You\r\nmay go to Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s Picnic.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n[Illustration: STARTING FOR BRUNO\xe2\x80\x99S PICNIC]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo they set off. He staid _pink_!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t kiss you like this, you know, if you were _black_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOo\xe2\x80\x99d _have_ to!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said with great decision. \xe2\x80\x9cBesides, Bruno wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n_Bruno_, oo know\xe2\x80\x94I mean, Bruno wasn\xe2\x80\x99t _me_\xe2\x80\x94I mean\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t talk nonsense,\r\nSylvie!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t do it again!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said very humbly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so, as they went\r\nalong, the Lion said \xe2\x80\x98Oh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what I used to do when I was a\r\nyoung Lion. I used to hide behind trees, to watch for little Boys.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(Bruno cuddled a little closer to her.) \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98And, if a little thin scraggy\r\nBoy came by, why, I used to let him go. But, if a little fat juicy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno could bear no more. \xe2\x80\x9cPretend he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t juicy!\xe2\x80\x9d he pleaded,\r\nhalf-sobbing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie briskly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be done in a moment!\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x94if a little fat juicy Boy came by, why, I used to spring out and\r\ngobble him up! Oh, you\xe2\x80\x99ve no _idea_ what a delicious thing it is\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nlittle juicy Boy!\xe2\x80\x99 And Bruno said \xe2\x80\x98Oh, if you please, Sir, _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ talk\r\nabout eating little boys! It makes me so _shivery_!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe real Bruno shivered, in sympathy with the hero.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Lion said \xe2\x80\x98Oh, well, we won\xe2\x80\x99t talk about it, then! I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell\r\nyou what happened on my wedding-day\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like _this_ part better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, patting my cheek to keep me\r\nawake.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98There was, oh, such a lovely wedding-breakfast! At _one_ end of the\r\ntable there was a large plum-pudding. And at the other end there was a\r\nnice roasted _Lamb_! Oh, you\xe2\x80\x99ve no _idea_ what a delicious thing it is\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nnice roasted Lamb!\xe2\x80\x99 And the Lamb said \xe2\x80\x98Oh, if you please, Sir, _don\xe2\x80\x99t_\r\ntalk about eating Lambs! It makes me so _shivery_!\xe2\x80\x99 And the Lion said\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, well, we won\xe2\x80\x99t talk about it, then!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XV.\r\n                           THE LITTLE FOXES.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, when they got to the top of the hill, Bruno opened the hamper: and\r\nhe took out the Bread, and the Apples, and the Milk: and they ate, and\r\nthey drank. And when they\xe2\x80\x99d finished the Milk, and eaten half the Bread\r\nand half the Apples, the Lamb said \xe2\x80\x98Oh, my paws is so sticky! I want to\r\nwash my paws!\xe2\x80\x99 And the Lion said \xe2\x80\x98Well, go down the hill, and wash them\r\nin the brook, yonder. We\xe2\x80\x99ll wait for you!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt never comed back!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno solemnly whispered to me.\r\n\r\nBut Sylvie overheard him. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not to whisper, Bruno! It spoils the\r\nstory! And when the Lamb had been gone a long time, the Lion said to\r\nBruno \xe2\x80\x98Do go and see after that silly little Lamb! It must have lost its\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x99 And Bruno went down the hill. And when he got to the brook, he saw\r\nthe Lamb sitting on the bank: and who should be sitting by it but an old\r\nFox!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know who _should_ be sitting by it,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said thoughtfully to\r\nhimself. \xe2\x80\x9cA old Fox _were_ sitting by it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the old Fox were saying,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie went on, for once conceding the\r\ngrammatical point, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yes, my dear, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be ever so happy with us, if\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll only come and see us! I\xe2\x80\x99ve got three little Foxes there, and we\r\ndo love little Lambs so dearly!\xe2\x80\x99 And the Lamb said \xe2\x80\x98But you never _eat_\r\nthem, do you, Sir?\xe2\x80\x99 And the Fox said \xe2\x80\x98Oh, no! What, _eat_ a Lamb? We\r\nnever _dream_ of doing such a thing!\xe2\x80\x99 So the Lamb said \xe2\x80\x98Then I\xe2\x80\x99ll come\r\nwith you.\xe2\x80\x99 And off they went, hand in hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat Fox were welly extremely wicked, _weren\xe2\x80\x99t_ it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie, rather shocked at such violent language. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t quite so bad as that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I mean, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t nice,\xe2\x80\x9d the little fellow corrected himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so Bruno went back to the Lion. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, come quick!\xe2\x80\x99 he said. \xe2\x80\x98The Fox\r\nhas taken the Lamb to his house with him! I\xe2\x80\x99m _sure_ he means to eat\r\nit!\xe2\x80\x99 And the Lion said \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll come as quick as ever I can!\xe2\x80\x99 And they\r\ntrotted down the hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo oo think he caught the Fox, Mister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. I shook my\r\nhead, not liking to speak: and Sylvie went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when they got to the house, Bruno looked in at the window. And\r\nthere he saw the three little Foxes sitting round the table, with their\r\nclean pinafores on, and spoons in their hands\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpoons in their hands!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno repeated in an ecstasy of delight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Fox had got a great big knife\xe2\x80\x94all ready to kill the poor little\r\nLamb\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cOo needn\xe2\x80\x99t be flightened, Mister Sir!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno put in, in a\r\nhasty whisper.)\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98ENTER THE LION\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd just as he was going to do it, Bruno heard a great ROAR\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (The\r\nreal Bruno put his hand into mine, and held tight), \xe2\x80\x9cand the Lion came\r\n_bang_ through the door, and the next moment it had bitten off the old\r\nFox\xe2\x80\x99s head! And Bruno jumped in at the window, and went leaping round\r\nthe room, and crying out \xe2\x80\x98Hooray! Hooray! The old Fox is dead! The old\r\nFox is dead!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno got up in some excitement. \xe2\x80\x9cMay I do it now?\xe2\x80\x9d he enquired.\r\n\r\nSylvie was quite decided on this point. \xe2\x80\x9cWait till afterwards,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cThe speeches come next, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know? You always love the\r\nspeeches, _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I doos,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno: and sat down again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lion\xe2\x80\x99s speech. \xe2\x80\x98Now, you silly little Lamb, go home to your mother,\r\nand never listen to old Foxes again. And be very good and obedient.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lamb\xe2\x80\x99s speech. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, indeed, Sir, I will, Sir!\xe2\x80\x99 and the Lamb went\r\naway.\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cBut _oo_ needn\xe2\x80\x99t go away!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno explained. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quite the\r\nnicest part\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s coming now!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie smiled. She liked having an\r\nappreciative audience.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lion\xe2\x80\x99s speech to Bruno. \xe2\x80\x98Now, Bruno, take those little Foxes home\r\nwith you, and teach them to be good obedient little Foxes! Not like that\r\nwicked old thing there, that\xe2\x80\x99s got no head!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cThat hasn\xe2\x80\x99t got no\r\nhead,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno repeated.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBruno\xe2\x80\x99s speech to the Lion. \xe2\x80\x98Oh, indeed, Sir, I will, Sir!\xe2\x80\x99 And the\r\nLion went away.\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cIt gets betterer and betterer, now,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno whispered\r\nto me, \xe2\x80\x9cright away to the end!\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBruno\xe2\x80\x99s speech to the little Foxes. \xe2\x80\x98Now, little Foxes, you\xe2\x80\x99re going to\r\nhave your first lesson in being good. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to put you into the\r\nhamper, along with the Apples and the Bread: and you\xe2\x80\x99re not to eat the\r\nApples: and you\xe2\x80\x99re not to eat the Bread: and you\xe2\x80\x99re not to eat\r\n_anything_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94till we get to my house: and then you\xe2\x80\x99ll have your supper.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe little Foxes\xe2\x80\x99 speech to Bruno. The little Foxes said nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Bruno put the Apples into the hamper\xe2\x80\x94and the little Foxes\xe2\x80\x94and the\r\nBread\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cThey had picnicked all the Milk,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno explained in a\r\nwhisper) \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94and he set off to go to his house.\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re getting near the\r\nend now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, when he had got a little way, he thought he would look into the\r\nhamper, and see how the little Foxes were getting on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he opened the door\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9c_you\xe2\x80\x99re_ not telling the story! So he\r\nopened the door, and behold, there were no Apples! So Bruno said \xe2\x80\x98Eldest\r\nlittle Fox, have _you_ been eating the Apples?\xe2\x80\x99 And the eldest little\r\nFox said \xe2\x80\x98No no no!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d (It is impossible to give the tone in which Sylvie\r\nrepeated this rapid little \xe2\x80\x98No no no!\xe2\x80\x99 The nearest I can come to it is\r\nto say that it was much as if a young and excited duck had tried to\r\nquack the words. It was too quick for a quack, and yet too harsh to be\r\nanything else.) \xe2\x80\x9cThen he said \xe2\x80\x98Second little Fox, have _you_ been eating\r\nthe Apples?\xe2\x80\x99 And the second little Fox said \xe2\x80\x98No no no!\xe2\x80\x99 Then he said\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Youngest little Fox, have _you_ been eating the Apples?\xe2\x80\x99 And the\r\nyoungest little Fox _tried_ to say \xe2\x80\x98No no no!\xe2\x80\x99 but its mouth was so\r\nfull, it couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, and it only said \xe2\x80\x98Wauch! Wauch! Wauch!\xe2\x80\x99 And Bruno\r\nlooked into its mouth. And its mouth was full of Apples! And Bruno shook\r\nhis head, and he said \xe2\x80\x98Oh dear, oh dear! What bad creatures these Foxes\r\nare!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno was listening intently: and, when Sylvie paused to take breath, he\r\ncould only just gasp out the words \xe2\x80\x9cAbout the Bread?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Bread comes next. So he shut the door again;\r\nand he went a little further; and then he thought he\xe2\x80\x99d just peep in once\r\nmore. And behold, there was no Bread!\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do \xe2\x80\x98behold\xe2\x80\x99 _mean_?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBruno. \xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.) \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he said \xe2\x80\x98Eldest little Fox, have _you_\r\nbeen eating the Bread?\xe2\x80\x99 And the eldest little Fox said \xe2\x80\x98No no no!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Second little Fox, have _you_ been eating the Bread?\xe2\x80\x99 And the second\r\nlittle Fox only said \xe2\x80\x98Wauch! Wauch! Wauch!\xe2\x80\x99 And Bruno looked into its\r\nmouth, and its mouth was full of Bread!\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cIt might have chokeded it,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Bruno.) \xe2\x80\x9cSo he said \xe2\x80\x98Oh dear, oh dear! What _shall_ I do with these\r\nFoxes?\xe2\x80\x99 And he went a little further.\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cNow comes the most interesting\r\npart,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno whispered.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when Bruno opened the hamper again, what do you think he saw?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cOnly _two_ Foxes!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno cried in a great hurry.) \xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\nit so quick. However, he _did_ see only _two_ Foxes. And he said \xe2\x80\x98Eldest\r\nlittle Fox, have you been eating the youngest little Fox?\xe2\x80\x99 And the\r\neldest little Fox said \xe2\x80\x98No no no!\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Second little Fox, have _you_ been\r\neating the youngest little Fox?\xe2\x80\x99 And the second little Fox did its very\r\nbest to say \xe2\x80\x98No no no!\xe2\x80\x99 but it could only say \xe2\x80\x98Weuchk! Weuchk! Weuchk!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd when Bruno looked into its mouth, it was half full of Bread, and\r\nhalf full of Fox!\xe2\x80\x9d (Bruno said nothing in the pause this time. He was\r\nbeginning to pant a little, as he knew the crisis was coming.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when he\xe2\x80\x99d got nearly home, he looked once more into the hamper, and\r\nhe saw\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno began, but a generous thought struck him, and he looked\r\nat me. \xe2\x80\x9c_Oo_ may say it, _this_ time, Mister Sir!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered. It was\r\na noble offer, but I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t rob him of the treat. \xe2\x80\x9cGo on, Bruno,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cyou say it much the best.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOnly\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94_one_\xe2\x80\x94Fox!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said with\r\ngreat solemnity.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98WHIHUAUCH! WHIHUAUCH!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Eldest little Fox,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, dropping the narrative-form in her\r\neagerness, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98you\xe2\x80\x99ve been _so_ good that I can hardly believe _you\xe2\x80\x99ve_\r\nbeen disobedient: but I\xe2\x80\x99m _afraid_ you\xe2\x80\x99ve been eating your little\r\nsister?\xe2\x80\x99 And the eldest little Fox said \xe2\x80\x98Whihuauch! Whihuauch!\xe2\x80\x99 and then\r\nit choked. And Bruno looked into its mouth, and it _was_ full!\xe2\x80\x9d (Sylvie\r\npaused to take breath, and Bruno lay back among the daisies, and looked\r\nat me triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it _grand_, Mister Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d said he. I tried\r\nhard to assume a critical tone. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s grand,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cbut it frightens\r\none so!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOo may sit a little closer to _me_, if oo like,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so Bruno went home: and took the hamper into the kitchen, and\r\nopened it. And he saw\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie looked at _me_, this time, as if she\r\nthought I had been rather neglected and ought to be allowed _one_ guess,\r\nat any rate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t guess!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno cried eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cI \xe2\x80\x99fraid I _must_ tell him!\r\nThere weren\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94_nuffin_ in the hamper!\xe2\x80\x9d I shivered in terror, and Bruno\r\nclapped his hands with delight. \xe2\x80\x9cHe _is_ flightened, Sylvie! Tell the\r\nrest!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Bruno said \xe2\x80\x98Eldest little Fox, have you been eating _yourself_, you\r\nwicked little Fox?\xe2\x80\x99 And the eldest little Fox said \xe2\x80\x98Whihuauch!\xe2\x80\x99 And then\r\nBruno saw there was only its _mouth_ in the hamper! So he took the\r\nmouth, and he opened it, and shook, and shook! And at last he shook the\r\nlittle Fox out of its own mouth! And then he said \xe2\x80\x98Open your mouth\r\nagain, you wicked little thing!\xe2\x80\x99 And he shook, and shook! And he shook\r\nout the second little Fox! And he said \xe2\x80\x98Now open _your_ mouth!\xe2\x80\x99 And he\r\nshook, and shook! And he shook out the youngest little Fox, and all the\r\nApples, and all the Bread!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then Bruno stood the little Foxes up against the wall: and he made\r\nthem a little speech. \xe2\x80\x98Now, little Foxes, you\xe2\x80\x99ve begun very wickedly\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to be punished. First you\xe2\x80\x99ll go up to the nursery, and wash\r\nyour faces, and put on clean pinafores. Then you\xe2\x80\x99ll hear the bell ring\r\nfor supper. Then you\xe2\x80\x99ll come down: and _you won\xe2\x80\x99t have any supper_: but\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll have a good _whipping_! Then you\xe2\x80\x99ll go to bed. Then in the\r\nmorning you\xe2\x80\x99ll hear the bell ring for breakfast. _But you won\xe2\x80\x99t have any\r\nbreakfast!_ You\xe2\x80\x99ll have a good _whipping_! Then you\xe2\x80\x99ll have your\r\nlessons. And, perhaps, if you\xe2\x80\x99re _very_ good, when dinner-time comes,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll have a little dinner, and no more whipping!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cHow _very_ kind\r\nhe was!\xe2\x80\x9d I whispered to Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9c_Middling_ kind,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno corrected me\r\ngravely.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo the little Foxes ran up to the nursery. And soon Bruno went into the\r\nhall, and rang the big bell. \xe2\x80\x98Tingle, tingle, tingle! Supper, supper,\r\nsupper!\xe2\x80\x99 Down came the little Foxes, in such a hurry for their supper!\r\nClean pinafores! Spoons in their hands! And, when they got into the\r\ndining-room, there was ever such a white table-cloth on the table! But\r\nthere was nothing on it but a big whip. And they had _such_ a whipping!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(I put my handkerchief to my eyes, and Bruno hastily climbed upon my\r\nknee and stroked my face. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly _one_ more whipping, Mister Sir!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwhispered. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t cry more than oo ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t help!\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the next morning early, Bruno rang the big bell again. \xe2\x80\x98Tingle,\r\ntingle, tingle! Breakfast, breakfast, breakfast!\xe2\x80\x99 Down came the little\r\nFoxes! Clean pinafores! Spoons in their hands! No breakfast! Only the\r\nbig whip! Then came lessons,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie hurried on, for I still had my\r\nhandkerchief to my eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the little Foxes were ever so good! And\r\nthey learned their lessons backwards, and forwards, and upside-down. And\r\nat last Bruno rang the big bell again. \xe2\x80\x98Tingle, tingle, tingle! Dinner,\r\ndinner, dinner!\xe2\x80\x99 And when the little Foxes came down\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cHad they clean\r\npinafores on?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno enquired. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd spoons?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you _know_ they had!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be _certain_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.) \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94they\r\ncame as slow as slow! And they said \xe2\x80\x98Oh! There\xe2\x80\x99ll be no dinner! There\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nonly be the big whip!\xe2\x80\x99 But, when they got into the room, they saw the\r\nmost _lovely_ dinner!\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cBuns?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno, clapping his hands.) \xe2\x80\x9cBuns,\r\nand cake, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94and jam?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.) \xe2\x80\x9cYes, jam\xe2\x80\x94and soup\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94and _sugar plums_!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno put in once more; and Sylvie seemed\r\nsatisfied.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd ever after that, they _were_ such good little Foxes! They did their\r\nlessons as good as gold\xe2\x80\x94and they never did what Bruno told them not\r\nto\xe2\x80\x94and they never ate each other any more\xe2\x80\x94and _they never ate\r\nthemselves_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe story came to an end so suddenly, it almost took my breath away;\r\nhowever I did my best to make a pretty speech of thanks. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvery\xe2\x80\x94very\xe2\x80\x94very much so, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure!\xe2\x80\x9d I seemed to hear myself say.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XVI.\r\n                          BEYOND THESE VOICES.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite catch what you said!\xe2\x80\x9d were the next words that reached\r\nmy ear, but certainly _not_ in the voice either of Sylvie or of Bruno,\r\nwhom I could just see, through the crowd of guests, standing by the\r\npiano, and listening to the Count\xe2\x80\x99s song. Mein Herr was the speaker. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t quite catch what you said!\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\xe2\x80\x99ve no doubt you\r\ntake _my_ view of it. Thank you _very_ much for your kind attention.\r\nThere is only but _one_ verse left to be sung!\xe2\x80\x9d These last words were\r\nnot in the gentle voice of Mein Herr, but in the deep bass of the French\r\nCount. And, in the silence that followed, the final stanza of \xe2\x80\x98Tottles\xe2\x80\x99\r\nrang through the room.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98NEVER!\xe2\x80\x99 YELLED TOTTLES]\r\n\r\n  See now this couple settled down\r\n  In quiet lodgings, out of town:\r\n  Submissively the tearful wife\r\n  Accepts a plain and humble life:\r\n  Yet begs one boon on bended knee:\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98My ducky-darling, don\xe2\x80\x99t resent it!\r\n  Mamma might come for two or three\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98NEVER!\xe2\x80\x99 yelled Tottles. And he meant it.\r\n\r\nThe conclusion of the song was followed by quite a chorus of thanks and\r\ncompliments from all parts of the room, which the gratified singer\r\nresponded to by bowing low in all directions. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is to me a great\r\nprivilege,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Lady Muriel, \xe2\x80\x9cto have met with this so marvellous\r\na song. The accompaniment to him is so strange, so mysterious: it is as\r\nif a new music were to be invented! I will play him once again so as\r\nthat to show you what I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d He returned to the piano, but the song\r\nhad vanished.\r\n\r\nThe bewildered singer searched through the heap of music lying on an\r\nadjoining table, but it was not there, either. Lady Muriel helped in the\r\nsearch: others soon joined: the excitement grew. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat _can_ have become\r\nof it?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lady Muriel. Nobody knew: one thing only was certain,\r\nthat no one had been near the piano since the Count had sung the last\r\nverse of the song.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNevare mind him!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, most good-naturedly. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall give it you\r\nwith memory alone!\xe2\x80\x9d He sat down, and began vaguely fingering the notes;\r\nbut nothing resembling the tune came out. Then he, too, grew excited.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what oddness! How much of singularity! That I might lose, not the\r\nwords alone, but the tune also\xe2\x80\x94that is quite curious, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all supposed it, heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was that sweet little boy, who found it for me,\xe2\x80\x9d the Count\r\nsuggested. \xe2\x80\x9cQuite perhaps _he_ is the thief?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course he is!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cBruno! Where are you, my\r\ndarling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut no Bruno replied: it seemed that the two children had vanished as\r\nsuddenly, and as mysteriously, as the song.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are playing us a trick!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel gaily exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is\r\nonly an _ex tempore_ game of Hide-and-Seek! That little Bruno is an\r\nembodied Mischief!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe suggestion was a welcome one to most of us, for some of the guests\r\nwere beginning to look decidedly uneasy. A general search was set on\r\nfoot with much enthusiasm: curtains were thrown back and shaken,\r\ncupboards opened, and ottomans turned over; but the number of possible\r\nhiding-places proved to be strictly limited; and the search came to an\r\nend almost as soon as it had begun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey must have run out, while we were wrapped up in the song,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady\r\nMuriel said, addressing herself to the Count, who seemed more agitated\r\nthan the others; \xe2\x80\x9cand no doubt they\xe2\x80\x99ve found their way back to the\r\nhousekeeper\xe2\x80\x99s room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot by _this_ door!\xe2\x80\x9d was the earnest protest of a knot of two or three\r\ngentlemen, who had been grouped round the door (one of them actually\r\nleaning against it) for the last half-hour, as they declared. \xe2\x80\x9c_This_\r\ndoor has not been opened since the song began!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn uncomfortable silence followed this announcement. Lady Muriel\r\nventured no further conjectures, but quietly examined the fastenings of\r\nthe windows, which opened as doors. They all proved to be well fastened,\r\n_inside_.\r\n\r\nNot yet at the end of her resources, Lady Muriel rang the bell. \xe2\x80\x9cAsk the\r\nhousekeeper to step here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand to bring the children\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwalking-things with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve brought them, my Lady,\xe2\x80\x9d said the obsequious housekeeper, entering\r\nafter another minute of silence. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought the young lady would have\r\ncome to my room to put on her boots. Here\xe2\x80\x99s your boots, my love!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nadded cheerfully, looking in all directions for the children. There was\r\nno answer, and she turned to Lady Muriel with a puzzled smile. \xe2\x80\x9cHave the\r\nlittle darlings hid themselves?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see them, just now,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel replied, rather evasively.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can leave their things here, Wilson. _I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ dress them, when\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re ready to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two little hats, and Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s walking-jacket, were handed round\r\namong the ladies, with many exclamations of delight. There certainly was\r\na sort of witchery of beauty about them. Even the little boots did not\r\nmiss their share of favorable criticism. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch natty little things!\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nmusical young lady exclaimed, almost fondling them as she spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nwhat tiny tiny feet they must have!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFinally, the things were piled together on the centre-ottoman, and the\r\nguests, despairing of seeing the children again, began to wish\r\ngood-night and leave the house.\r\n\r\nThere were only some eight or nine left\xe2\x80\x94to whom the Count was\r\nexplaining, for the twentieth time, how he had had his eye on the\r\nchildren during the last verse of the song; how he had then glanced\r\nround the room, to see what effect \xe2\x80\x9cde great chest-note\xe2\x80\x9d had had upon\r\nhis audience; and how, when he looked back again, they had both\r\ndisappeared\xe2\x80\x94when exclamations of dismay began to be heard on all sides,\r\nthe Count hastily bringing his story to an end to join in the outcry.\r\n\r\nThe walking-things had all disappeared!\r\n\r\nAfter the utter failure of the search for the _children_, there was a\r\nvery half-hearted search made for their _apparel_. The remaining guests\r\nseemed only too glad to get away, leaving only the Count and our four\r\nselves.\r\n\r\nThe Count sank into an easy-chair, and panted a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho then _are_ these dear children, I pray you?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy come\r\nthey, why go they, in this so little ordinary a fashion? That the music\r\nshould make itself to vanish\xe2\x80\x94that the hats, the boots, should make\r\nthemselves to vanish\xe2\x80\x94how is it, I pray you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea where they are!\xe2\x80\x9d was all I could say, on finding myself\r\nappealed to, by general consent, for an explanation.\r\n\r\nThe Count seemed about to ask further questions, but checked himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe hour makes himself to become late,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish to you a very\r\ngood night, my Lady. I betake myself to my bed\xe2\x80\x94to dream\xe2\x80\x94if that indeed I\r\nbe not dreaming now!\xe2\x80\x9d And he hastily left the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay awhile, stay awhile!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl, as I was about to follow the\r\nCount. \xe2\x80\x9c_You_ are not a guest, you know! Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s friend is at _home_\r\nhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks!\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as, with true English instincts, we drew our chairs\r\ntogether round the fire-place, though no fire was burning\xe2\x80\x94Lady Muriel\r\nhaving taken the heap of music on her knee, to have one more search for\r\nthe strangely-vanished song.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you sometimes feel a wild longing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, addressing herself\r\nto me, \xe2\x80\x9cto have something more to do with your hands, while you talk,\r\nthan just holding a cigar, and now and then knocking off the ash? Oh, I\r\nknow all that you\xe2\x80\x99re going to say!\xe2\x80\x9d (This was to Arthur, who appeared\r\nabout to interrupt her.) \xe2\x80\x9cThe Majesty of Thought supersedes the work of\r\nthe fingers. A Man\xe2\x80\x99s severe thinking, _plus_ the shaking-off a\r\ncigar-ash, comes to the same total as a Woman\xe2\x80\x99s trivial fancies, _plus_\r\nthe most elaborate embroidery. _That\xe2\x80\x99s_ your sentiment, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it, only\r\nbetter expressed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur looked into the radiant, mischievous face, with a grave and very\r\ntender smile. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said resignedly: \xe2\x80\x9cthat is my sentiment,\r\nexactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRest of body, and activity of mind,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in. \xe2\x80\x9cSome writer tells us\r\n_that_ is the acme of human happiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlenty of _bodily_ rest, at any rate!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel replied, glancing at\r\nthe three recumbent figures around her. \xe2\x80\x9cBut what you call activity of\r\n_mind_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94is the privilege of young Physicians _only_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl. \xe2\x80\x9cWe old\r\nmen have no claim to be active! _What can an old man do but die?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good many other things, I should _hope_,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur said earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, maybe. Still you have the advantage of me in many ways, dear boy!\r\nNot only that _your_ day is dawning while _mine_ is setting, but your\r\n_interest_ in Life\xe2\x80\x94somehow I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t help envying you _that_. It will be\r\nmany a year before you lose your hold of _that_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet surely many human interests _survive_ human Life?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMany do, no doubt. And _some_ forms of Science; but only _some_, I\r\nthink. Mathematics, for instance: _that_ seems to possess an endless\r\ninterest: one ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t imagine _any_ form of Life, or _any_ race of\r\nintelligent beings, where Mathematical truth would lose its meaning. But\r\nI fear _Medicine_ stands on a different footing. Suppose you discover a\r\nremedy for some disease hitherto supposed to be incurable. Well, it is\r\ndelightful for the moment, no doubt\xe2\x80\x94full of interest\xe2\x80\x94perhaps it brings\r\nyou fame and fortune. But what then? Look on, a few years, into a life\r\nwhere disease has no existence. What is your discovery worth, _then_?\r\nMilton makes Jove promise too much. \xe2\x80\x98_Of so much fame in heaven expect\r\nthy meed._\xe2\x80\x99 Poor comfort, when one\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98fame\xe2\x80\x99 concerns matters that will\r\nhave ceased to have a meaning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate, one wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t care to make any _fresh_ medical\r\ndiscoveries,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cI see no help for _that_\xe2\x80\x94though I shall be\r\nsorry to give up my favorite studies. Still, medicine, disease, pain,\r\nsorrow, sin\xe2\x80\x94I fear they\xe2\x80\x99re all linked together. Banish sin, and you\r\nbanish them all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Military_ science is a yet stronger instance,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl. \xe2\x80\x9cWithout\r\nsin, _war_ would surely be impossible. Still any mind, that has had in\r\nthis life any keen interest, not in _itself_ sinful, will surely find\r\nitself _some_ congenial line of work hereafter. Wellington may have no\r\nmore _battles_ to fight\xe2\x80\x94and yet\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98We doubt not that, for one so true,\r\n  There must be other, nobler work to do,\r\n  Than when he fought at Waterloo,\r\n        And Victor he must ever be!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lingered over the beautiful words, as if he loved them: and his\r\nvoice, like distant music, died away into silence.\r\n\r\nAfter a minute or two he began again. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I\xe2\x80\x99m not wearying you, I would\r\nlike to tell you an idea of the future Life which has haunted me for\r\nyears, like a sort of waking nightmare\xe2\x80\x94I ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t reason myself out of\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray do,\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur and I replied, almost in a breath.
