Lady Muriel put\r\naside the heap of music, and folded her hands together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one idea,\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl resumed, \xe2\x80\x9cthat has seemed to me to overshadow\r\nall the rest, is that of _Eternity_\xe2\x80\x94involving, as it seems to do, the\r\nnecessary _exhaustion_ of all subjects of human interest. Take Pure\r\nMathematics, for instance\xe2\x80\x94a Science independent of our present\r\nsurroundings. I have studied it, myself, a little. Take the subject of\r\ncircles and ellipses\xe2\x80\x94what we call \xe2\x80\x98curves of the second degree.\xe2\x80\x99 In a\r\nfuture Life, it would only be a question of so many years (or _hundreds_\r\nof years, if you like), for a man to work out _all_ their properties.\r\nThen he _might_ go to curves of the third degree. Say _that_ took ten\r\ntimes as long (you see we have _unlimited_ time to deal with). I can\r\nhardly imagine his _interest_ in the subject holding out even for those;\r\nand, though there is no limit to the _degree_ of the curves he might\r\nstudy, yet surely the time, needed to exhaust _all_ the novelty and\r\ninterest of the subject, would be absolutely _finite_? And so of all\r\nother branches of Science. And, when I transport myself, in thought,\r\nthrough some thousands or millions of years, and fancy myself possessed\r\nof as much Science as one created reason can carry, I ask myself \xe2\x80\x98What\r\nthen? With nothing more to learn, can one rest content on _knowledge_,\r\nfor the eternity yet to be lived through?\xe2\x80\x99 It has been a very wearying\r\nthought to me. I have sometimes fancied one _might_, in that event, say\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is better _not_ to be,\xe2\x80\x99 and pray for personal _annihilation_\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nNirvana of the Buddhists.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that is only half the picture,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cBesides working for\r\n_oneself_, may there not be the helping of _others_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely, surely!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel exclaimed in a tone of relief, looking at\r\nher father with sparkling eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl, \xe2\x80\x9cso long as there _were_ any others needing help.\r\nBut, given ages and ages more, surely all created reasons would at\r\nlength reach the same dead level of _satiety_. And _then_ what is there\r\nto look forward to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that weary feeling,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young Doctor. \xe2\x80\x9cI have gone through\r\nit all, more than once. Now let me tell you how I have put it to myself.\r\nI have imagined a little child, playing with toys on his nursery-floor,\r\nand yet able to _reason_, and to look on, thirty years ahead. Might he\r\nnot say to himself \xe2\x80\x98By that time I shall have had enough of bricks and\r\nninepins. How weary Life will be!\xe2\x80\x99 Yet, if we look forward through those\r\nthirty years, we find him a great statesman, full of interests and joys\r\nfar more intense than his baby-life could give\xe2\x80\x94joys wholly inconceivable\r\nto his baby-mind\xe2\x80\x94joys such as no baby-language could in the faintest\r\ndegree describe. Now, may not our life, a million years hence, have the\r\nsame relation, to our life now, that the man\xe2\x80\x99s life has to the child\xe2\x80\x99s?\r\nAnd, just as one might try, all in vain, to express to that child, in\r\nthe language of bricks and ninepins, the meaning of \xe2\x80\x98politics,\xe2\x80\x99 so\r\nperhaps all those descriptions of Heaven, with its music, and its\r\nfeasts, and its streets of gold, may be only attempts to describe, in\r\n_our_ words, things for which we _really_ have no words at all. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou think that, in _your_ picture of another life, you are in fact\r\ntransplanting that child into political life, without making any\r\nallowance for his growing up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl. \xe2\x80\x9cThe music of Heaven _may_ be\r\nsomething beyond our powers of thought. Yet the music of Earth is sweet!\r\nMuriel, my child, sing us something before we go to bed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, as he rose and lit the candles on the cottage-piano,\r\nlately banished from the drawing-room to make room for a \xe2\x80\x98semi-grand.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is a song here, that I have never heard you sing.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98Hail to thee, blithe spirit!\r\n    Bird thou never wert,\r\n  That from Heaven, or near it,\r\n    Pourest thy full heart!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nhe read from the page he had spread open before her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd our little life here,\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl went on, \xe2\x80\x9cis, to that grand time,\r\nlike a child\xe2\x80\x99s summer-day! One gets tired as night draws on,\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\nwith a touch of sadness in his voice, \xe2\x80\x9cand one gets to long for bed! For\r\nthose welcome words \xe2\x80\x98Come, child, \xe2\x80\x99tis bed-time!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER XVII.\r\n                             TO THE RESCUE!\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ bed-time!\xe2\x80\x9d said a sleepy little voice. \xe2\x80\x9cThe owls hasn\xe2\x80\x99t gone\r\nto bed, and I s\xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t go to seep wizout oo sings to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know the owls have only just got\r\nup? But the _frogs_ have gone to bed, ages ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, _I_ aren\xe2\x80\x99t a frog,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall I sing?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie, skilfully avoiding the argument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk Mister Sir,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno lazily replied, clasping his hands behind his\r\ncurly head, and lying back on his fern-leaf, till it almost bent over\r\nwith his weight. \xe2\x80\x9cThis aren\xe2\x80\x99t a comfable leaf, Sylvie. Find me a\r\ncomfabler\xe2\x80\x94please!\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as an after-thought, in obedience to a\r\nwarning finger held up by Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cI doosn\xe2\x80\x99t like being feet-upwards!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a pretty sight to see\xe2\x80\x94the motherly way in which the fairy-child\r\ngathered up her little brother in her arms, and laid him on a stronger\r\nleaf. She gave it just a touch to set it rocking, and it went on\r\nvigorously by itself, as if it contained some hidden machinery. It\r\ncertainly wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the wind, for the evening-breeze had quite died away\r\nagain, and not a leaf was stirring over our heads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy does that one leaf rock so, without the others?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked Sylvie.\r\nShe only smiled sweetly and shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know _why_,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cIt always does, if it\xe2\x80\x99s got a fairy-child on it. It _has_ to, you\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd can people see the leaf rock, who ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t see the Fairy on it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cA leaf\xe2\x80\x99s a leaf, and everybody can see\r\nit; but Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s Bruno, and they ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t see _him_, unless they\xe2\x80\x99re eerie,\r\nlike you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen I understood how it was that one sometimes sees\xe2\x80\x94going through the\r\nwoods in a still evening\xe2\x80\x94one fern-leaf rocking steadily on, all by\r\nitself. Haven\xe2\x80\x99t you ever seen that? Try if you can see the fairy-sleeper\r\non it, next time; but don\xe2\x80\x99t _pick_ the leaf, whatever you do; let the\r\nlittle one sleep on!\r\n\r\nBut all this time Bruno was getting sleepier and sleepier. \xe2\x80\x9cSing, sing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe murmured fretfully. Sylvie looked to me for instructions. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall\r\nit be?\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you sing him the nursery-song you once told me of?\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one that had been put through the mind-mangle, you know. \xe2\x80\x98_The\r\nlittle man that had a little gun_,\xe2\x80\x99 I think it was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that are one of the _Professor\xe2\x80\x99s_ songs!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cI likes\r\nthe little man; and I likes the way they spinned him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94like a\r\nteetle-totle-tum.\xe2\x80\x9d And he turned a loving look on the gentle old man who\r\nwas sitting at the other side of his leaf-bed, and who instantly began\r\nto sing, accompanying himself on his Outlandish guitar, while the snail,\r\non which he sat, waved its horns in time to the music.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: BRUNO\xe2\x80\x99S BED-TIME]\r\n\r\n  In stature the Manlet was dwarfish\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    No burly big Blunderbore he:\r\n  And he wearily gazed on the crawfish\r\n    His Wifelet had dressed for his tea.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cNow reach me, sweet Atom, my gunlet,\r\n    And hurl the old shoelet for luck:\r\n  Let me hie to the bank of the runlet,\r\n              And shoot thee a Duck!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  She has reached him his minikin gunlet:\r\n    She has hurled the old shoelet for luck:\r\n  She is busily baking a bunlet,\r\n    To welcome him home with his Duck.\r\n  On he speeds, never wasting a wordlet,\r\n    Though thoughtlets cling, closely as wax,\r\n  To the spot where the beautiful birdlet\r\n                So quietly quacks.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98LONG CEREMONIOUS CALLS\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n  Where the Lobsterlet lurks, and the Crablet\r\n    So slowly and sleepily crawls:\r\n  Where the Dolphin\xe2\x80\x99s at home, and the Dablet\r\n    Pays long ceremonious calls:\r\n  Where the Grublet is sought by the Froglet:\r\n    Where the Frog is pursued by the Duck:\r\n  Where the Ducklet is chased by the Doglet\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n              So runs the world\xe2\x80\x99s luck!\r\n\r\n[Illustration: THE VOICES]\r\n\r\n  He has loaded with bullet and powder:\r\n    His footfall is noiseless as air:\r\n  But the Voices grow louder and louder,\r\n    And bellow, and bluster, and blare.\r\n  They bristle before him and after,\r\n    They flutter above and below,\r\n  Shrill shriekings of lubberly laughter,\r\n                Weird wailings of woe!\r\n\r\n  They echo without him, within him:\r\n    They thrill through his whiskers and beard:\r\n  Like a teetotum seeming to spin him,\r\n    With sneers never hitherto sneered.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cAvengement,\xe2\x80\x9d they cry, \xe2\x80\x9con our Foelet!\r\n    Let the Manikin weep for our wrongs!\r\n  Let us drench him, from toplet to toelet,\r\n              With Nursery-Songs!\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98HIS SOUL SHALL BE SAD FOR THE SPIDER\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cHe shall muse upon \xe2\x80\x98Hey! Diddle! Diddle!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n    On the Cow that surmounted the Moon:\r\n  He shall rave of the Cat and the Fiddle,\r\n    And the Dish that eloped with the Spoon:\r\n  And his soul shall be sad for the Spider,\r\n    When Miss Muffet was sipping her whey,\r\n  That so tenderly sat down beside her,\r\n                And scared her away!\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cThe music of Midsummer-madness\r\n    Shall sting him with many a bite,\r\n  Till, in rapture of rollicking sadness,\r\n    He shall groan with a gloomy delight:\r\n  He shall swathe him, like mists of the morning,\r\n    In platitudes luscious and limp,\r\n  Such as deck, with a deathless adorning,\r\n              The Song of the Shrimp!\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the Ducklet\xe2\x80\x99s dark doom is decided,\r\n    We will trundle him home in a trice:\r\n  And the banquet, so plainly provided,\r\n    Shall round into rose-buds and rice:\r\n  In a blaze of pragmatic invention\r\n    He shall wrestle with Fate, and shall reign:\r\n  But he has not a friend fit to mention,\r\n              So hit him again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  He has shot it, the delicate darling!\r\n    And the Voices have ceased from their strife:\r\n  Not a whisper of sneering or snarling;\r\n    As he carries it home to his wife:\r\n  Then, cheerily champing the bunlet\r\n    His spouse was so skilful to bake,\r\n  He hies him once more to the runlet,\r\n              To fetch her the Drake!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s sound asleep now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie, carefully tucking in the edge of a\r\nviolet-leaf, which she had been spreading over him as a sort of blanket:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cgood night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood night!\xe2\x80\x9d I echoed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may well say \xe2\x80\x98good night\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed Lady Muriel, rising and\r\nshutting up the piano as she spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen you\xe2\x80\x99ve been nid\xe2\x80\x94nid\xe2\x80\x94nodding\r\nall the time I\xe2\x80\x99ve been singing for your benefit! What was it all about,\r\nnow?\xe2\x80\x9d she demanded imperiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething about a duck?\xe2\x80\x9d I hazarded. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, a bird of some kind?\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\ncorrected myself, perceiving at once that _that_ guess was wrong, at any\r\nrate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Something about a bird of some kind!_\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel repeated, with as\r\nmuch withering scorn as her sweet face was capable of conveying. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s the way he speaks of Shelley\xe2\x80\x99s Sky-Lark, is it? When the Poet\r\nparticularly says \xe2\x80\x98_Hail to thee, blithe spirit!_ Bird _thou never\r\nwert_!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: LORDS OF THE CREATION]\r\n\r\nShe led the way to the smoking-room, where, ignoring all the usages of\r\nSociety and all the instincts of Chivalry, the three Lords of the\r\nCreation reposed at their ease in low rocking-chairs, and permitted the\r\none lady who was present to glide gracefully about among us, supplying\r\nour wants in the form of cooling drinks, cigarettes, and lights. Nay, it\r\nwas only _one_ of the three who had the chivalry to go beyond the\r\ncommon-place \xe2\x80\x9cthank you,\xe2\x80\x9d and to quote the Poet\xe2\x80\x99s exquisite description\r\nof how Geraint, when waited on by Enid, was moved\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cTo stoop and kiss the tender little thumb\r\n  That crossed the platter as she laid it down,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nand to suit the action to the word\xe2\x80\x94an audacious liberty for which, I\r\nfeel bound to report, he was _not_ duly reprimanded.\r\n\r\nAs no topic of conversation seemed to occur to any one, and as we were,\r\nall four, on those delightful terms with one another (the only terms, I\r\nthink, on which any friendship, that deserves the name of _intimacy_,\r\ncan be maintained) which involve no sort of necessity for _speaking_ for\r\nmere speaking\xe2\x80\x99s sake, we sat in silence for some minutes.\r\n\r\nAt length I broke the silence by asking \xe2\x80\x9cIs there any fresh news from\r\nthe harbour about the Fever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone since this morning,\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl said, looking very grave. \xe2\x80\x9cBut that\r\nwas alarming enough. The Fever is spreading fast: the London doctor has\r\ntaken fright and left the place, and the only one now available isn\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\nregular doctor at all: he is apothecary, and doctor, and dentist, and I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what other trades, all in one. It\xe2\x80\x99s a bad outlook for those\r\npoor fishermen\xe2\x80\x94and a worse one for all the women and children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many are there of them altogether?\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were nearly one hundred, a week ago.\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl: \xe2\x80\x9cbut there\r\nhave been twenty or thirty deaths since then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what religious ministrations are there to be had?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are three brave men down there,\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl replied, his voice\r\ntrembling with emotion, \xe2\x80\x9cgallant heroes as ever won the Victoria Cross!\r\nI am certain that no one of the three will ever leave the place merely\r\nto save his own life. There\xe2\x80\x99s the Curate: his wife is with him: they\r\nhave no children. Then there\xe2\x80\x99s the Roman Catholic Priest. And there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe Wesleyan Minister. They go amongst their own flocks, mostly; but I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ntold that those who are dying like to have _any_ of the three with them.\r\nHow slight the barriers seem to be that part Christian from Christian,\r\nwhen one has to deal with the great facts of Life and the reality of\r\nDeath!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it must be, and so it should be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Arthur was beginning, when the\r\nfront-door bell rang, suddenly and violently.\r\n\r\nWe heard the front-door hastily opened, and voices outside: then a knock\r\nat the door of the smoking-room, and the old house-keeper appeared,\r\nlooking a little scared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo persons, my Lord, to speak with Dr. Forester.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur stepped outside at once, and we heard his cheery \xe2\x80\x9cWell, my men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbut the answer was less audible, the only words I could distinctly catch\r\nbeing \xe2\x80\x9cten since morning, and two more just\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there _is_ a doctor there?\xe2\x80\x9d we heard Arthur say: and a deep voice,\r\nthat we had not heard before, replied \xe2\x80\x9cDead, Sir. Died three hours ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel shuddered, and hid her face in her hands: but at this moment\r\nthe front-door was quietly closed, and we heard no more.\r\n\r\nFor a few minutes we sat quite silent: then the Earl left the room, and\r\nsoon returned to tell us that Arthur had gone away with the two\r\nfishermen, leaving word that he would be back in about an hour. And,\r\ntrue enough, at the end of that interval\xe2\x80\x94during which very little was\r\nsaid, none of us seeming to have the heart to talk\xe2\x80\x94the front-door once\r\nmore creaked on its rusty hinges, and a step was heard in the passage,\r\nhardly to be recognised as Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s, so slow and uncertain was it, like\r\na blind man feeling his way.\r\n\r\nHe came in, and stood before Lady Muriel, resting one hand heavily on\r\nthe table, and with a strange look in his eyes, as if he were walking in\r\nhis sleep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuriel\xe2\x80\x94my love\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he paused, and his lips quivered: but after a minute\r\nhe went on more steadily. \xe2\x80\x9cMuriel\xe2\x80\x94my darling\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x94_want_ me\xe2\x80\x94down in the\r\nharbour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Must_ you go?\xe2\x80\x9d she pleaded, rising and laying her hands on his\r\nshoulders, and looking up into his face with her great eyes brimming\r\nover with tears. \xe2\x80\x9cMust _you_ go, Arthur? It may mean\xe2\x80\x94death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe met her gaze without flinching. \xe2\x80\x9cIt _does_ mean death,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a\r\nhusky whisper: \xe2\x80\x9cbut\xe2\x80\x94darling\xe2\x80\x94I am _called_. And even my life itself\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHis voice failed him, and he said no more.\r\n\r\nFor a minute she stood quite silent, looking upwards with a helpless\r\ngaze, as if even prayer were now useless, while her features worked and\r\nquivered with the great agony she was enduring. Then a sudden\r\ninspiration seemed to come upon her and light up her face with a strange\r\nsweet smile. \xe2\x80\x9c_Your_ life?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not _yours_ to give!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArthur had recovered himself by this time, and could reply quite firmly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not _mine_ to give. It is _yours_, now,\r\nmy\xe2\x80\x94wife that is to be! And you\xe2\x80\x94do _you_ forbid me to go? Will you not\r\nspare me, my own beloved one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill clinging to him, she laid her head softly on his breast. She had\r\nnever done such a thing in my presence before, and I knew how deeply she\r\nmust be moved. \xe2\x80\x9cI _will_ spare you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, calmly and quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cto\r\nGod.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to God\xe2\x80\x99s poor,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to God\xe2\x80\x99s poor,\xe2\x80\x9d she added. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen must it be, sweet love?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98WILL YOU NOT SPARE ME?\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow morning,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I have much to do before then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he told us how he had spent his hour of absence. He had been to\r\nthe Vicarage, and had arranged for the wedding to take place at eight\r\nthe next morning (there was no legal obstacle, as he had, some time\r\nbefore this, obtained a Special License) in the little church we knew so\r\nwell. \xe2\x80\x9cMy old friend here,\xe2\x80\x9d indicating me, \xe2\x80\x9cwill act as \xe2\x80\x98Best Man,\xe2\x80\x99 I\r\nknow: your father will be there to give you away: and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94you will\r\ndispense with bride\xe2\x80\x99s-maids, my darling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nodded: no words came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then I can go with a willing heart\xe2\x80\x94to do God\xe2\x80\x99s work\xe2\x80\x94knowing that we\r\nare _one_\xe2\x80\x94and that we are together in _spirit_, though not in bodily\r\npresence\xe2\x80\x94and are most of all together when we pray! Our _prayers_ will\r\ngo up together\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d sobbed Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you must not stay longer now, my\r\ndarling! Go home and take some rest. You will need all your strength\r\nto-morrow\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I will go,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur. \xe2\x80\x9cWe will be here in good time to-morrow.\r\nGood night, my own own darling!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI followed his example, and we two left the house together. As we walked\r\nback to our lodgings, Arthur sighed deeply once or twice, and seemed\r\nabout to speak\xe2\x80\x94but no words came, till we had entered the house, and had\r\nlit our candles, and were at our bedroom-doors. Then Arthur said \xe2\x80\x9cGood\r\nnight, old fellow! God bless you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless you!\xe2\x80\x9d I echoed, from the very depths of my heart.\r\n\r\nWe were back again at the Hall by eight in the morning, and found Lady\r\nMuriel and the Earl, and the old Vicar, waiting for us. It was a\r\nstrangely sad and silent party that walked up to the little church and\r\nback; and I could not help feeling that it was much more like a funeral\r\nthan a wedding: to Lady Muriel it _was_ in fact, a funeral rather than a\r\nwedding, so heavily did the presentiment weigh upon her (as she told us\r\nafterwards) that her newly-won husband was going forth to his death.\r\n\r\nThen we had breakfast; and, all too soon, the vehicle was at the door,\r\nwhich was to convey Arthur, first to his lodgings, to pick up the things\r\nhe was taking with him, and then as far towards the death-stricken\r\nhamlet as it was considered safe to go. One or two of the fishermen were\r\nto meet him on the road, to carry his things the rest of the way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd are you quite sure you are taking all that you will need?\xe2\x80\x9d Lady\r\nMuriel asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll that I shall need as a _doctor_, certainly. And my own personal\r\nneeds are few: I shall not even take any of my own wardrobe\xe2\x80\x94there is a\r\nfisherman\xe2\x80\x99s suit, ready-made, that is waiting for me at my lodgings. I\r\nshall only take my watch, and a few books, and\xe2\x80\x94stay\xe2\x80\x94there _is_ one book\r\nI should like to add, a pocket-Testament\xe2\x80\x94to use at the bedsides of the\r\nsick and dying\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake mine!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel: and she ran upstairs to fetch it. \xe2\x80\x9cIt has\r\nnothing written in it but \xe2\x80\x98Muriel,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d she said as she returned with it:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cshall I inscribe\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my own one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Arthur, taking it from her. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat _could_ you\r\ninscribe better than that? Could any human name mark it more clearly as\r\nmy own individual property? Are _you_ not mine? Are you not,\xe2\x80\x9d (with all\r\nthe old playfulness of manner) \xe2\x80\x9cas Bruno would say, \xe2\x80\x98my _very mine_\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bade a long and loving adieu to the Earl and to me, and left the\r\nroom, accompanied only by his wife, who was bearing up bravely, and\r\nwas\xe2\x80\x94_outwardly_, at least\xe2\x80\x94less overcome than her old father. We waited\r\nin the room a minute or two, till the sound of wheels had told us that\r\nArthur had driven away; and even then we waited still, for the step of\r\nLady Muriel, going upstairs to her room, to die away in the distance.\r\nHer step, usually so light and joyous, now sounded slow and weary, like\r\none who plods on under a load of hopeless misery; and I felt almost as\r\nhopeless, and almost as wretched, as she. \xe2\x80\x9cAre we four destined _ever_\r\nto meet again, on this side the grave?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked myself, as I walked to\r\nmy home. And the tolling of a distant bell seemed to answer me, \xe2\x80\x9cNo! No!\r\nNo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER XVIII.\r\n                          A NEWSPAPER-CUTTING.\r\n\r\n\r\n                _EXTRACT FROM THE \xe2\x80\x9cFAYFIELD CHRONICLE.\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\n_Our readers will have followed with painful interest, the accounts we\r\nhave from time to time published of the terrible epidemic which has,\r\nduring the last two months, carried off most of the inhabitants of the\r\nlittle fishing-harbour adjoining the village of Elveston. The last\r\nsurvivors, numbering twenty-three only, out of a population which, three\r\nshort months ago, exceeded one hundred and twenty, were removed on\r\nWednesday last, under the authority of the Local Board, and safely\r\nlodged in the County Hospital: and the place is now veritably \xe2\x80\x98a city of\r\nthe dead,\xe2\x80\x99 without a single human voice to break its silence._\r\n\r\n_The rescuing party consisted of six sturdy fellows\xe2\x80\x94fishermen from the\r\nneighbourhood\xe2\x80\x94directed by the resident Physician of the Hospital, who\r\ncame over for that purpose, heading a train of hospital-ambulances. The\r\nsix men had been selected\xe2\x80\x94from a much larger number who had volunteered\r\nfor this peaceful \xe2\x80\x98forlorn hope\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94for their strength and robust health,\r\nas the expedition was considered to be, even now, when the malady has\r\nexpended its chief force, not unattended with danger._\r\n\r\n_Every precaution that science could suggest, against the risk of\r\ninfection, was adopted: and the sufferers were tenderly carried on\r\nlitters, one by one, up the steep hill, and placed in the ambulances\r\nwhich, each provided with a hospital nurse, were waiting on the level\r\nroad. The fifteen miles, to the Hospital, were done at a walking-pace,\r\nas some of the patients were in too prostrate a condition to bear\r\njolting, and the journey occupied the whole afternoon._\r\n\r\n_The twenty-three patients consist of nine men, six women, and eight\r\nchildren. It has not been found possible to identify them all, as some\r\nof the children\xe2\x80\x94left with no surviving relatives\xe2\x80\x94are infants; and two\r\nmen and one woman are not yet able to make rational replies, the\r\nbrain-powers being entirely in abeyance. Among a more well-to-do-race,\r\nthere would no doubt have been names marked on the clothes; but here no\r\nsuch evidence is forthcoming._\r\n\r\n_Besides the poor fishermen and their families, there were but five\r\npersons to be accounted for: and it was ascertained, beyond a doubt,\r\nthat all five are numbered with the dead. It is a melancholy pleasure to\r\nplace on record the names of these genuine martyrs\xe2\x80\x94than whom none,\r\nsurely, are more worthy to be entered on the glory-roll of England\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheroes! They are as follows:\xe2\x80\x94_\r\n\r\n_The Rev. James Burgess, M.A., and Emma his wife. He was the Curate at\r\nthe Harbour, not thirty years old, and had been married only two years.\r\nA written record was found in their house, of the dates of their\r\ndeaths._\r\n\r\n_Next to theirs we will place the honoured name of Dr. Arthur Forester,\r\nwho, on the death of the local physician, nobly faced the imminent peril\r\nof death, rather than leave these poor folk uncared for in their last\r\nextremity. No record of his name, or of the date of his death, was\r\nfound: but the corpse was easily identified, although dressed in the\r\nordinary fisherman\xe2\x80\x99s suit (which he was known to have adopted when he\r\nwent down there), by a copy of the New Testament, the gift of his wife,\r\nwhich was found, placed next his heart, with his hands crossed over it.\r\nIt was not thought prudent to remove the body, for burial elsewhere: and\r\naccordingly it was at once committed to the ground, along with four\r\nothers found in different houses, with all due reverence. His wife,\r\nwhose maiden name was Lady Muriel Orme, had been married to him on the\r\nvery morning on which he undertook his self-sacrificing mission._\r\n\r\n_Next we record the Rev. Walter Saunders, Wesleyan Minister. His death\r\nis believed to have taken place two or three weeks ago, as the words\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Died October 5\xe2\x80\x99 were found written on the wall of the room which he is\r\nknown to have occupied\xe2\x80\x94the house being shut up, and apparently not\r\nhaving been entered for some time._\r\n\r\n_Last\xe2\x80\x94though not a whit behind the other four in glorious self-denial\r\nand devotion to duty\xe2\x80\x94let us record the name of Father Francis, a young\r\nJesuit Priest who had been only a few months in the place. He had not\r\nbeen dead many hours when the exploring party came upon the body, which\r\nwas identified, beyond the possibility of doubt, by the dress, and by\r\nthe crucifix which was, like the young Doctor\xe2\x80\x99s Testament, clasped\r\nclosely to his heart._\r\n\r\n_Since reaching the hospital, two of the men and one of the children\r\nhave died. Hope is entertained for all the others: though there are two\r\nor three cases where the vital powers seem to be so entirely exhausted\r\nthat it is but \xe2\x80\x98hoping against hope\xe2\x80\x99 to regard ultimate recovery as even\r\npossible._\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XIX.\r\n                             A FAIRY-DUET.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe year\xe2\x80\x94what an eventful year it had been for me!\xe2\x80\x94was drawing to a\r\nclose, and the brief wintry day hardly gave light enough to recognise\r\nthe old familiar objects, bound up with so many happy memories, as the\r\ntrain glided round the last bend into the station, and the hoarse cry of\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cElveston! Elveston!\xe2\x80\x9d resounded along the platform.\r\n\r\nIt was sad to return to the place, and to feel that I should never again\r\nsee the glad smile of welcome, that had awaited me here so few months\r\nago. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet, if I were to find him here,\xe2\x80\x9d I muttered, as in solitary\r\nstate I followed the porter, who was wheeling my luggage on a barrow,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand if he _were_ to \xe2\x80\x98_strike a sudden hand in mine, And ask a thousand\r\nthings of home_,\xe2\x80\x99 I should not\xe2\x80\x94no, \xe2\x80\x98_I should not feel it to be\r\nstrange_\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHaving given directions to have my luggage taken to my old lodgings, I\r\nstrolled off alone, to pay a visit, before settling down in my own\r\nquarters, to my dear old friends\xe2\x80\x94for such I indeed felt them to be,\r\nthough it was barely half a year since first we met\xe2\x80\x94the Earl and his\r\nwidowed daughter.\r\n\r\nThe shortest way, as I well remembered, was to cross through the\r\nchurchyard. I pushed open the little wicket-gate and slowly took my way\r\namong the solemn memorials of the quiet dead, thinking of the many who\r\nhad, during the past year, disappeared from the place, and had gone to\r\n\xe2\x80\x98join the majority.\xe2\x80\x99 A very few steps brought me in sight of the object\r\nof my search. Lady Muriel, dressed in the deepest mourning, her face\r\nhidden by a long crape veil, was kneeling before a little marble cross,\r\nround which she was fastening a wreath of flowers.\r\n\r\nThe cross stood on a piece of level turf, unbroken by any mound, and I\r\nknew that it was simply a memorial-cross, for one whose dust reposed\r\nelsewhere, even before reading the simple inscription:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n                         _In loving Memory of_\r\n                         ARTHUR FORESTER, M.D.\r\n              _whose mortal remains lie buried by the sea:\r\n             whose spirit has returned to God who gave it_.\r\n\r\n               _\xe2\x80\x9cGreater love hath no man than this, that\r\n               a man lay down his life for his friends.\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\nShe threw back her veil on seeing me approach, and came forwards to meet\r\nme, with a quiet smile, and far more self-possessed than I could have\r\nexpected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is quite like old times, seeing _you_ here again!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in\r\ntones of genuine pleasure. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you been to see my father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cI was on my way there, and came through here as the\r\nshortest way. I hope he is well, and you also?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, we are both quite well. And you? Are you any better yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much better, I fear: but no worse, I am thankful to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us sit here awhile, and have a quiet chat,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. The\r\ncalmness\xe2\x80\x94almost indifference\xe2\x80\x94of her manner quite took me by surprise. I\r\nlittle guessed what a fierce restraint she was putting upon herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne can be so quiet here,\xe2\x80\x9d she resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cI come here every\xe2\x80\x94every day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very peaceful,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou got my letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but I delayed writing. It is so hard to say\xe2\x80\x94on _paper_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know. It was kind of you. You were with us when we saw the last of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe paused a moment, and went on more hurriedly. \xe2\x80\x9cI went down to the\r\nharbour several times, but no one knows which of those vast graves it\r\nis. However, they showed me the house he died in: that was some comfort.\r\nI stood in the very room where\xe2\x80\x94where\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d She struggled in vain to go on.\r\nThe flood-gates had given way at last, and the outburst of grief was the\r\nmost terrible I had ever witnessed. Totally regardless of my presence,\r\nshe flung herself down on the turf, burying her face in the grass, and\r\nwith her hands clasped round the little marble cross, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my darling,\r\nmy darling!\xe2\x80\x9d she sobbed. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd God meant your life to be so beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: IN THE CHURCH-YARD]\r\n\r\nI was startled to hear, thus repeated by Lady Muriel, the very words of\r\nthe darling child whom I had seen weeping so bitterly over the dead\r\nhare. Had some mysterious influence passed, from that sweet\r\nfairy-spirit, ere she went back to Fairyland, into the human spirit that\r\nloved her so dearly? The idea seemed too wild for belief. And yet, are\r\nthere not \xe2\x80\x98_more things in heaven and earth than are dreamt of in our\r\nphilosophy_\xe2\x80\x99?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod _meant_ it to be beautiful,\xe2\x80\x9d I whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cand surely it _was_\r\nbeautiful? God\xe2\x80\x99s purpose never fails!\xe2\x80\x9d I dared say no more, but rose and\r\nleft her. At the entrance-gate to the Earl\xe2\x80\x99s house I waited, leaning on\r\nthe gate and watching the sun set, revolving many memories\xe2\x80\x94some happy,\r\nsome sorrowful\xe2\x80\x94until Lady Muriel joined me.\r\n\r\nShe was quite calm again now. \xe2\x80\x9cDo come in,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cMy father will be\r\nso pleased to see you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man rose from his chair, with a smile, to welcome me; but his\r\nself-command was far less than his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s, and the tears coursed\r\ndown his face as he grasped both my hands in his, and pressed them\r\nwarmly.\r\n\r\nMy heart was too full to speak; and we all sat silent for a minute or\r\ntwo. Then Lady Muriel rang the bell for tea. \xe2\x80\x9cYou _do_ take five o\xe2\x80\x99clock\r\ntea, I know!\xe2\x80\x9d she said to me, with the sweet playfulness of manner I\r\nremembered so well, \xe2\x80\x9ceven though you _ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_ work your wicked will on\r\nthe Law of Gravity, and make the teacups descend into Infinite Space, a\r\nlittle faster than the tea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis remark gave the tone to our conversation. By a tacit mutual\r\nconsent, we avoided, during this our first meeting after her great\r\nsorrow, the painful topics that filled our thoughts, and talked like\r\nlight-hearted children who had never known a care.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever ask yourself the question,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel began, _\xc3\xa0 propos_\r\nof nothing, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is the _chief_ advantage of being a Man instead of a\r\nDog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cbut I think there are advantages on the _Dog\xe2\x80\x99s_\r\nside of the question, as well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, with that pretty mock-gravity that became her\r\nso well: \xe2\x80\x9cbut, on _Man\xe2\x80\x99s_ side, the chief advantage seems to me to\r\nconsist in _having pockets_! It was borne in upon me\xe2\x80\x94upon _us_, I should\r\nsay; for my father and I were returning from a walk\xe2\x80\x94only yesterday. We\r\nmet a dog carrying home a bone. What it wanted it for, I\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea:\r\ncertainly there was no _meat_ on it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA strange sensation came over me, that I had heard all this, or\r\nsomething exactly like it, before: and I almost expected her next words\r\nto be \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps he meant to make a cloak for the winter?\xe2\x80\x9d However what\r\nshe really said was \xe2\x80\x9cand my father tried to account for it by some\r\nwretched joke about _pro bono publico_. Well, the dog laid down the\r\nbone\xe2\x80\x94_not_ in disgust with the pun, which would have shown it to be a\r\ndog of taste\xe2\x80\x94but simply to rest its jaws, poor thing! I _did_ pity it\r\nso! Won\xe2\x80\x99t you join my _Charitable Association for supplying dogs with\r\npockets_? How would _you_ like to have to carry your walking-stick in\r\nyour mouth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIgnoring the difficult question as to the _raison d\xe2\x80\x99\xc3\xaatre_ of a\r\nwalking-stick, supposing one had no _hands_, I mentioned a curious\r\ninstance, I had once witnessed, of reasoning by a dog. A gentleman, with\r\na lady, and child, and a large dog, were down at the end of a pier on\r\nwhich I was walking. To amuse his child, I suppose, the gentleman put\r\ndown on the ground his umbrella and the lady\xe2\x80\x99s parasol, and then led the\r\nway to the other end of the pier, from which he sent the dog back for\r\nthe deserted articles. I was watching with some curiosity. The dog came\r\nracing back to where I stood, but found an unexpected difficulty in\r\npicking up the things it had come for. With the umbrella in its mouth,\r\nits jaws were so far apart that it could get no firm grip on the\r\nparasol. After two or three failures, it paused and considered the\r\nmatter.\r\n\r\nThen it put down the umbrella and began with the parasol. Of course that\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t open its jaws nearly so wide, and it was able to get a good hold\r\nof the umbrella, and galloped off in triumph. One couldn\xe2\x80\x99t doubt that it\r\nhad gone through a real train of logical thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI entirely agree with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel: \xe2\x80\x9cbut don\xe2\x80\x99t orthodox\r\nwriters condemn that view, as putting Man on the level of the lower\r\nanimals? Don\xe2\x80\x99t they draw a sharp boundary-line between Reason and\r\nInstinct?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat certainly _was_ the orthodox view, a generation ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nEarl. \xe2\x80\x9cThe truth of Religion seemed ready to stand or fall with the\r\nassertion that Man was the only reasoning animal. But that is at an end\r\nnow. Man can still claim _certain_ monopolies\xe2\x80\x94for instance, such a use\r\nof _language_ as enables us to utilise the work of many, by \xe2\x80\x98division of\r\nlabour.\xe2\x80\x99 But the belief, that we have a monopoly of _Reason_, has long\r\nbeen swept away. Yet no catastrophe has followed. As some old poet says,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_God is where he was_.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost religious believers would _now_ agree with Bishop Butler,\xe2\x80\x9d said I,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand not reject a line of argument, even if it led straight to the\r\nconclusion that animals have some kind of _soul_, which survives their\r\nbodily death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _would_ like to know _that_ to be true!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cIf\r\nonly for the sake of the poor horses. Sometimes I\xe2\x80\x99ve thought that, if\r\nanything _could_ make me cease to believe in a God of perfect justice,\r\nit would be the sufferings of horses\xe2\x80\x94without guilt to deserve it, and\r\nwithout any compensation!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only part of the great Riddle,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy innocent\r\nbeings _ever_ suffer. It _is_ a great strain on Faith\xe2\x80\x94but not a\r\n_breaking_ strain, I think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sufferings of _horses_,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9care chiefly caused by _Man\xe2\x80\x99s_\r\ncruelty. So _that_ is merely one of the many instances of Sin causing\r\nsuffering to others than the Sinner himself. But don\xe2\x80\x99t you find a\r\n_greater_ difficulty in sufferings inflicted by animals upon each other?\r\nFor instance, a cat playing with a mouse. Assuming it to have no _moral_\r\nresponsibility, isn\xe2\x80\x99t that a greater mystery than a man over-driving a\r\nhorse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it _is_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, looking a mute appeal to her\r\nfather.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat right have we to make that assumption?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl. \xe2\x80\x9c_Many_ of\r\nour religious difficulties are merely deductions from unwarranted\r\nassumptions. The wisest answer to most of them, is, I think, \xe2\x80\x98_behold,\r\nwe know not anything_.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mentioned \xe2\x80\x98division of labour,\xe2\x80\x99 just now,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cSurely it is\r\ncarried to a wonderful perfection in a hive of bees?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo wonderful\xe2\x80\x94so entirely super-human\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl, \xe2\x80\x9cand so entirely\r\ninconsistent with the intelligence they show in other ways\xe2\x80\x94that I feel\r\nno doubt at all that it is _pure_ Instinct, and _not_, as some hold, a\r\nvery high order of Reason. Look at the utter stupidity of a bee, trying\r\nto find its way out of an open window! It _doesn\xe2\x80\x99t_ try, in any\r\nreasonable sense of the word: it simply bangs itself about! We should\r\ncall a puppy _imbecile_, that behaved so. And yet we are asked to\r\nbelieve that its intellectual level is above Sir Isaac Newton!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you hold that _pure_ Instinct contains no _Reason_ at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Earl, \xe2\x80\x9cI hold that the work of a bee-hive\r\ninvolves Reason of the _highest_ order. But none of it is done by the\r\n_Bee_. _God_ has reasoned it all out, and has put into the mind of the\r\nBee the _conclusions_, only, of the reasoning process.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how do their minds come to work _together_?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat right have we to assume that they _have_ minds?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpecial pleading, special pleading!\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel cried, in a most\r\nunfilial tone of triumph. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you yourself said, just now, \xe2\x80\x98the mind\r\nof the Bee\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I did _not_ say \xe2\x80\x98_minds_,\xe2\x80\x99 my child,\xe2\x80\x9d the Earl gently replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nhas occurred to me, as the most probable solution of the \xe2\x80\x98Bee\xe2\x80\x99-mystery,\r\nthat a swarm of Bees _have only one mind among them_. We often see one\r\nmind animating a most complex collection of limbs and organs, _when\r\njoined together_. How do we know that any material connection is\r\nnecessary? May not mere neighbourhood be enough? If so, a swarm of bees\r\nis simply a single animal whose many limbs are not quite close\r\ntogether!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a bewildering thought,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cand needs a night\xe2\x80\x99s rest to\r\ngrasp it properly. Reason and Instinct _both_ tell me I ought to go\r\nhome. So, good-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll \xe2\x80\x98set\xe2\x80\x99 you part of the way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had no walk\r\nto-day. It will do me good, and I have more to say to you. Shall we go\r\nthrough the wood? It will be pleasanter than over the common, even\r\nthough it _is_ getting a little dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe turned aside into the shade of interlacing boughs, which formed an\r\narchitecture of almost perfect symmetry, grouped into lovely groined\r\narches, or running out, far as the eye could follow, into endless\r\naisles, and chancels, and naves, like some ghostly cathedral, fashioned\r\nout of the dream of a moon-struck poet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways, in this wood,\xe2\x80\x9d she began after a pause (silence seemed natural\r\nin this dim solitude), \xe2\x80\x9cI begin thinking of Fairies! May I ask you a\r\nquestion?\xe2\x80\x9d she added hesitatingly. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you believe in Fairies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe momentary impulse was so strong to tell her of my experiences in\r\nthis very wood, that I had to make a real effort to keep back the words\r\nthat rushed to my lips. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you mean, by \xe2\x80\x98believe,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98believe in their\r\n_possible_ existence,\xe2\x80\x99 I say \xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99 For their _actual_ existence, of\r\ncourse, one would need _evidence_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were saying, the other day,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you would accept\r\n_anything_, on good evidence, that was not _\xc3\xa0 priori_ impossible. And I\r\nthink you named _Ghosts_ as an instance of a _provable_ phenomenon.\r\nWould _Fairies_ be another instance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think so.\xe2\x80\x9d And again it was hard to check the wish to say more:\r\nbut I was not yet sure of a sympathetic listener.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have you any theory as to what sort of place they would occupy in\r\nCreation? Do tell me what you think about them! Would they, for instance\r\n(supposing such beings to exist), would they have any moral\r\nresponsibility? I mean\xe2\x80\x9d (and the light bantering tone suddenly changed\r\nto one of deep seriousness) \xe2\x80\x9cwould they be capable of _sin_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey can reason\xe2\x80\x94on a lower level, perhaps, than men and women\xe2\x80\x94never\r\nrising, I think, above the faculties of a child; and they have a moral\r\nsense, most surely. Such a being, without _free will_, would be an\r\nabsurdity. So I am driven to the conclusion that they _are_ capable of\r\nsin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou believe in them?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried delightedly, with a sudden motion as if\r\nabout to clap her hands. \xe2\x80\x9cNow tell me, have you any reason for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd still I strove to keep back the revelation I felt sure was coming.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe that there is _life_ everywhere\xe2\x80\x94not _material_ only, not\r\nmerely what is palpable to our senses\xe2\x80\x94but immaterial and invisible as\r\nwell. We believe in our own immaterial essence\xe2\x80\x94call it \xe2\x80\x98soul,\xe2\x80\x99 or\r\n\xe2\x80\x98spirit,\xe2\x80\x99 or what you will. Why should not other similar essences exist\r\naround us, _not_ linked on to a visible and _material_ body? Did not God\r\nmake this swarm of happy insects, to dance in this sunbeam for one hour\r\nof bliss, for no other object, that we can imagine, than to swell the\r\nsum of conscious happiness? And where shall we dare to draw the line,\r\nand say \xe2\x80\x98He has made all these and no more\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d she assented, watching me with sparkling eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cBut these\r\nare only reasons for not _denying_. You have more reasons than this,\r\nhave you not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, feeling I might safely tell all now. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I could\r\nnot find a fitter time or place to say it. I have _seen_ them\xe2\x80\x94and in\r\nthis very wood!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel asked no more questions. Silently she paced at my side, with\r\nhead bowed down and hands clasped tightly together. Only, as my tale\r\nwent on, she drew a little short quick breath now and then, like a child\r\npanting with delight. And I told her what I had never yet breathed to\r\nany other listener, of my double life, and, more than that (for _mine_\r\nmight have been but a noonday-dream), of the double life of those two\r\ndear children.\r\n\r\nAnd when I told her of Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s wild gambols, she laughed merrily; and\r\nwhen I spoke of Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s sweetness and her utter unselfishness and\r\ntrustful love, she drew a deep breath, like one who hears at last some\r\nprecious tidings for which the heart has ached for a long while; and the\r\nhappy tears chased one another down her cheeks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have often longed to meet an angel,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered, so low that I\r\ncould hardly catch the words. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m _so_ glad I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen Sylvie! My heart\r\nwent out to the child the first moment that I saw her\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 Listen!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nbroke off suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Sylvie singing! I\xe2\x80\x99m sure of it! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nknow her voice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard _Bruno_ sing, more than once,\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cbut I never heard\r\nSylvie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have only heard her _once_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was that day when\r\nyou brought us those mysterious flowers. The children had run out into\r\nthe garden; and I saw Eric coming in that way, and went to the window to\r\nmeet him: and Sylvie was singing, under the trees, a song I had never\r\nheard before. The words were something like \xe2\x80\x98I think it is Love, I feel\r\nit is Love.\xe2\x80\x99 Her voice sounded far away, like a dream, but it was\r\nbeautiful beyond all words\xe2\x80\x94as sweet as an infant\xe2\x80\x99s first smile, or the\r\nfirst gleam of the white cliffs when one is coming _home_ after weary\r\nyears\xe2\x80\x94a voice that seemed to fill one\xe2\x80\x99s whole being with peace and\r\nheavenly thoughts\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 Listen!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, breaking off again in her\r\nexcitement. \xe2\x80\x9cThat _is_ her voice, and that\xe2\x80\x99s the very song!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could distinguish no words, but there was a dreamy sense of music in\r\nthe air that seemed to grow ever louder and louder, as if coming nearer\r\nto us. We stood quite silent, and in another minute the two children\r\nappeared, coming straight towards us through an arched opening among the\r\ntrees. Each had an arm round the other, and the setting sun shed a\r\ngolden halo round their heads, like what one sees in pictures of saints.\r\nThey were looking in our direction, but evidently did not see us, and I\r\nsoon made out that Lady Muriel had for once passed into a condition\r\nfamiliar to _me_, that we were both of us \xe2\x80\x98eerie,\xe2\x80\x99 and that, though we\r\ncould see the children so plainly, we were quite invisible to _them_.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: A FAIRY-DUET]\r\n\r\nThe song ceased just as they came into sight: but, to my delight, Bruno\r\ninstantly said \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s sing it all again, Sylvie! It _did_ sound so\r\npretty!\xe2\x80\x9d And Sylvie replied \xe2\x80\x9cVery well. It\xe2\x80\x99s _you_ to begin, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Bruno began, in the sweet childish treble I knew so well:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cSay, what is the spell, when her fledgelings are cheeping,\r\n    That lures the bird home to her nest?\r\n  Or wakes the tired mother, whose infant is weeping,\r\n    To cuddle and croon it to rest?\r\n  What\xe2\x80\x99s the magic that charms the glad babe in her arms,\r\n    Till it cooes with the voice of the dove?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd now ensued quite the strangest of all the strange experiences that\r\nmarked the wonderful year whose history I am writing\xe2\x80\x94the experience of\r\n_first_ hearing Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s voice in song. Her part was a very short\r\none\xe2\x80\x94only a few words\xe2\x80\x94and she sang it timidly, and very low indeed,\r\nscarcely audibly, but the _sweetness_ of her voice was simply\r\nindescribable; I have never heard any earthly music like it.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a secret, and so let us whisper it low\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    And the name of the secret is Love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn me the first effect of her voice was a sudden sharp pang that seemed\r\nto pierce through one\xe2\x80\x99s very heart. (I had felt such a pang only once\r\nbefore in my life, and it had been from _seeing_ what, at the moment,\r\nrealised one\xe2\x80\x99s idea of perfect beauty\xe2\x80\x94it was in a London exhibition,\r\nwhere, in making my way through a crowd, I suddenly met, face to face, a\r\nchild of quite unearthly beauty.) Then came a rush of burning tears to\r\nthe eyes, as though one could weep one\xe2\x80\x99s soul away for pure delight. And\r\nlastly there fell on me a sense of awe that was almost terror\xe2\x80\x94some such\r\nfeeling as Moses must have had when he heard the words \xe2\x80\x9c_Put off thy\r\nshoes from off thy feet, for the place whereon thou standest is holy\r\nground_.\xe2\x80\x9d The figures of the children became vague and shadowy, like\r\nglimmering meteors: while their voices rang together in exquisite\r\nharmony as they sang:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFor I think it is Love,\r\n      For I feel it is Love,\r\n  For I\xe2\x80\x99m sure it is nothing but Love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time I could see them clearly once more. Bruno again sang by\r\nhimself:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cSay, whence is the voice that, when anger is burning,\r\n    Bids the whirl of the tempest to cease?\r\n  That stirs the vexed soul with an aching\xe2\x80\x94a yearning\r\n    For the brotherly hand-grip of peace?\r\n  Whence the music that fills all our being\xe2\x80\x94that thrills\r\n    Around us, beneath, and above?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie sang more courageously, this time: the words seemed to carry her\r\naway, out of herself:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a secret: none knows how it comes, how it goes:\r\n    But the name of the secret is Love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd clear and strong the chorus rang out:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFor I think it is Love,\r\n      For I feel it is Love,\r\n  For I\xe2\x80\x99m sure it is nothing but Love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce more we heard Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s delicate little voice alone:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cSay whose is the skill that paints valley and hill,\r\n    Like a picture so fair to the sight?\r\n  That flecks the green meadow with sunshine and shadow,\r\n    Till the little lambs leap with delight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd again uprose that silvery voice, whose angelic sweetness I could\r\nhardly bear:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis a secret untold to hearts cruel and cold,\r\n    Though \xe2\x80\x99tis sung, by the angels above,\r\n  In notes that ring clear for the ears that can hear\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    And the name of the secret is Love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then Bruno joined in again with\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFor I think it is Love,\r\n      For I feel it is Love,\r\n  For I\xe2\x80\x99m sure it is nothing but Love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat _are_ pretty!\xe2\x80\x9d the little fellow exclaimed, as the children passed\r\nus\xe2\x80\x94so closely that we drew back a little to make room for them, and it\r\nseemed we had only to reach out a hand to touch them: but this we did\r\nnot attempt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo use to try and stop them!\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as they passed away into the\r\nshadows. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, they could not even _see_ us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo use at all,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel echoed with a sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cOne would _like_ to\r\nmeet them again, in living form! But I feel, somehow, _that_ can never\r\nbe. They have passed out of _our_ lives!\xe2\x80\x9d She sighed again; and no more\r\nwas said, till we came out into the main road, at a point near my\r\nlodgings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I will leave you here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to get back before\r\ndark: and I have a cottage-friend to visit, first. Good night, dear\r\nfriend! Let us see you soon\xe2\x80\x94and often!\xe2\x80\x9d she added, with an affectionate\r\nwarmth that went to my very heart. \xe2\x80\x9c_For those are few we hold as\r\ndear!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood night!\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cTennyson said that of a worthier friend than\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTennyson didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what he was talking about!\xe2\x80\x9d she saucily rejoined,\r\nwith a touch of her old childish gaiety; and we parted.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XX.\r\n                          GAMMON AND SPINACH.\r\n\r\n\r\nMy landlady\xe2\x80\x99s welcome had an extra heartiness about it: and though, with\r\na rare delicacy of feeling, she made no direct allusion to the friend\r\nwhose companionship had done so much to brighten life for me, I felt\r\nsure that it was a kindly sympathy with my solitary state that made her\r\nso specially anxious to do all she could think of to ensure my comfort,\r\nand make me feel at home.\r\n\r\nThe lonely evening seemed long and tedious: yet I lingered on, watching\r\nthe dying fire, and letting Fancy mould the red embers into the forms\r\nand faces belonging to bygone scenes. Now it seemed to be Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroguish smile that sparkled for a moment, and died away: now it was\r\nSylvie\xe2\x80\x99s rosy cheek: and now the Professor\xe2\x80\x99s jolly round face, beaming\r\nwith delight. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re welcome, my little ones!\xe2\x80\x9d he seemed to say. And\r\nthen the red coal, which for the moment embodied the dear old Professor,\r\nbegan to wax dim, and with its dying lustre the words seemed to die away\r\ninto silence. I seized the poker, and with an artful touch or two\r\nrevived the waning glow, while Fancy\xe2\x80\x94no coy minstrel she\xe2\x80\x94sang me once\r\nagain the magic strain I loved to hear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re welcome, little ones!\xe2\x80\x9d the cheery voice repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cI told them\r\nyou were coming. Your rooms are all ready for you. And the Emperor and\r\nthe Empress\xe2\x80\x94well, I think they\xe2\x80\x99re rather pleased than otherwise! In\r\nfact, Her Highness said \xe2\x80\x98I hope they\xe2\x80\x99ll be in time for the Banquet!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nThose were her very words, I assure you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill Uggug be at the Banquet?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno asked. And both children looked\r\nuneasy at the dismal suggestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course he will!\xe2\x80\x9d chuckled the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s his\r\n_birthday_, don\xe2\x80\x99t you know? And his health will be drunk, and all that\r\nsort of thing. What would the Banquet be without _him_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEver so much nicer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. But he said it in a _very_ low voice,\r\nand nobody but Sylvie heard him.\r\n\r\nThe Professor chuckled again. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be a jolly Banquet, now _you\xe2\x80\x99ve_\r\ncome, my little man! I _am_ so glad to see you again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI \xe2\x80\x99fraid we\xe2\x80\x99ve been very long in coming,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno politely remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor assented. \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, you\xe2\x80\x99re very short now\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re come: that\xe2\x80\x99s _some_ comfort.\xe2\x80\x9d And he went on to enumerate the\r\nplans for the day. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Lecture comes first,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9c_That_ the\r\nEmpress _insists_ on. She says people will eat so much at the Banquet,\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll be too sleepy to attend to the Lecture afterwards\xe2\x80\x94and perhaps\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s right. There\xe2\x80\x99ll just be a little _refreshment_, when the people\r\nfirst arrive\xe2\x80\x94as a kind of surprise for the Empress, you know. Ever since\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s been\xe2\x80\x94well, not _quite_ so clever as she once was\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99ve found it\r\ndesirable to concoct little surprises for her. _Then_ comes the\r\nLecture\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? The Lecture you were getting ready\xe2\x80\x94ever so long ago?\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie\r\nenquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the one,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor rather reluctantly admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\n_has_ taken a goodish time to prepare. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got so many other things to\r\nattend to. For instance, I\xe2\x80\x99m Court-Physician. I have to keep all the\r\nRoyal Servants in good health\xe2\x80\x94and that reminds me!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, ringing\r\nthe bell in a great hurry. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is Medicine-Day! We only give Medicine\r\nonce a week. If we were to begin giving it every day, the bottles would\r\n_soon_ be empty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if they were ill on the _other_ days?\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, ill on the wrong _day_!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, that would\r\nnever do! A Servant would be dismissed _at once_, who was ill on the\r\nwrong day! This is the Medicine for _today_,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, taking down a\r\nlarge jug from a shelf. \xe2\x80\x9cI mixed it, myself, first thing this morning.\r\nTaste it!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, holding out the jug to Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cDip in your finger,\r\nand taste it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno did so, and made such an excruciatingly wry face that Sylvie\r\nexclaimed, in alarm, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno, you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s welly extremely nasty!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said, as his face resumed its\r\nnatural shape.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNasty?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of _course_ it is! Tell them not to begin, on any account, till\r\nI come! It\xe2\x80\x99s really _wonderful_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning to the children, \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nsuccess I\xe2\x80\x99ve had in curing Diseases! But they _both_ happened: there\xe2\x80\x99s no doubt of _that_. The\r\n_Medicine\xe2\x80\x99s_ the great thing, you know. The _Diseases_ are much less\r\nimportant. You can keep a _Medicine_, for years and years: but nobody\r\never wants to keep a _Disease_! By the way, come and look at the\r\nplatform. The Gardener asked me to come and see if it would do. We may\r\nas well go before it gets dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99d like to, very much!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie replied. \xe2\x80\x9cCome, Bruno, put on your\r\nhat. Don\xe2\x80\x99t keep the dear Professor waiting!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCa\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t find my hat!\xe2\x80\x9d the little fellow sadly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cI were rolling\r\nit about. He looked round at us, but said nothing\r\ntill he had cracked and eaten the nut. Then he asked the old question.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the Lecture all ready?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll begin in an hour,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor said, evading the question.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst, we must have something to surprise the Empress. And then comes\r\nthe Banquet\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Banquet!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Other Professor, springing up, and filling the\r\nroom with a cloud of dust. \xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99d better go and\xe2\x80\x94and brush myself a\r\nlittle. What a state I\xe2\x80\x99m in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe _does_ want brushing!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor said, with a critical air,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s your hat, little man! I had put it on by mistake. I\xe2\x80\x99d quite\r\nforgotten I had _one_ on, already. Let\xe2\x80\x99s go and look at the platform.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s that nice old Gardener singing still!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno exclaimed in\r\ndelight, as we went out into the garden. \xe2\x80\x9cI do believe he\xe2\x80\x99s been singing\r\nthat very song ever since we went away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course he has!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be the\r\nthing to leave off, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be _what_ thing?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno: but the Professor thought it\r\nbest not to hear the question. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing with that hedgehog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe shouted at the Gardener, whom they found standing upon one foot,\r\nsinging softly to himself, and rolling a hedgehog up and down with the\r\nother foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wanted fur to know what hedgehogs lives on: so I be a-keeping\r\nthis here hedgehog\xe2\x80\x94fur to see if it eats potatoes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch better keep a potato,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor; \xe2\x80\x9cand see if hedgehogs\r\neat it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat be the roight way, sure-ly!\xe2\x80\x9d the delighted Gardener exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cBe\r\nyou come to see the platform?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAye, aye!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor cheerily replied. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the children have come\r\nback, you see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Gardener looked round at them with a grin. Then he led the way to\r\nthe Pavilion; and as he went he sang:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cHe looked again, and found it was\r\n    A Double Rule of Three:\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98And all its Mystery,\xe2\x80\x99 he said,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98Is clear as day to me!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been _months_ over that song,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it\r\nfinished yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere be only one verse more,\xe2\x80\x9d the Gardener sadly replied. And, with\r\ntears streaming down his cheeks, he sang the last verse:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cHe thought he saw an Argument\r\n    That proved he was the Pope:\r\n  He looked again, and found it was\r\n    A Bar of Mottled Soap.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x98A fact so dread,\xe2\x80\x99 he faintly said,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98Extinguishes all hope!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nChoking with sobs, the Gardener hastily stepped on a few yards ahead of\r\nthe party, to conceal his emotion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid _he_ see the Bar of Mottled Soap?\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie enquired, as we followed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, certainly!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cThat song is his own history, you\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTears of an ever-ready sympathy glittered in Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99s _welly_\r\nsorry he isn\xe2\x80\x99t the Pope!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t _you_ sorry, Sylvie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I hardly know,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie replied in the vaguest manner. \xe2\x80\x9cWould it\r\nmake him any happier?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked the Professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make the _Pope_ any happier,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthe platform _lovely_?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, as we entered the Pavilion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve put an extra beam under it!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Gardener, patting it\r\naffectionately as he spoke. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now it\xe2\x80\x99s that strong, as\xe2\x80\x94as a mad\r\nelephant might dance upon it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you _very_ much!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor heartily rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nthat we shall exactly require\xe2\x80\x94but it\xe2\x80\x99s convenient to know.\xe2\x80\x9d And he led\r\nthe children upon the platform, to explain the arrangements to them.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere are three seats, you see, for the Emperor and the Empress and\r\nPrince Uggug. The\r\nrest of the Saloon had been cleared of furniture, to make room for the\r\nguests. I was much struck by the great change a few months had made in\r\nthe faces of the Imperial Pair. A vacant stare was now the _Emperor\xe2\x80\x99s_\r\nusual expression; while over the face of the _Empress_ there flitted,\r\never and anon, a meaningless smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you\xe2\x80\x99re come at last!\xe2\x80\x9d the Emperor sulkily remarked, as the Professor\r\nand the children took their places. Then all the\r\nflags were dropped in a moment, as the Head-Cook raised the cover from\r\nan enormous dish.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98HER IMPERIAL HIGHNESS IS SURPRISED!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d the Empress said faintly, as she put her spy-glass to her\r\neye. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s _Spinach_, I declare!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer Imperial Highness is surprised,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor explained to the\r\nattendants: and some of them clapped their hands. The Head-Cook made a\r\nlow bow, and in doing so dropped a spoon on the table, as if by\r\naccident, just within reach of the Empress, who looked the other way and\r\npretended not to see it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _am_ surprised!\xe2\x80\x9d the Empress said to Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cI heard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d but Sylvie put her hand over his\r\nmouth, and spoke for him. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s rather tired, I think. He wants the\r\nLecture to begin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want the _supper_ to begin,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno corrected her.\r\n\r\nThe Empress took up the spoon in an absent manner, and tried to balance\r\nit across the back of her hand, and in doing this she dropped it into\r\nthe dish: and, when she took it out again, it was full of spinach. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\ncurious!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and put it into her mouth. \xe2\x80\x9cIt tastes just like\r\n_real_ spinach! I thought it was an imitation\xe2\x80\x94but I do believe it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreal!\xe2\x80\x9d And she took another spoonful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t be real much longer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\nBut the Empress had had enough spinach by this time, and somehow\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nfailed to notice the exact process\xe2\x80\x94we all found ourselves in the\r\nPavilion, and the Professor in the act of beginning the long-expected\r\nLecture.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XXI.\r\n                        THE PROFESSOR\xe2\x80\x99S LECTURE.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Science\xe2\x80\x94in fact, in most things\xe2\x80\x94it is usually best _to begin at the\r\nbeginning_. In _some_ things, of course, it\xe2\x80\x99s better to begin at the\r\n_other_ end. For instance, if you wanted to paint a dog green, it\r\n_might_ be best to begin with the _tail_, as it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t bite at _that_\r\nend. And so\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay _I_ help oo?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno interrupted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHelp me to do _what_?\xe2\x80\x9d said the puzzled Professor, looking up for a\r\nmoment, but keeping his finger on the book he was reading from, so as\r\nnot to lose his place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo paint a dog green!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9c_Oo_ can begin wiz its _mouf_, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cWe haven\xe2\x80\x99t got to the _Experiments_ yet.\r\nAnd so,\xe2\x80\x9d returning to his note-book, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll give you the Axioms of\r\nScience. After that I shall exhibit some Specimens. Then I shall explain\r\na Process or two. And I shall conclude with a few Experiments. An\r\n_Axiom_, you know, is a thing that you accept without contradiction. For\r\ninstance, if I were to say \xe2\x80\x98Here we are!\xe2\x80\x99, that would be accepted\r\nwithout any contradiction, and it\xe2\x80\x99s a nice sort of remark to _begin_ a\r\nconversation with. So it would be an _Axiom_. Or again, supposing I were\r\nto say \xe2\x80\x98Here we are not!\xe2\x80\x99 _that_ would be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94a fib!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _Bruno_!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie in a warning whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course it would be\r\nan _Axiom_, if the Professor said it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94that would be accepted, if people were civil,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the\r\nProfessor; \xe2\x80\x9cso it would be _another_ Axiom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _might_ be an Axledum,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said: \xe2\x80\x9cbut it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be _true_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIgnorance of Axioms,\xe2\x80\x9d the Lecturer continued, \xe2\x80\x9cis a great drawback in\r\nlife. It wastes so much time to have to say them over and over again.\r\nFor instance, take the Axiom \xe2\x80\x98_Nothing is greater than itself_\xe2\x80\x99; that\r\nis, \xe2\x80\x98_Nothing can contain itself_.\xe2\x80\x99 How often you hear people say \xe2\x80\x98He\r\nwas so excited, he was quite unable to contain himself,\xe2\x80\x99 Why, _of\r\ncourse_ he was unable! The _excitement_ had nothing to do with it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, look here, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Emperor, who was getting a little\r\nrestless. \xe2\x80\x9cHow many Axioms are you going to give us? At _this_ rate, we\r\nsha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t get to the _Experiments_ till to-morrow-week!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, sooner than _that_, I assure you!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor replied, looking\r\nup in alarm. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are only,\xe2\x80\x9d (he referred to his notes again) \xe2\x80\x9conly\r\n_two_ more, that are really _necessary_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRead \xe2\x80\x99em out, and get on to the _Specimens_,\xe2\x80\x9d grumbled the Emperor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _First_ Axiom,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor read out in a great hurry, \xe2\x80\x9cconsists\r\nof these words, \xe2\x80\x98_Whatever is, is_.\xe2\x80\x99 And the Second consists of _these_\r\nwords, \xe2\x80\x98_Whatever isn\xe2\x80\x99t, isn\xe2\x80\x99t_.\xe2\x80\x99 We will now go on to the _Specimens_.\r\nThe first tray contains Crystals and other Things.\xe2\x80\x9d He drew it towards\r\nhim, and again referred to his note-book. \xe2\x80\x9cSome of the labels\xe2\x80\x94owing to\r\ninsufficient adhesion\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Here he stopped again, and carefully examined\r\nthe page with his eyeglass. \xe2\x80\x9cI ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t quite read the rest of the\r\nsentence,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it _means_ that the labels have come\r\nloose, and the Things have got mixed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet _me_ stick \xe2\x80\x99em on again!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno eagerly, and began licking\r\nthem, like postage-stamps, and dabbing them down upon the Crystals and\r\nthe other Things. But the Professor hastily moved the tray out of his\r\nreach. \xe2\x80\x9cThey _might_ get fixed to the _wrong_ Specimens, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOo shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have any _wrong_ peppermints in the tray!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno boldly\r\nreplied. \xe2\x80\x9c_Should_ he, Sylvie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Sylvie only shook her head.\r\n\r\nThe Professor heard him not. He had taken up one of the bottles, and was\r\ncarefully reading the label through his eye-glass. \xe2\x80\x9cOur first\r\nSpecimen\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he announced, as he placed the bottle in front of the other\r\nThings, \xe2\x80\x9cis\xe2\x80\x94that is, it is called\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d here he took it up, and examined\r\nthe label again, as if he thought it might have changed since he last\r\nsaw it, \xe2\x80\x9cis called Aqua Pura\xe2\x80\x94common water\xe2\x80\x94the fluid that cheers\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHip!
