Nobody ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t go among my bones. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t room!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur Second Experiment,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor announced, as Bruno returned to\r\nhis place, still thoughtfully rubbing his elbows, \xe2\x80\x9cis the production of\r\nthat seldom-seen-but-greatly-to-be-admired phenomenon, Black Light! You\r\nhave seen White Light, Red Light, Green Light, and so on: but never,\r\ntill this wonderful day, have any eyes but mine seen _Black Light_! This\r\nbox,\xe2\x80\x9d carefully lifting it upon the table, and covering it with a heap\r\nof blankets, \xe2\x80\x9cis quite full of it. The way I made it was this\xe2\x80\x94I took a\r\nlighted candle into a dark cupboard and shut the door. Of course the\r\ncupboard was then full of _Yellow_ Light. Then I took a bottle of Black\r\nink, and poured it over the candle: and, to my delight, every atom of\r\nthe Yellow Light turned _Black_! That was indeed the proudest moment of\r\nmy life! Then I filled a box with it. And now\xe2\x80\x94would any one like to get\r\nunder the blankets and see it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDead silence followed this appeal: but at last Bruno said \xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ get\r\nunder, if it won\xe2\x80\x99t jingle my elbows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSatisfied on this point, Bruno crawled under the blankets, and, after a\r\nminute or two, crawled out again, very hot and dusty, and with his hair\r\nin the wildest confusion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you see in the box?\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie eagerly enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw _nuffin_!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno sadly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIt were too dark!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has described the appearance of the thing exactly!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor\r\nexclaimed with enthusiasm. \xe2\x80\x9cBlack Light, and Nothing, look so extremely\r\nalike, at first sight, that I don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder he failed to distinguish\r\nthem! We will now proceed to the Third Experiment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Professor came down, and led the way to where a post had been driven\r\nfirmly into the ground. To one side of the post was fastened a chain,\r\nwith an iron weight hooked on to the end of it, and from the other side\r\nprojected a piece of whalebone, with a ring at the end of it. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a\r\n_most_ interesting Experiment!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor announced. \xe2\x80\x9cIt will need\r\n_time_, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid: but that is a trifling disadvantage. Now observe. If\r\nI were to unhook this weight, and let go, it would fall to the ground.\r\nYou do not deny _that_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNobody denied it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd in the same way, if I were to bend this piece of whalebone round\r\nthe post\xe2\x80\x94thus\xe2\x80\x94and put the ring over this hook\xe2\x80\x94thus\xe2\x80\x94it stays bent: but,\r\nif I unhook it, it straightens itself again. You do not deny _that_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain, nobody denied it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, suppose we left things just as they are, for a long time.\r\nThe force of the _whalebone_ would get exhausted, you know, and it would\r\nstay bent, even when you unhooked it. Now, _why_ shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t the same\r\nthing happen with the _weight_? The _whalebone_ gets so used to being\r\nbent, that it ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t _straighten_ itself any more. Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\n_weight_ get so used to being held up, that it ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t _fall_ any more?\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s what _I_ want to know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what _we_ want to know!\xe2\x80\x9d echoed the crowd.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long must we wait?\xe2\x80\x9d grumbled the Emperor.\r\n\r\nThe Professor looked at his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I _think_ a thousand years\r\nwill do to _begin_ with,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThen we will cautiously unhook the\r\nweight: and, if it _still_ shows (as perhaps it will) a _slight_\r\ntendency to fall, we will hook it on to the chain again, and leave it\r\nfor _another_ thousand years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the Empress experienced one of those flashes of Common Sense which\r\nwere the surprise of all around her. \xe2\x80\x9cMeanwhile there\xe2\x80\x99ll be time for\r\nanother Experiment,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere will _indeed_!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the delighted Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us return to\r\nthe platform, and proceed to the _Fourth_ Experiment!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor this concluding Experiment, I will take a certain Alkali, or Acid\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nforget which. Now you\xe2\x80\x99ll see what will happen when I mix it with Some\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhere he took up a bottle, and looked at it doubtfully, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94when I mix it\r\nwith\xe2\x80\x94with Something\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the Emperor interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the _name_ of the stuff?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t remember the _name_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor: \xe2\x80\x9cand the label has\r\ncome off.\xe2\x80\x9d He emptied it quickly into the other bottle, and, with a\r\ntremendous bang, both bottles flew to pieces, upsetting all the\r\nmachines, and filling the Pavilion with thick black smoke. I sprang to\r\nmy feet in terror, and\xe2\x80\x94and found myself standing before my solitary\r\nhearth, where the poker, dropping at last from the hand of the sleeper,\r\nhad knocked over the tongs and the shovel, and had upset the kettle,\r\nfilling the air with clouds of steam. With a weary sigh, I betook myself\r\nto bed.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: AN EXPLOSION]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER XXII.\r\n                              THE BANQUET.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Heaviness may endure for a night: but joy cometh in the morning._\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nnext day found me quite another being. Even the memories of my lost\r\nfriend and companion were sunny as the genial weather that smiled around\r\nme. I did not venture to trouble Lady Muriel, or her father, with\r\nanother call so soon: but took a walk into the country, and only turned\r\nhomewards when the low sunbeams warned me that day would soon be over.\r\n\r\nOn my way home, I passed the cottage where the old man lived, whose face\r\nalways recalled to me the day when I first met Lady Muriel; and I\r\nglanced in as I passed, half-curious to see if he were still living\r\nthere.\r\n\r\nYes: the old man was still alive. He was sitting out in the porch,\r\nlooking just as he did when I first saw him at Fayfield Junction\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nseemed only a few days ago!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening!\xe2\x80\x9d I said, pausing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, Maister!\xe2\x80\x9d he cheerfully responded. \xe2\x80\x9cWo\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t ee step in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI stepped in, and took a seat on the bench in the porch. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad to\r\nsee you looking so hearty,\xe2\x80\x9d I began. \xe2\x80\x9cLast time, I remember, I chanced\r\nto pass just as Lady Muriel was coming away from the house. Does she\r\nstill come to see you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEes,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has na forgotten me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t lose her\r\nbonny face for many days together. Well I mind the very first time she\r\ncome, after we\xe2\x80\x99d met at Railway Station. She told me as she come to mak\xe2\x80\x99\r\namends. Dear child! Only think o\xe2\x80\x99 that! To mak\xe2\x80\x99 amends!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo make amends for what?\xe2\x80\x9d I enquired. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat could _she_ have done to\r\nneed it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it were loike this, you see? We were both on us a-waiting fur t\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntrain at t\xe2\x80\x99 Junction. And I had setten mysen down upat t\xe2\x80\x99 bench. And\r\nStation-Maister, _he_ comes and he orders me off\xe2\x80\x94fur t\xe2\x80\x99 mak\xe2\x80\x99 room for\r\nher Ladyship, you understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember it all,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI was there myself, that day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Was_ you, now? Well, an\xe2\x80\x99 she axes my pardon fur\xe2\x80\x99t. Think o\xe2\x80\x99 that, now!\r\n_My_ pardon! An owd ne\xe2\x80\x99er-do-weel like me! Ah! She\xe2\x80\x99s been here many a\r\ntime, sin\xe2\x80\x99 then. Why, she were in here only yestere\xe2\x80\x99en, as it were,\r\nasittin\xe2\x80\x99, as it might be, where you\xe2\x80\x99re a-sitting now, an\xe2\x80\x99 lookin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsweeter and kinder nor an angel! An\xe2\x80\x99 she says \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99ve not got your\r\nMinnie, now,\xe2\x80\x99 she says, \xe2\x80\x98to fettle for ye.\xe2\x80\x99 Minnie was my\r\ngrand-daughter, Sir, as lived wi\xe2\x80\x99 me. She died, a matter of two months\r\nago\xe2\x80\x94or it may be three. She was a bonny lass\xe2\x80\x94and a good lass, too. Eh,\r\nbut life has been rare an\xe2\x80\x99 lonely without her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe covered his face in his hands: and I waited a minute or two, in\r\nsilence, for him to recover himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo she says \xe2\x80\x98Just tak\xe2\x80\x99 _me_ fur your Minnie!\xe2\x80\x99 she says. \xe2\x80\x98Didna Minnie\r\nmak\xe2\x80\x99 your tea fur you?\xe2\x80\x99 says she. \xe2\x80\x98Ay,\xe2\x80\x99 says I. An she mak\xe2\x80\x99s the tea.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98An\xe2\x80\x99 didna Minnie light your pipe?\xe2\x80\x99 says she. \xe2\x80\x98Ay,\xe2\x80\x99 says I. An\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nlights the pipe for me. \xe2\x80\x98An\xe2\x80\x99 didna Minnie set out your tea in t\xe2\x80\x99 porch?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAn\xe2\x80\x99 I says \xe2\x80\x98My dear,\xe2\x80\x99 I says, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m thinking you\xe2\x80\x99re Minnie hersen!\xe2\x80\x99 An\xe2\x80\x99\r\nshe cries a bit. We both on us cries a bit\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain I kept silence for a while.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 while I smokes my pipe, she sits an\xe2\x80\x99 talks to me\xe2\x80\x94as loving an\xe2\x80\x99 as\r\npleasant! I\xe2\x80\x99ll be bound I thowt it were Minnie come again! An\xe2\x80\x99 when she\r\ngets up to go, I says \xe2\x80\x98Winnot ye shak\xe2\x80\x99 hands wi\xe2\x80\x99 me?\xe2\x80\x99 says I. An\xe2\x80\x99 she\r\nsays \xe2\x80\x98Na,\xe2\x80\x99 she says: \xe2\x80\x98a cannot _shak\xe2\x80\x99 hands_ wi\xe2\x80\x99 thee!\xe2\x80\x99 she says.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry she said _that_,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in, thinking it was the only instance\r\nI had ever known of pride of rank showing itself in Lady Muriel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless you, it werena _pride_!\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, reading my thoughts.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe says \xe2\x80\x98_Your_ Minnie never _shook hands_ wi\xe2\x80\x99 you!\xe2\x80\x99 she says. \xe2\x80\x98An\xe2\x80\x99\r\n_I\xe2\x80\x99m_ your Minnie now,\xe2\x80\x99 she says. An\xe2\x80\x99 she just puts her dear arms about\r\nmy neck\xe2\x80\x94and she kisses me on t\xe2\x80\x99 cheek\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 may God in Heaven bless her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd here the poor old man broke down entirely, and could say no more.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98A CANNOT SHAK\xe2\x80\x99 HANDS WI\xe2\x80\x99 THEE!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless her!\xe2\x80\x9d I echoed. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd good night to you!\xe2\x80\x9d I pressed his hand,\r\nand left him. \xe2\x80\x9cLady Muriel,\xe2\x80\x9d I said softly to myself as I went\r\nhomewards, \xe2\x80\x9ctruly you know how to \xe2\x80\x98mak\xe2\x80\x99 amends\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeated once more by my lonely fireside, I tried to recall the strange\r\nvision of the night before, and to conjure up the face of the dear old\r\nProfessor among the blazing coals. \xe2\x80\x9cThat black one\xe2\x80\x94with just a touch of\r\nred\xe2\x80\x94would suit him well,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter such a catastrophe, it would\r\nbe sure to be covered with black stains\xe2\x80\x94and he would say:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe result of _that_ combination\xe2\x80\x94you may have noticed?\xe2\x80\x94was an\r\n_Explosion_! Shall I repeat the Experiment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! Don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d was the general cry. And we all\r\ntrooped off, in hot haste, to the Banqueting-Hall, where the feast had\r\nalready begun.\r\n\r\nNo time was lost in helping the dishes, and very speedily every guest\r\nfound his plate filled with good things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have always maintained the principle,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor began, \xe2\x80\x9cthat it\r\nis a good rule to take some food\xe2\x80\x94occasionally. The great advantage of\r\ndinner-parties\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, actually here\xe2\x80\x99s the Other\r\nProfessor!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s no place left for him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: THE OTHER PROFESSOR\xe2\x80\x99S FALL]\r\n\r\nThe Other Professor came in reading a large book, which he held close to\r\nhis eyes. One result of his not looking where he was going was that he\r\ntripped up, as he crossed the Saloon, flew up into the air, and fell\r\nheavily on his face in the middle of the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_ a pity!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the kind-hearted Professor, as he helped him up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be _me_, if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t trip,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Other Professor.\r\n\r\nThe Professor looked much shocked. \xe2\x80\x9cAlmost _anything_ would be better\r\nthan _that_!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt never does,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, aside to Bruno,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cto be anybody else, does it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo which Bruno gravely replied \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99s got nuffin on my plate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Professor hastily put on his spectacles, to make sure that the\r\n_facts_ were all right, to begin with: then he turned his jolly round\r\nface upon the unfortunate owner of the empty plate. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what would you\r\nlike next, my little man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said, a little doubtfully, \xe2\x80\x9cI think I\xe2\x80\x99ll take some\r\nplum-pudding, please\xe2\x80\x94while I think of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d (This was a whisper from Sylvie.) \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t good manners to\r\nask for a dish before it comes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Bruno whispered back \xe2\x80\x9cBut I might forget to ask for some, when it\r\ncomes, oo know\xe2\x80\x94I _do_ forget things, sometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, seeing Sylvie\r\nabout to whisper more.\r\n\r\nAnd _this_ assertion Sylvie did not venture to contradict.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile a chair had been placed for the Other Professor, between the\r\nEmpress and Sylvie. Sylvie found him a rather uninteresting neighbour:\r\nin fact, she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t afterwards remember that he had made more than\r\n_one_ remark to her during the whole banquet, and that was \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a\r\ncomfort a Dictionary is!\xe2\x80\x9d (She told Bruno, afterwards, that she had been\r\ntoo much afraid of him to say more than \xe2\x80\x9cYes, Sir,\xe2\x80\x9d in reply; and that\r\nhad been the end of their conversation. On which Bruno expressed a very\r\ndecided opinion that _that_ wasn\xe2\x80\x99t worth calling a \xe2\x80\x98conversation\xe2\x80\x99 at\r\nall. \xe2\x80\x9cOo should have asked him a riddle!\xe2\x80\x9d he added triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\r\n_I_ asked the Professor _three_ riddles! One was that one you asked me\r\nin the morning, \xe2\x80\x98How many pennies is there in two shillings?\xe2\x80\x99 And\r\nanother was\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9c_That_ wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\nriddle!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cIt _were_!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno fiercely replied.)\r\n\r\nBy this time a waiter had supplied Bruno with a plateful of _something_,\r\nwhich drove the plum-pudding out of his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother advantage of dinner-parties,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor cheerfully\r\nexplained, for the benefit of any one that would listen, \xe2\x80\x9cis that it\r\nhelps you to _see_ your friends. If you want to _see_ a man, offer him\r\nsomething to eat. It\xe2\x80\x99s the same rule with a mouse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis Cat\xe2\x80\x99s very kind to the Mouses,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said, stooping to stroke a\r\nremarkably fat specimen of the race, that had just waddled into the\r\nroom, and was rubbing itself affectionately against the leg of his\r\nchair. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease, Sylvie, pour some milk in your saucer. Pussie\xe2\x80\x99s ever so\r\nthirsty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you want _my_ saucer?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got one yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno: \xe2\x80\x9cbut I wanted _mine_ for to give it some\r\n_more_ milk in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie looked unconvinced: however it seemed quite impossible for her\r\n_ever_ to refuse what her brother asked: so she quietly filled her\r\nsaucer with milk, and handed it to Bruno, who got down off his chair to\r\nadminister it to the cat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe room\xe2\x80\x99s very hot, with all this crowd,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor said to\r\nSylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder why they don\xe2\x80\x99t put some lumps of ice in the grate? You\r\nfill it with lumps of coal in the winter, you know, and you sit round it\r\nand enjoy the warmth. How jolly it would be to fill it now with lumps of\r\nice, and sit round it and enjoy the coolth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHot as it was, Sylvie shivered a little at the idea. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very cold\r\n_outside_,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cMy feet got almost frozen to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the _shoemaker\xe2\x80\x99s_ fault!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor cheerfully replied. \xe2\x80\x9cHow\r\noften I\xe2\x80\x99ve explained to him that he _ought_ to make boots with little\r\niron frames under the soles, to hold lamps! But he never _thinks_. No\r\none would suffer from cold, if only they would _think_ of those little\r\nthings. I always use hot ink, myself, in the winter. Very few people\r\never think of _that_! Yet how simple it is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s very simple,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said politely. \xe2\x80\x9cHas the cat had enough?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThis was to Bruno, who had brought back the saucer only half-emptied.\r\n\r\nBut Bruno did not hear the question. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s somebody scratching at the\r\ndoor and wanting to come in,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. And he scrambled down off his\r\nchair, and went and cautiously peeped out through the door-way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was it wanted to come in?\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie asked, as he returned to his\r\nplace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt were a Mouse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd it peepted in. And it saw the Cat.\r\nAnd it said \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll come in another day.\xe2\x80\x99 And I said \xe2\x80\x98Oo needn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nflightened. The Cat\xe2\x80\x99s _welly_ kind to Mouses.\xe2\x80\x99 And it said \xe2\x80\x98But I\xe2\x80\x99s got\r\nsome imporkant business, what I _must_ attend to.\xe2\x80\x99 And it said \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ncall again to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x99 And it said \xe2\x80\x98Give my love to the Cat.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a fat cat it is!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Lord Chancellor, leaning across the\r\nProfessor to address his small neighbour. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quite a wonder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was awfully fat when it camed in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno: \xe2\x80\x9cso it would be more\r\nwonderfuller if it got thin all in a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that was the reason, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d the Lord Chancellor suggested,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhy you didn\xe2\x80\x99t give it the rest of the milk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cIt were a betterer reason. I tooked the saucer up\r\n\xe2\x80\x99cause it were so discontented!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t look so to _me_,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Lord Chancellor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat made you\r\nthink it was discontented?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Cause it grumbled in its throat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Bruno!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that\xe2\x80\x99s the way cats show they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n_pleased_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno looked doubtful. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not a good way,\xe2\x80\x9d he objected. \xe2\x80\x9cOo wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsay _I_ were pleased, if I made that noise in my throat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a singular boy!\xe2\x80\x9d the Lord Chancellor whispered to himself: but\r\nBruno had caught the words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do it mean to say \xe2\x80\x98a _singular_ boy\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered to Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt means _one_ boy,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie whispered in return. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd _plural_ means\r\ntwo or three.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99s welly glad I _is_ a singular boy!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said with great\r\nemphasis. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be _horrid_ to be two or three boys! P\xe2\x80\x99raps they\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t play with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy _should_ they?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Other Professor, suddenly waking up out of\r\na deep reverie. \xe2\x80\x9cThey might be asleep, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, if _I_ was awake,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said cunningly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, but they might indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d the Other Professor protested. \xe2\x80\x9cBoys don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nall go to sleep at once, you know. So these boys\xe2\x80\x94but who are you talking\r\nabout?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe _never_ remembers to ask that first!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor whispered to the\r\nchildren.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, the rest of _me_, a-course!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno exclaimed triumphantly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSupposing I was two or three boys!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Other Professor sighed, and seemed to be sinking back into his\r\nreverie; but suddenly brightened up again, and addressed the Professor.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing more to be done _now_, is there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there\xe2\x80\x99s the dinner to finish,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor said with a\r\nbewildered smile: \xe2\x80\x9cand the heat to bear. I hope you\xe2\x80\x99ll enjoy the\r\ndinner\xe2\x80\x94such as it is; and that you won\xe2\x80\x99t mind the heat\xe2\x80\x94such as it\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sentence _sounded_ well, but somehow I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t quite understand it;\r\nand the Other Professor seemed to be no better off. \xe2\x80\x9cSuch as it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n_what_?\xe2\x80\x9d he peevishly enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t as hot as it might be,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor replied, catching at the\r\nfirst idea that came to hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I see what you mean _now_!\xe2\x80\x9d the Other Professor graciously\r\nremarked. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very badly expressed, but I quite see it _now_! Thirteen\r\nminutes and a half ago,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, looking first at Bruno and then at\r\nhis watch as he spoke, \xe2\x80\x9cyou said \xe2\x80\x98this Cat\xe2\x80\x99s very kind to the Mouses.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nIt must be a singular animal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it _are_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, after carefully examining the Cat, to make\r\nsure how many there were of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how do you know it\xe2\x80\x99s kind to the Mouses\xe2\x80\x94or, more correctly\r\nspeaking, the _Mice_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Cause it _plays_ with the Mouses,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno; \xe2\x80\x9cfor to amuse them, oo\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that is just what I _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ know,\xe2\x80\x9d the Other Professor rejoined.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy belief is, it plays with them to _kill_ them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s quite a _accident_!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno began, so eagerly, that it was\r\nevident he had already propounded this very difficulty to the Cat. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\n\xe2\x80\x99splained all that to me, while it were drinking the milk. It said \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nteaches the Mouses new games: the Mouses likes it ever so much.\xe2\x80\x99 It said\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sometimes little accidents happens: sometimes the Mouses kills\r\ntheirselves.\xe2\x80\x99 It said \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99s always _welly_ sorry, when the Mouses kills\r\ntheirselves.\xe2\x80\x99 It said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it was so _very_ sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, rather disdainfully, \xe2\x80\x9cit\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t _eat_ the Mouses after they\xe2\x80\x99d killed themselves!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut this difficulty, also, had evidently not been lost sight of in the\r\nexhaustive ethical discussion just concluded. \xe2\x80\x9cIt said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (the orator\r\nconstantly omitted, as superfluous, his own share in the dialogue, and\r\nmerely gave us the replies of the Cat) \xe2\x80\x9cIt said \xe2\x80\x98Dead Mouses _never_\r\nobjecks to be eaten.\xe2\x80\x99 It said \xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s no use wasting good Mouses.\xe2\x80\x99 It\r\nsaid \xe2\x80\x98Wifful\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99 sumfinoruvver. It said \xe2\x80\x98And oo may live to say \xe2\x80\x98How much\r\nI wiss I had the Mouse that then I frew away!\xe2\x80\x99 It said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt hadn\xe2\x80\x99t _time_ to say such a lot of things!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie interrupted\r\nindignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOo doosn\xe2\x80\x99t know how Cats speaks!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno rejoined contemptuously. \xe2\x80\x9cCats\r\nspeaks _welly_ quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER XXIII.\r\n                             THE PIG-TALE.\r\n\r\n\r\nBy this time the appetites of the guests seemed to be nearly satisfied,\r\nand even _Bruno_ had the resolution to say, when the Professor offered\r\nhim a fourth slice of plum-pudding, \xe2\x80\x9cI thinks three helpings is enough!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly the Professor started as if he had been electrified. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I\r\nhad nearly forgotten the most important part of the entertainment! The\r\nOther Professor is to recite a Tale of a Pig\xe2\x80\x94I mean a Pig-Tale,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncorrected himself. \xe2\x80\x9cIt has Introductory Verses at the beginning, and at\r\nthe end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t have Introductory Verses at the _end_, can it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait till you hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor: \xe2\x80\x9cthen you\xe2\x80\x99ll see. I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\nsure it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t some in the _middle_, as well.\xe2\x80\x9d Here he rose to his feet,\r\nand there was an instant silence through the Banqueting-Hall: they\r\nevidently expected a speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLadies, and gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor began, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Other Professor is so\r\nkind as to recite a Poem. The title of it is \xe2\x80\x98The Pig-Tale.\xe2\x80\x99 He never\r\nrecited it before!\xe2\x80\x9d (General cheering among the guests.) \xe2\x80\x9cHe will never\r\nrecite it again!\xe2\x80\x9d (Frantic excitement, and wild cheering all down the\r\nhall, the Professor himself mounting the table in hot haste, to lead the\r\ncheering, and waving his spectacles in one hand and a spoon in the\r\nother.)\r\n\r\nThen the Other Professor got up, and began:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are dining\r\n      Warily and well,\r\n      Hid in mossy cell:\r\n  Hid, I say, by waiters\r\n  Gorgeous in their gaiters\xe2\x80\x94\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99ve a Tale to tell.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98TEACHING TIGRESSES TO SMILE\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are feeding\r\n      Justices with jam,\r\n      Rich in frizzled ham:\r\n  Rich, I say, in oysters\r\n  Haunting shady cloisters\xe2\x80\x94\r\n      That is what I am.\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are teaching\r\n      Tigresses to smile,\r\n      Innocent of guile:\r\n  Smile, I say, not smirkle\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Mouth a semicircle,\r\n      That\xe2\x80\x99s the proper style.\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are sleeping\r\n      All among the pins,\r\n      Where the loser wins:\r\n  Where, I say, he sneezes\r\n  When and how he pleases\xe2\x80\x94\r\n      So the Tale begins.\r\n\r\n  There was a Pig that sat alone\r\n    Beside a ruined Pump:\r\n  By day and night he made his moan\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  It would have stirred a heart of stone\r\n  To see him wring his hoofs and groan,\r\n    Because he could not jump.\r\n\r\n  A certain Camel heard him shout\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    A Camel with a hump.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cOh, is it Grief, or is it Gout?\r\n  What is this bellowing about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  That Pig replied, with quivering snout,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cBecause I cannot jump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  That Camel scanned him, dreamy-eyed.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cMethinks you are too plump.\r\n  I never knew a Pig so wide\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  That wobbled so from side to side\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Who could, however much he tried,\r\n    Do such a thing as _jump_!\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cYet mark those trees, two miles away,\r\n    All clustered in a clump:\r\n  If you could trot there twice a day,\r\n  Nor ever pause for rest or play,\r\n  In the far future\xe2\x80\x94Who can say?\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    You may be fit to jump.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98HORRID WAS THAT PIG\xe2\x80\x99S DESPAIR!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n  That Camel passed, and left him there,\r\n    Beside the ruined Pump.\r\n  Oh, horrid was that Pig\xe2\x80\x99s despair!\r\n  His shrieks of anguish filled the air.\r\n  He wrung his hoofs, he rent his hair,\r\n    Because he could not jump.\r\n\r\n  There was a Frog that wandered by\xe2\x80\x94\r\n    A sleek and shining lump:\r\n  Inspected him with fishy eye,\r\n  And said \xe2\x80\x9cO Pig, what makes you cry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  And bitter was that Pig\xe2\x80\x99s reply,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cBecause I cannot jump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  That Frog he grinned a grin of glee,\r\n    And hit his chest a thump\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cO Pig,\xe2\x80\x9d said, \xe2\x80\x9cbe ruled by me,\r\n  And you shall see what you shall see.\r\n  This minute, for a trifling fee,\r\n    I\xe2\x80\x99ll teach you how to jump!\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cYou may be faint from many a fall,\r\n    And bruised by many a bump:\r\n  But, if you persevere through all,\r\n  And practise first on something small,\r\n  Concluding with a ten-foot wall,\r\n    You\xe2\x80\x99ll find that you can jump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  That Pig looked up with joyful start:\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cOh Frog, you _are_ a trump!\r\n  Your words have healed my inward smart\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Come, name your fee and do your part:\r\n  Bring comfort to a broken heart,\r\n    By teaching me to jump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cMy fee shall be a mutton-chop,\r\n    My goal this ruined Pump.\r\n  Observe with what an airy flop\r\n  I plant myself upon the top!\r\n  Now bend your knees and take a hop,\r\n    For that\xe2\x80\x99s the way to jump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: THE FATAL JUMP]\r\n\r\n  Uprose that Pig, and rushed, full whack,\r\n    Against the ruined Pump:\r\n  Rolled over like an empty sack,\r\n  And settled down upon his back,\r\n  While all his bones at once went \xe2\x80\x98Crack!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n    It was a fatal jump.\r\n\r\nWhen the Other Professor had recited this Verse, he went across to the\r\nfire-place, and put his head up the chimney. In doing this, he lost his\r\nbalance, and fell head-first into the empty grate, and got so firmly\r\nfixed there that it was some time before he could be dragged out again.\r\n\r\nBruno had had time to say \xe2\x80\x9cI thought he wanted to see how many peoples\r\nwas up the chimbley.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Sylvie had said \xe2\x80\x9c_Chimney_\xe2\x80\x94not chimbley.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Bruno had said \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk \xe2\x80\x99ubbish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll this, while the Other Professor was being extracted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have blacked your face!\xe2\x80\x9d the Empress said anxiously. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me\r\nsend for some soap?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Other Professor, keeping his face turned away.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlack\xe2\x80\x99s quite a respectable colour. Besides, soap would be no use\r\nwithout water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKeeping his back well turned away from the audience, he went on with the\r\nIntroductory Verses:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98BATHING CROCODILES IN CREAM\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are writing\r\n      Interesting books,\r\n      To be read by cooks:\r\n  Read, I say, not roasted\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Letterpress, when toasted,\r\n      Loses its good looks.\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are playing\r\n      Bagpipes on the shore,\r\n      Where the tourists snore:\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cThanks!\xe2\x80\x9d they cry. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis thrilling!\r\n  Take, oh take this shilling!\r\n      Let us have no more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are bathing\r\n      Crocodiles in cream,\r\n      Like a happy dream:\r\n  Like, but not so lasting\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Crocodiles, when fasting,\r\n      Are not all they seem!\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98THAT PIG LAY STILL AS ANY STONE\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n  That Camel passed, as Day grew dim\r\n    Around the ruined Pump.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cO broken heart! O broken limb!\r\n  It needs,\xe2\x80\x9d that Camel said to him,\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cSomething more fairy-like and slim,\r\n    To execute a jump!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  That Pig lay still as any stone,\r\n    And could not stir a stump:\r\n  Nor ever, if the truth were known,\r\n  Was he again observed to moan,\r\n  Nor ever wring his hoofs and groan,\r\n    Because he could not jump.\r\n\r\n  That Frog made no remark, for he\r\n    Was dismal as a dump:\r\n  He knew the consequence must be\r\n  That he would never get his fee\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  And still he sits, in miserie,\r\n    Upon that ruined Pump!\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98STILL HE SITS IN MISERIE\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a miserable story!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cIt begins miserably, and it ends\r\nmiserablier. I think I shall cry. Sylvie, please lend me your\r\nhandkerchief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t got it with me,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I won\xe2\x80\x99t cry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno manfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are more Introductory Verses to come,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Other Professor,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d He sat down, cut a large slice of cake, put it on\r\nBruno\xe2\x80\x99s plate, and gazed at his own empty plate in astonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you get that cake?\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie whispered to Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe gived it me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ask for things! You _know_ you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _didn\xe2\x80\x99t_ ask,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, taking a fresh mouthful: \xe2\x80\x9che _gived_ it\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie considered this for a moment: then she saw her way out of it.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, ask him to give _me_ some!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to enjoy that cake?\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoos that mean \xe2\x80\x98munch\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno whispered to Sylvie.\r\n\r\nSylvie nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cIt means \xe2\x80\x98to munch\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98to _like_ to munch.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno smiled at the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cI _doos_ enjoy it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nThe Other Professor caught the word. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I hope you\xe2\x80\x99re enjoying\r\n_yourself_, little Man?\xe2\x80\x9d he enquired.\r\n\r\nBruno\xe2\x80\x99s look of horror quite startled him. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, _indeed_ I aren\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nThe Other Professor looked thoroughly puzzled. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, well!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry some cowslip wine!\xe2\x80\x9d And he filled a glass and handed it to Bruno.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrink this, my dear, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll be quite another man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho shall I be?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno, pausing in the act of putting it to his\r\nlips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask so many questions!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie interposed, anxious to save the\r\npoor old man from further bewilderment. \xe2\x80\x9cSuppose we get the Professor to\r\ntell us a story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno adopted the idea with enthusiasm. \xe2\x80\x9c_Please_ do!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried eagerly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSumfin about tigers\xe2\x80\x94and bumble-bees\xe2\x80\x94and robin-redbreasts, oo knows!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should you always have _live_ things in stories?\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nProfessor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you have events, or circumstances?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _please_ invent a story like that!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno.\r\n\r\nThe Professor began fluently enough. \xe2\x80\x9cOnce a coincidence was taking a\r\nwalk with a little accident, and they met an explanation\xe2\x80\x94a _very_ old\r\nexplanation\xe2\x80\x94so old that it was quite doubled up, and looked more like a\r\nconundrum\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Please_ go on!\xe2\x80\x9d both children exclaimed.\r\n\r\nThe Professor made a candid confession. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a very difficult sort to\r\ninvent, I find. Suppose Bruno tells one, first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno was only too happy to adopt the suggestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce there were a Pig, and a Accordion, and two Jars of\r\nOrange-marmalade\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _dramatis person\xc3\xa6_,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, what then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, when the Pig played on the Accordion,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno went on, \xe2\x80\x9cone of the\r\nJars of Orange-marmalade didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the tune, and the other Jar of\r\nOrange-marmalade did like the tune\xe2\x80\x94I _know_ I shall get confused among\r\nthose Jars of Orange-marmalade, Sylvie!\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will now recite the other Introductory Verses,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Other\r\nProfessor.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98BLESSED BY HAPPY STAGS\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are choking\r\n      Baronets with bun,\r\n      Taught to fire a gun:\r\n  Taught, I say, to splinter\r\n  Salmon in the winter\xe2\x80\x94\r\n      Merely for the fun.\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are hiding\r\n      Crimes in carpet-bags,\r\n      Blessed by happy stags:\r\n  Blessed, I say, though beaten\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  Since our friends are eaten\r\n      When the memory flags.\r\n\r\n  Little Birds are tasting\r\n      Gratitude and gold,\r\n      Pale with sudden cold\r\n  Pale, I say, and wrinkled\xe2\x80\x94\r\n  When the bells have tinkled\r\n      And the Tale is told.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe next thing to be done,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor cheerfully remarked to the\r\nLord Chancellor, as soon as the applause, caused by the recital of the\r\nPig-Tale, had come to an end, \xe2\x80\x9cis to drink the Emperor\xe2\x80\x99s health, is it\r\nnot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly!\xe2\x80\x9d the Lord Chancellor replied with much solemnity, as he\r\nrose to his feet to give the necessary directions for the ceremony.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFill your glasses!\xe2\x80\x9d he thundered. All did so, instantly. \xe2\x80\x9cDrink the\r\nEmperor\xe2\x80\x99s health!\xe2\x80\x9d A general gurgling resounded all through the Hall.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree cheers for the Emperor!\xe2\x80\x9d The faintest possible sound followed\r\n_this_ announcement: and the Chancellor, with admirable presence of\r\nmind, instantly proclaimed \xe2\x80\x9cA speech from the Emperor!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Emperor had begun his speech almost before the words were uttered.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHowever unwilling to be Emperor\xe2\x80\x94since you all wish me to be Emperor\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nknow how badly the late Warden managed things\xe2\x80\x94with such enthusiasm as\r\nyou have shown\xe2\x80\x94he persecuted you\xe2\x80\x94he taxed you too heavily\xe2\x80\x94you know who\r\nis fittest man to be Emperor\xe2\x80\x94my brother had no sense\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHow long this curious speech might have lasted it is impossible to say,\r\nfor just at this moment a hurricane shook the palace to its foundations,\r\nbursting open the windows, extinguishing some of the lamps, and filling\r\nthe air with clouds of dust, which took strange shapes in the air, and\r\nseemed to form words.\r\n\r\nBut the storm subsided as suddenly as it had risen\xe2\x80\x94the casements swung\r\ninto their places again: the dust vanished: all was as it had been a\r\nminute ago\xe2\x80\x94with the exception of the Emperor and Empress, over whom had\r\ncome a wondrous change. The vacant stare, the meaningless smile, had\r\npassed away: all could see that these two strange beings had returned to\r\ntheir senses.\r\n\r\nThe Emperor continued his speech as if there had been no interruption.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we have behaved\xe2\x80\x94my wife and I\xe2\x80\x94like two arrant Knaves. We deserve no\r\nbetter name. When my brother went away, you lost the best Warden you\r\never had. And I\xe2\x80\x99ve been doing my best, wretched hypocrite that I am, to\r\ncheat you into making me an Emperor. Me! One that has hardly got the\r\nwits to be a shoe-black!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Lord Chancellor wrung his hands in despair. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is mad, good\r\npeople!\xe2\x80\x9d he was beginning. But both speeches stopped suddenly\xe2\x80\x94and, in\r\nthe dead silence that followed, a knocking was heard at the outer door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d was the general cry. People began running in and out. The\r\nexcitement increased every moment. The Lord Chancellor, forgetting all\r\nthe rules of Court-ceremony, ran full speed down the hall, and in a\r\nminute returned, pale and gasping for breath.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             CHAPTER XXIV.\r\n                          THE BEGGAR\xe2\x80\x99S RETURN.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Imperial Highnesses!\xe2\x80\x9d he began. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the old Beggar again! Shall\r\nwe set the dogs at him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring him here!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Emperor.\r\n\r\nThe Chancellor could scarcely believe his ears. \xe2\x80\x9c_Here_, your Imperial\r\nHighness? Did I rightly understand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring him here!\xe2\x80\x9d the Emperor thundered once more. The Chancellor\r\ntottered down the hall\xe2\x80\x94and in another minute the crowd divided, and the\r\npoor old Beggar was seen entering the Banqueting-Hall.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: THE OLD BEGGAR\xe2\x80\x99S RETURN]\r\n\r\nHe was indeed a pitiable object: the rags, that hung about him, were all\r\nsplashed with mud: his white hair and his long beard were tossed about\r\nin wild disorder. Yet he walked upright, with a stately tread, as if\r\nused to command: and\xe2\x80\x94strangest sight of all\xe2\x80\x94Sylvie and Bruno came with\r\nhim, clinging to his hands, and gazing at him with looks of silent love.\r\n\r\nMen looked eagerly to see how the Emperor would receive the bold\r\nintruder. Would he hurl him from the steps of the da\xc3\xafs? But no. To their\r\nutter astonishment, the Emperor knelt as the beggar approached, and with\r\nbowed head murmured \xe2\x80\x9cForgive us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive us!\xe2\x80\x9d the Empress, kneeling at her husband\xe2\x80\x99s side, meekly\r\nrepeated.\r\n\r\nThe Outcast smiled. \xe2\x80\x9cRise up!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI forgive you!\xe2\x80\x9d And men saw\r\nwith wonder that a change had passed over the old beggar, even as he\r\nspoke. What had seemed, but now, to be vile rags and splashes of mud,\r\nwere seen to be in truth kingly trappings, broidered with gold, and\r\nsparkling with gems. All knew him now, and bent low before the Elder\r\nBrother, the true Warden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrother mine, and Sister mine!\xe2\x80\x9d the Warden began, in a clear voice that\r\nwas heard all through that vast hall. \xe2\x80\x9cI come not to disturb you. Rule\r\non, as Emperor, and rule wisely. For I am chosen King of Elfland.\r\nTo-morrow I return there, taking nought from hence, save only\xe2\x80\x94save\r\nonly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d his voice trembled, and with a look of ineffable tenderness, he\r\nlaid his hands in silence on the heads of the two little ones who clung\r\naround him.\r\n\r\nBut he recovered himself in a moment, and beckoned to the Emperor to\r\nresume his place at the table. The company seated themselves again\xe2\x80\x94room\r\nbeing found for the Elfin-King between his two children\xe2\x80\x94and the Lord\r\nChancellor rose once more, to propose the next toast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe next toast\xe2\x80\x94the hero of the day\xe2\x80\x94why, he isn\xe2\x80\x99t here!\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off in\r\nwild confusion.\r\n\r\nGood gracious! Everybody had forgotten Prince Uggug!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was told of the Banquet, of course?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Emperor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUndoubtedly!\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Chancellor. \xe2\x80\x9c_That_ would be the duty of the\r\nGold Stick in Waiting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet the Gold Stick come forwards!\xe2\x80\x9d the Emperor gravely said.\r\n\r\nThe Gold Stick came forwards. \xe2\x80\x9cI attended on His Imperial Fatness,\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nthe statement made by the trembling official. \xe2\x80\x9cI told him of the Lecture\r\nand the Banquet\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat followed?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Emperor: for the unhappy man seemed almost too\r\nfrightened to go on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Imperial Fatness was graciously pleased to be sulky. His Imperial\r\nFatness was graciously pleased to box my ears. His Imperial Fatness was\r\ngraciously pleased to say \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t care!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t-care\xe2\x80\x99 came to a bad end,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie whispered to Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\nsure, but I _believe_ he was hanged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Professor overheard her. \xe2\x80\x9c_That_ result,\xe2\x80\x9d he blandly remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cwas\r\nmerely a case of mistaken identity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoth children looked puzzled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPermit me to explain. \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t-care\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Care\xe2\x80\x99 were twin-brothers.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Care,\xe2\x80\x99 you know, killed the Cat. And they caught \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t-care\xe2\x80\x99 by\r\nmistake, and hanged him instead. And so \xe2\x80\x98Care\xe2\x80\x99 is alive still. But he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvery unhappy without his brother. That\xe2\x80\x99s why they say \xe2\x80\x98Begone, dull\r\nCare!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, heartily. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very extremely interesting.\r\nWhy, it seems to explain _everything_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not quite _everything_,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor modestly rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\r\nare two or three scientific difficulties\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was your general impression as to His Imperial Fatness?\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nEmperor asked the Gold Stick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy impression was that His Imperial Fatness was getting more\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore _what_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll listened breathlessly for the next word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore PRICKLY!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must be sent for _at once_!\xe2\x80\x9d the Emperor exclaimed. And the Gold\r\nStick went off like a shot. The Elfin-King sadly shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\r\nuse, no use!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cLoveless, loveless!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPale, trembling, speechless, the Gold Stick came slowly back again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Emperor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy does not the Prince appear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne can easily guess,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cHis Imperial Fatness is,\r\nwithout doubt, a little preoccupied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBruno turned a look of solemn enquiry on his old friend. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do that\r\nword mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the Professor took no notice of the question. He was eagerly\r\nlistening to the Gold Stick\xe2\x80\x99s reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease your Highness! His Imperial Fatness is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Not a word more could\r\nhe utter.\r\n\r\nThe Empress rose in an agony of alarm. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us go to him!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.\r\nAnd there was a general rush for the door.\r\n\r\nBruno slipped off his chair in a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cMay we go too?\xe2\x80\x9d he eagerly\r\nasked. But the King did not hear the question, as the Professor was\r\nspeaking to him. \xe2\x80\x9c_Preoccupied_, your Majesty!\xe2\x80\x9d he was saying. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is\r\nwhat he is, no doubt!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay we go and see him?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno repeated. The King nodded assent, and the\r\nchildren ran off. In a minute or two they returned, slowly and gravely.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d said the King. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with the Prince?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94what _you_ said,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno replied, looking at the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nhard word.\xe2\x80\x9d And he looked to Sylvie for assistance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPorcupine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor corrected her. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_Pre-occupied_,\xe2\x80\x99 you mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98PORCUPINE!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s _porcupine_,\xe2\x80\x9d persisted Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cNot that other word at all.\r\nAnd please will you come? The house is all in an uproar.\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cAnd oo\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbetter bring an uproar-glass wiz oo!\xe2\x80\x9d added Bruno.)\r\n\r\nWe got up in great haste, and followed the children upstairs. No one\r\ntook the least notice of _me_, but I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t at all surprised at this, as\r\nI had long realised that I was quite invisible to them all\xe2\x80\x94even to\r\nSylvie and Bruno.\r\n\r\nAll along the gallery, that led to the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s apartment, an excited\r\ncrowd was surging to and fro, and the Babel of voices was deafening:\r\nagainst the door of the room three strong men were leaning, vainly\r\ntrying to shut it\xe2\x80\x94for some great animal inside was constantly bursting\r\nit half open, and we had a glimpse, before the men could push it back\r\nagain, of the head of a furious wild beast, with great fiery eyes and\r\ngnashing teeth. Its voice was a sort of mixture\xe2\x80\x94there was the roaring of\r\na lion, and the bellowing of a bull, and now and then a scream like a\r\ngigantic parrot. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no judging by the voice!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor cried\r\nin great excitement. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted to the men at the door.\r\nAnd a general chorus of voices answered him \xe2\x80\x9cPorcupine! Prince Uggug has\r\nturned into a Porcupine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA new Specimen!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the delighted Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cPray let me go in.\r\nIt should be labeled at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the strong men only pushed him back. \xe2\x80\x9cLabel it, indeed! Do you want\r\nto be eaten up?\xe2\x80\x9d they cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind about Specimens, Professor!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Emperor, pushing his\r\nway through the crowd. \xe2\x80\x9cTell us how to keep him safe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA large cage!\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor promptly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBring a large cage,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid to the people generally, \xe2\x80\x9cwith strong bars of steel, and a\r\nportcullis made to go up and down like a mouse-trap! Does any one happen\r\nto have such a thing about him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt didn\xe2\x80\x99t sound a likely sort of thing for any one to have about him;\r\nhowever, they brought him one directly: curiously enough, there happened\r\nto be one standing in the gallery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut it facing the opening of the door, and draw up the portcullis!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThis was done in a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlankets now!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a most interesting\r\nExperiment!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere happened to be a pile of blankets close by: and the Professor had\r\nhardly said the word, when they were all unfolded and held up like\r\ncurtains all around. The Professor rapidly arranged them in two rows, so\r\nas to make a dark passage, leading straight from the door to the mouth\r\nof the cage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow fling the door open!\xe2\x80\x9d This did not need to be done: the three men\r\nhad only to leap out of the way, and the fearful monster flung the door\r\nopen for itself, and, with a yell like the whistle of a steam-engine,\r\nrushed into the cage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDown with the portcullis!\xe2\x80\x9d No sooner said than done: and all breathed\r\nfreely once more, on seeing the Porcupine safely caged.\r\n\r\nThe Professor rubbed his hands in childish delight. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Experiment has\r\nsucceeded!\xe2\x80\x9d he proclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cAll that is needed now is to feed it three\r\ntimes a day, on chopped carrots and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind about its food, just now!\xe2\x80\x9d the Emperor interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us\r\nreturn to the Banquet. Brother, will you lead the way?\xe2\x80\x9d And the old man,\r\nattended by his children, headed the procession down stairs. \xe2\x80\x9cSee the\r\nfate of a loveless life!\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Bruno, as they returned to their\r\nplaces. To which Bruno made reply, \xe2\x80\x9cI always loved Sylvie, so I\xe2\x80\x99ll never\r\nget prickly like that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe _is_ prickly, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor, who had caught the\r\nlast words, \xe2\x80\x9cbut we must remember that, however porcupiny, he is royal\r\nstill! After this feast is over, I\xe2\x80\x99m going to take a little present to\r\nPrince Uggug\xe2\x80\x94just to soothe him, you know: it isn\xe2\x80\x99t pleasant living in a\r\ncage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99ll you give him for a birthday-present?\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno enquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA small saucer of chopped carrots,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cIn giving\r\nbirthday-presents, _my_ motto is\xe2\x80\x94cheapness! I should think I save forty\r\npounds a year by giving\xe2\x80\x94oh, _what_ a twinge of pain!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie anxiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy old enemy!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cLumbago\xe2\x80\x94rheumatism\xe2\x80\x94that sort of\r\nthing. I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and lie down a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d And he hobbled out of the\r\nSaloon, watched by the pitying eyes of the two children.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll be better soon!\xe2\x80\x9d the Elfin-King said cheerily. \xe2\x80\x9cBrother!\xe2\x80\x9d turning\r\nto the Emperor, \xe2\x80\x9cI have some business to arrange with you to-night. The\r\nEmpress will take care of the children.\xe2\x80\x9d And the two Brothers went away\r\ntogether, arm-in-arm.\r\n\r\nThe Empress found the children rather sad company. They could talk of\r\nnothing but \xe2\x80\x9cthe dear Professor,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a pity he\xe2\x80\x99s so ill!\xe2\x80\x9d, till\r\nat last she made the welcome proposal \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go and see him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe children eagerly grasped the hands she offered them: and we went off\r\nto the Professor\xe2\x80\x99s study, and found him lying on the sofa, covered up\r\nwith blankets, and reading a little manuscript-book. \xe2\x80\x9cNotes on Vol.\r\nThree!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, looking up at us. And there, on a table near him,\r\nlay the book he was seeking when first I saw him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how are you now, Professor?\xe2\x80\x9d the Empress asked, bending over the\r\ninvalid.\r\n\r\nThe Professor looked up, and smiled feebly. \xe2\x80\x9cAs devoted to your Imperial\r\nHighness as ever!\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a weak voice. \xe2\x80\x9cAll of me, that is not\r\nLumbago, is Loyalty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA sweet sentiment!\xe2\x80\x9d the Empress exclaimed with tears in her eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nseldom hear anything so beautiful as that\xe2\x80\x94even in a Valentine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must take you to stay at the seaside,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, tenderly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ndo you ever so much good! And the Sea\xe2\x80\x99s so grand!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut a Mountain\xe2\x80\x99s grander!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is there grand about the Sea?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you could\r\nput it all into a teacup!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Some_ of it,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie corrected him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99d only want a certain number of tea-cups to hold it _all_.\r\nAnd _then_ where\xe2\x80\x99s the grandeur? Then as to a Mountain\xe2\x80\x94why, you could\r\ncarry it all away in a wheel-barrow, in a certain number of years!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t look grand\xe2\x80\x94the bits of it in the wheel-barrow,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie\r\ncandidly admitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut when oo put it together again\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you\xe2\x80\x99re older,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ll know that you _ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_\r\nput Mountains together again so easily! One lives and one learns, you\r\nknow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it needn\xe2\x80\x99t be the _same_ one, need it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t it do,\r\nif _I_ live, and if _Sylvie_ learns?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t_ learn without living!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I _can_ live without learning!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cOo just try me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I meant, was\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor began, looking much puzzled,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94was\xe2\x80\x94that you don\xe2\x80\x99t know _everything_, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I _do_ know everything I know!\xe2\x80\x9d persisted the little fellow. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknow ever so many things! Everything, \xe2\x80\x99cept the things I _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ know.\r\nAnd Sylvie knows all the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Professor sighed, and gave it up. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what a Boojum is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ know!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the thing what wrenches people out of\r\ntheir boots!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe means \xe2\x80\x98bootjack,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie explained in a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t wrench people out of _boots_,\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor mildly\r\nobserved.\r\n\r\nBruno laughed saucily. \xe2\x80\x9cOo _can_, though! Unless they\xe2\x80\x99re _welly_ tight\r\nin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce upon a time there was a Boojum\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d the Professor began, but stopped\r\nsuddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cI forget the rest of the Fable,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd there was a\r\nlesson to be learned from it. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I forget _that_, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ tell oo a Fable!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno began in a great hurry. \xe2\x80\x9cOnce there were\r\na Locust, and a Magpie, and a Engine-driver. And the Lesson is, to learn\r\nto get up early\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t a bit interesting!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said contemptuously. \xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nput the Lesson so soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you invent that Fable?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cLast week?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cA deal shorter ago than that. Guess again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ca\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t guess,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cHow long ago?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t invented yet!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno exclaimed triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\n_have_ invented a lovely one! Shall I say it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ve _finished_ inventing it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sylvie. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd let the Lesson be\r\n\xe2\x80\x98to try again\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bruno with great decision. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Lesson are \xe2\x80\x98_not_ to try\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOnce there were a lovely china man, what stood on the\r\nchimbley-piece. And he stood, and he stood. And one day he tumbleded\r\noff, and he didn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt his self one bit. Only he _would_ try again. And\r\nthe next time he tumbleded off, he hurted his self welly much, and\r\nbreaked off ever so much varnish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how did he come back on the chimney-piece after his first tumble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Empress. (It was the first sensible question she had asked in\r\nall her life.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ put him there!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you know something about his tumbling,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nProfessor. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you pushed him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo which Bruno replied, very seriously, \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t pushed him _much_\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nwere a _lovely_ china man,\xe2\x80\x9d he added hastily, evidently very anxious to\r\nchange the subject.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, my children!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Elfin-King, who had just entered the room.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must have a little chat together, before you go to bed.\xe2\x80\x9d And he was\r\nleading them away, but at the door they let go his hands, and ran back\r\nagain to wish the Professor good night.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98GOOD-NIGHT, PROFESSOR!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood night, Professor, good night!\xe2\x80\x9d And Bruno solemnly shook hands with\r\nthe old man, who gazed at him with a loving smile, while Sylvie bent\r\ndown to press her sweet lips upon his forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood night, little ones!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Professor. \xe2\x80\x9cYou may leave me now\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nruminate. I\xe2\x80\x99m as jolly as the day is long, except when it\xe2\x80\x99s necessary to\r\nruminate on some very difficult subject. All of me,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured\r\nsleepily as we left the room, \xe2\x80\x9call of me, that isn\xe2\x80\x99t _Bonhommie_, is\r\nRumination!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_ did he say, Bruno?\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie enquired, as soon as we were safely\r\nout of hearing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _think_ he said \xe2\x80\x98All of me that isn\xe2\x80\x99t Bone-disease is Rheumatism.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nWhatever _are_ that knocking, Sylvie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie stopped, and listened anxiously. It sounded like some one kicking\r\nat a door. \xe2\x80\x9cI _hope_ it isn\xe2\x80\x99t that Porcupine breaking loose!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nexclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go on!\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno said hastily. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nuffin to wait for, oo\r\nknow!\xe2\x80\x9c\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                              CHAPTER XXV\r\n                           LIFE OUT OF DEATH.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sound of kicking, or knocking, grew louder every moment: and at last\r\na door opened somewhere near us. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you say \xe2\x80\x98come in!\xe2\x80\x99 Sir?\xe2\x80\x9d my\r\nlandlady asked timidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh yes, come in!\xe2\x80\x9d I replied. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA note has just been left for you, Sir, by the baker\xe2\x80\x99s boy. He said he\r\nwas passing the Hall, and they asked him to come round and leave it\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe note contained five words only. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease come at once. Muriel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA sudden terror seemed to chill my very heart. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Earl is ill!\xe2\x80\x9d I said\r\nto myself. \xe2\x80\x9cDying, perhaps!\xe2\x80\x9d And I hastily prepared to leave the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo bad news, Sir, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d my landlady said, as she saw me out. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nboy said as some one had arrived unexpectedly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope that is it!\xe2\x80\x9d I said. But my feelings were those of fear rather\r\nthan of hope: though, on entering the house, I was somewhat reassured by\r\nfinding luggage lying in the entrance, bearing the initials \xe2\x80\x9cE. L.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only Eric Lindon after all!\xe2\x80\x9d I thought, half relieved and half\r\nannoyed. \xe2\x80\x9cSurely she need not have sent for me for _that_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Muriel met me in the passage. Her eyes were gleaming\xe2\x80\x94but it was the\r\nexcitement of joy, rather than of grief. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a surprise for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that Eric Lindon is here?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, vainly trying to disguise\r\nthe involuntary bitterness of my tone. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_The funeral baked meats did\r\ncoldly furnish forth the marriage-tables_,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d I could not help repeating\r\nto myself. How cruelly I was misjudging her!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d she eagerly replied. \xe2\x80\x9cAt least\xe2\x80\x94Eric _is_ here. But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94,\xe2\x80\x9d her\r\nvoice quivered, \xe2\x80\x9cbut there is _another_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo need for further question. I eagerly followed her in. There on the\r\nbed, he lay\xe2\x80\x94pale and worn\xe2\x80\x94the mere shadow of his old self\xe2\x80\x94my old friend\r\ncome back again from the dead!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArthur!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed. I could not say another word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, back again, old boy!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, smiling as I grasped his hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_He_,\xe2\x80\x9d indicating Eric, who stood near, \xe2\x80\x9csaved my life\xe2\x80\x94_He_ brought me\r\nback. Next to God, we must thank _him_, Muriel, my wife!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSilently I shook hands with Eric and with the Earl: and with one consent\r\nwe moved into the shaded side of the room, where we could talk without\r\ndisturbing the invalid, who lay, silent and happy, holding his wife\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhand in his, and watching her with eyes that shone with the deep steady\r\nlight of Love.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been delirious till to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d Eric explained in a low voice: \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\neven to-day he has been wandering more than once. But the sight of _her_\r\nhas been new life to him.\xe2\x80\x9d And then he went on to tell us, in would-be\r\ncareless tones\xe2\x80\x94I knew how he hated any display of feeling\xe2\x80\x94how he had\r\ninsisted on going back to the plague-stricken town, to bring away a man\r\nwhom the doctor had abandoned as dying, but who _might_, he fancied,\r\nrecover if brought to the hospital: how he had seen nothing in the\r\nwasted features to remind him of Arthur, and only recognised him when he\r\nvisited the hospital a month after: how the doctor had forbidden him to\r\nannounce the discovery, saying that any shock to the over taxed brain\r\nmight kill him at once: how he had staid on at the hospital, and nursed\r\nthe sick man by night and day\xe2\x80\x94all this with the studied indifference of\r\none who is relating the commonplace acts of some chance acquaintance!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this was his _rival_!\xe2\x80\x9d I thought. \xe2\x80\x9cThe man who had won from him the\r\nheart of the woman he loved!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98HIS WIFE KNELT DOWN AT HIS SIDE\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sun is setting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Muriel, rising and leading the way to\r\nthe open window. \xe2\x80\x9cJust look at the western sky! What lovely crimson\r\ntints! We shall have a glorious day to-morrow\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d We had followed her\r\nacross the room, and were standing in a little group, talking in low\r\ntones in the gathering gloom, when we were startled by the voice of the\r\nsick man, murmuring words too indistinct for the ear to catch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is wandering again,\xe2\x80\x9d Lady Muriel whispered, and returned to the\r\nbedside. We drew a little nearer also: but no, this had none of the\r\nincoherence of delirium. \xe2\x80\x9c_What reward shall I give unto the Lord_,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\ntremulous lips were saying, \xe2\x80\x9c_for all the benefits that He hath done\r\nunto me? I will receive the cup of salvation, and call\xe2\x80\x94and call_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d but\r\nhere the poor weakened memory failed, and the feeble voice died into\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\nHis wife knelt down at the bedside, raised one of his arms, and drew it\r\nacross her own, fondly kissing the thin white hand that lay so\r\nlistlessly in her loving grasp. It seemed to me a good opportunity for\r\nstealing away without making her go through any form of parting: so,\r\nnodding to the Earl and Eric, I silently left the room. Eric followed me\r\ndown the stairs, and out into the night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it Life or Death?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked him, as soon as we were far enough from\r\nthe house for me to speak in ordinary tones.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is _Life_!\xe2\x80\x9d he replied with eager emphasis. \xe2\x80\x9cThe doctors are quite\r\nagreed as to _that_. All he needs now, they say, is rest, and perfect\r\nquiet, and good nursing. He\xe2\x80\x99s quite sure to get rest and quiet, here:\r\nand, as for the nursing why, I think it\xe2\x80\x99s just _possible_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (he tried\r\nhard to make his trembling voice assume a playful tone) \xe2\x80\x9che may even get\r\nfairly well nursed, in his present quarters!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure of it!\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you so much for coming out to tell me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd, thinking he had now said all he had come to say, I held out my hand\r\nto bid him good night. He grasped it warmly, and added, turning his face\r\naway as he spoke, \xe2\x80\x9cBy the way, there is one other thing I wanted to say.\r\nI thought you\xe2\x80\x99d like to know that\xe2\x80\x94that I\xe2\x80\x99m not\xe2\x80\x94not in the mind I was in\r\nwhen last we met. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94that I can accept Christian belief\xe2\x80\x94at least,\r\nnot yet. But all this came about so strangely. And she had prayed, you\r\nknow. And I had prayed. And\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d his voice broke, and I could only just\r\ncatch the concluding words, \xe2\x80\x9c_there is a God that answers prayer!_ I\r\nknow it for certain now.\xe2\x80\x9d He wrung my hand once more, and left me\r\nsuddenly. Never before had I seen him so deeply moved.\r\n\r\nSo, in the gathering twilight, I paced slowly homewards, in a tumultuous\r\nwhirl of happy thoughts: my heart seemed full, and running over, with\r\njoy and thankfulness: all that I had so fervently longed for, and prayed\r\nfor, seemed now to have come to pass. And, though I reproached myself,\r\nbitterly, for the unworthy suspicion I had for one moment harboured\r\nagainst the true-hearted Lady Muriel, I took comfort in knowing it had\r\nbeen but a passing thought.\r\n\r\nNot Bruno himself could have mounted the stairs with so buoyant a step,\r\nas I felt my way up in the dark, not pausing to strike a light in the\r\nentry, as I knew I had left the lamp burning in my sitting-room.\r\n\r\nBut it was no common _lamplight_ into which I now stepped, with a\r\nstrange, new, dreamy sensation of some subtle witchery that had come\r\nover the place. Light, richer and more golden than any lamp could give,\r\nflooded the room, streaming in from a window I had somehow never noticed\r\nbefore, and lighting up a group of three shadowy figures, that grew\r\nmomently more distinct\xe2\x80\x94a grave old man in royal robes, leaning back in\r\nan easy chair, and two children, a girl and a boy, standing at his side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you the Jewel still, my child?\xe2\x80\x9d the old man was saying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _yes_!\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie exclaimed with unusual eagerness. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n_ever_ lose it or forget it?\xe2\x80\x9d She undid the ribbon round her neck, as\r\nshe spoke, and laid the Jewel in her father\xe2\x80\x99s hand.\r\n\r\nBruno looked at it admiringly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a lovely brightness!\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just like a little red star! May I take it in my hand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie nodded: and Bruno carried it off to the window, and held it aloft\r\nagainst the sky, whose deepening blue was already spangled with stars.\r\nSoon he came running back in some excitement. \xe2\x80\x9cSylvie! Look here!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncried. \xe2\x80\x9cI can see right through it when I hold it up to the sky. And it\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t red a bit: it\xe2\x80\x99s, oh such a lovely blue! And the words are all\r\ndifferent! Do look at it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie was quite excited, too, by this time; and the two children\r\neagerly held up the Jewel to the light, and spelled out the legend\r\nbetween them, \xe2\x80\x9cALL WILL LOVE SYLVIE.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: THE BLUE LOCKET]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, this is the _other_ Jewel!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bruno. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you remember,\r\nSylvie? The one you _didn\xe2\x80\x99t_ choose!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie took it from him, with a puzzled look, and held it, now up to the\r\nlight, now down. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s blue, _one_ way,\xe2\x80\x9d she said softly to herself,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand it\xe2\x80\x99s red, the _other_ way! Why, I thought there were _two_ of\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94Father!\xe2\x80\x9d she suddenly exclaimed, laying the Jewel once more in his\r\nhand, \xe2\x80\x9cI do believe it was the _same_ Jewel all the time!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you choosed it from _itself_,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno thoughtfully remarked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather, _could_ Sylvie choose a thing from itself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my own one,\xe2\x80\x9d the old man replied to Sylvie, not noticing Bruno\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nembarrassing question, \xe2\x80\x9cit _was_ the same Jewel\xe2\x80\x94but you chose quite\r\nright.\xe2\x80\x9d And he fastened the ribbon round her neck again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSYLVIE WILL LOVE ALL\xe2\x80\x94ALL WILL LOVE SYLVIE,\xe2\x80\x9d Bruno murmured, raising\r\nhimself on tiptoe to kiss the \xe2\x80\x98little red star.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, when you look\r\n_at_ it, it\xe2\x80\x99s red and fierce like the sun\xe2\x80\x94and, when you look _through_\r\nit, it\xe2\x80\x99s gentle and blue like the sky!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod\xe2\x80\x99s own sky,\xe2\x80\x9d Sylvie said, dreamily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod\xe2\x80\x99s own sky,\xe2\x80\x9d the little fellow repeated, as they stood, lovingly\r\nclinging together, and looking out into the night. \xe2\x80\x9cBut oh, Sylvie, what\r\nmakes the sky such a _darling_ blue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSylvie\xe2\x80\x99s sweet lips shaped themselves to reply, but her voice sounded\r\nfaint and very far away. The vision was fast slipping from my eager\r\ngaze: but it seemed to me, in that last bewildering moment, that not\r\nSylvie but an angel was looking out through those trustful brown eyes,\r\nand that not Sylvie\xe2\x80\x99s but an angel\xe2\x80\x99s voice was whispering\r\n\r\n                             \xe2\x80\x9cIt is love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x98IT IS LOVE!\xe2\x80\x99]\r\n\r\n\r\n                                THE END.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                             GENERAL INDEX.\r\n\r\n\r\n[N.B. \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99 refers to \xe2\x80\x9cSylvie and Bruno,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x98II\xe2\x80\x99 to \xe2\x80\x9cSylvie and Bruno\r\nConcluded.\xe2\x80\x9d]\r\n\r\n                                   A\r\n  Accelerated Velocity, causes of; II. 190\r\n  Air, Cotton-wool lighter than, how to obtain; II. 166\r\n  Animal-Suffering, mystery of; II. 296\r\n  Anti-Teetotal Card; II. 139\r\n  Artistic effect said to require Indistinctness; I. 241\r\n  Asylums, Lunatic-, future use for; II. 132\r\n  Axioms of Science; II. 330\r\n\r\n                                   B\r\n  Badgers, the Three (Poem); I. 247\r\n  Barometer, sideways motion of; I. 13\r\n  Baron Doppelgeist; I. 85\r\n  Bath, Portable, for Tourists; I. 25\r\n  Bazaars, Charity-; II. 44\r\n  Beauty, Pain of realising; II. 337\r\n  Bed, reason for never going to; II. 141\r\n  Bees, Mind of; II. 29\r\n  Bessie\xe2\x80\x99s Song; II. 76\r\n  Bible-Selections for Children; I. xiii\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9d    \xe2\x80\x9d    learning by heart; I. xiv\r\n  Black Light, how to produce; II. 341\r\n  Boat, motion of, how to imitate on land; II. 108\r\n  Books, or Minds. Our own age is\r\nmore fortunate, on this single score at least, having a larger and far\r\nnobler proportion of female writers; among whom, since the death of\r\nGeorge Eliot, there is none left whose touch is so exquisite and\r\nmasterly, whose love is so thoroughly according to knowledge, whose\r\nbright and sweet invention is so fruitful, so truthful, or so delightful\r\nas Mrs. Molesworth\xe2\x80\x99s.
